The Three Types of Reports
Maturidi defines reports as a means of knowledge and classifies them into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's classification of reports into three types and the foundational role of reports in human knowledge.

Core Idea
Reports are the basis of identity and knowledge, coming in three epistemological categories.

Maturidi begins his discussion of reports (akhbar) by highlighting their fundamental role in human knowledge: through reports we know our lineage, names, and even the origin of life. He then introduces a tripartite classification that is essential for evaluating the certainty of transmitted information. Let's examine this classification directly from his words.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on reports and their types
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Maturidi first establishes the importance of reports in daily knowledge before categorizing them.
2
Identify the three types
From the passage, list the three types of reports: reports of the messengers, mutawatir reports, and single reports. Understand what distinguishes each.
The passage defines each type briefly.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself: why does Maturidi start with a general statement about the role of reports? This sets the stage for his epistemology.
This connects to the broader theme of sources of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الخبر فهو من وسائل المعرفة، وبه يعرف المرء قبل كل شيء نسبه واسمه ومائيته وكذلك العرق الذي ينتمي إليه، ويصل به إلى معرفة اسمه الشخصي وأسماء كل شيء من الخلق، ويؤمن بذلك العلم بمنشأ الحياة البشرية واستدامتها. فالأخبار ثلاثة أنواع: أخبار الرسل أي الأخبار الآتية عن طريق الرسل، والخبر المتواتر أي الخبر الذي ثبت عن طريق التواتر، وخبر الواحد. إن الآيات والمعجزات هي التي توضح صدق الرسل؛ فالأخبار التي تصل إلينا عن طريقها لزم قبولها بضرورة العقل، إذ الخبر الوارد من قبل هؤلاء الرسل متى بلغ درجة التواتر تنبع منه المعرفة الصادقة فالتواتر يعني النظر بالعقل إلى هؤلاء الناقلين للخبر كما وكيفا ، ثم الحكم بصدقه . وكذلك يذهب الماتريدي إلى الاعتراف بأن الإجماع المبنى على اجتهادات العلماء يعتبر في حد التواتر؛ إذ يقول بأن هؤلاء العلماء، رغم انفرادهم بتفرق الهمم واختلافف الأهواء، فإن اتفاقهم في رأي مشترك لن يكون دون لطف الله العلي الذي يملك إظهار حقه وعصمة خلقه فيما شاء. ويعني ذلك أن الخبر المتواتر الحاصل بالإجماع شبيه عنده بأخبار الرسل.
As for the report (al-khabar), it is a means of knowledge; through it a person knows before everything his lineage, name, essence (māhiyyā), as well as the race to which he belongs, and through it he reaches knowledge of his personal name and the names of everything among creation, and he believes through that knowledge the origin of human life and its continuity. Reports are of three types: reports of the messengers (akhbār al-rusul), i.e., the reports coming via the messengers; the mutawātir report, i.e., the report established by way of tawātur (mass transmission); and the report of a single person (khabar al-wāḥid). Indeed, the signs and miracles are what clarify the truthfulness of the messengers; the reports that reach us through them must be accepted by necessity of reason (bi-ḍarūrat al-ʿaql), for when the report coming from these messengers reaches the degree of tawātur, true knowledge arises from it. Tawātur means rationally examining the transmitters of the report in terms of quantity and quality, then judging its truthfulness. Likewise, al-Maturidi goes to acknowledge that consensus (al-ijmāʿ) built upon the independent reasoning (ijtihādāt) of scholars is considered at the level of tawātur; for he says that these scholars, despite their individuality due to the divergence of intentions and the difference of inclinations, their agreement on a common opinion would not be without the grace of Allah the Most High, Who possesses the power to manifest His truth and protect His creation in whatever He wills. This means that the mutawātir report obtained by consensus is similar in his view to the reports of the messengers.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a classification and epistemological evaluation of reports as a source of knowledge. Maturidi first notes the importance of reports for knowing identity, lineage, and history. He then classifies reports into three types: (1) reports from messengers (prophetic reports), (2) mutawātir reports (continuously transmitted by a large number making collusion impossible), and (3) single reports (khabar al-wāḥid). He explains that miracles establish the truthfulness of messengers, so their reports must be accepted by rational necessity when they reach tawātur. Tawātur itself involves rational scrutiny of the transmitters and their circumstances, leading to a judgment of truth. Maturidi then makes a remarkable claim: scholarly consensus (ijmāʿ) based on ijtihad is equivalent to tawātur. Despite scholars having different motives and inclinations, their unanimous agreement indicates divine grace and protection, making their consensus as reliable as the reports of the messengers. Thus, mutawātir knowledge can arise from both mass transmission and consensus.

Key Terms
الخبر
أخبار الرسل
الخبر المتواتر
خبر الواحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all reports provide the same level of certainty.
Correction: Maturidi classifies reports into three types with different epistemological strengths; only prophetic and mutawatir reports yield necessary knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many types of reports does Maturidi identify?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Reports are essential for human knowledge and are divided into three types: prophetic, mutawatir, and single, each with different degrees of certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that reports are the means by which you know your lineage, name, and even the origin of human life. Think of one core belief you hold about your religion—your belief in the Prophet, the Qur'an, or the afterlife. Did you ever verify the chain of reports that brought that belief to you? If not, what would it take for you to honestly examine that chain today?
?
The passage divides reports into three levels of certainty. Where do you place your own knowledge of Allah and His messengers? Is it based on mutawātir reports that leave no room for doubt, or on weaker reports you accepted without question? Commit to tracing at least one of your foundational Islamic beliefs back to its source and determining its level of certainty within the next week.
?
You rely on reports every day—news, social media, word of mouth. Yet the passage teaches that only certain types of reports yield certainty. Which reports about your faith have you treated as certain without checking their transmission? Are you willing to set aside pride and ask yourself whether your certainty is truly justified by the evidence?

Prophetic Reports and the Rational Necessity of Tawatur
Maturidi explains how prophetic reports with miracles and tawatur yield certain knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi argues that prophetic reports, when accompanied by miracles and reaching tawatur, must be accepted by rational necessity.

Core Idea
Prophetic reports become certain knowledge through miracles and mass transmission.

After classifying reports, Maturidi turns to the epistemological grounding of prophetic reports. He explains that the miracles of the prophets verify their truthfulness, and when their reports reach the level of tawatur (mass transmission), they yield necessary knowledge. He then defines tawatur as a rational evaluation of the transmitters. Let's examine this key argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on miracles and tawatur
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that Maturidi connects the truthfulness of messengers to miracles, and then to tawatur.
2
Identify the role of miracles
Miracles establish the truthfulness of the prophets, so their reports are trustworthy. This is the foundation.
3
Understand tawatur
Maturidi defines tawatur as rationally examining the transmitters in quantity and quality, then judging the report as true. This is a rational process.
This is not blind acceptance.
4
See the necessity
When a report from a prophet reaches tawatur, it produces true knowledge (ma'rifa sadiqa) and must be accepted by rational necessity ('aql).
This is the epistemological conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الخبر فهو من وسائل المعرفة، وبه يعرف المرء قبل كل شيء نسبه واسمه ومائيته وكذلك العرق الذي ينتمي إليه، ويصل به إلى معرفة اسمه الشخصي وأسماء كل شيء من الخلق، ويؤمن بذلك العلم بمنشأ الحياة البشرية واستدامتها. فالأخبار ثلاثة أنواع: أخبار الرسل أي الأخبار الآتية عن طريق الرسل، والخبر المتواتر أي الخبر الذي ثبت عن طريق التواتر، وخبر الواحد. إن الآيات والمعجزات هي التي توضح صدق الرسل؛ فالأخبار التي تصل إلينا عن طريقها لزم قبولها بضرورة العقل، إذ الخبر الوارد من قبل هؤلاء الرسل متى بلغ درجة التواتر تنبع منه المعرفة الصادقة فالتواتر يعني النظر بالعقل إلى هؤلاء الناقلين للخبر كما وكيفا ، ثم الحكم بصدقه . وكذلك يذهب الماتريدي إلى الاعتراف بأن الإجماع المبنى على اجتهادات العلماء يعتبر في حد التواتر؛ إذ يقول بأن هؤلاء العلماء، رغم انفرادهم بتفرق الهمم واختلافف الأهواء، فإن اتفاقهم في رأي مشترك لن يكون دون لطف الله العلي الذي يملك إظهار حقه وعصمة خلقه فيما شاء. ويعني ذلك أن الخبر المتواتر الحاصل بالإجماع شبيه عنده بأخبار الرسل.
As for the report (al-khabar), it is a means of knowledge; through it a person knows before everything his lineage, name, essence (māhiyyā), as well as the race to which he belongs, and through it he reaches knowledge of his personal name and the names of everything among creation, and he believes through that knowledge the origin of human life and its continuity. Reports are of three types: reports of the messengers (akhbār al-rusul), i.e., the reports coming via the messengers; the mutawātir report, i.e., the report established by way of tawātur (mass transmission); and the report of a single person (khabar al-wāḥid). Indeed, the signs and miracles are what clarify the truthfulness of the messengers; the reports that reach us through them must be accepted by necessity of reason (bi-ḍarūrat al-ʿaql), for when the report coming from these messengers reaches the degree of tawātur, true knowledge arises from it. Tawātur means rationally examining the transmitters of the report in terms of quantity and quality, then judging its truthfulness. Likewise, al-Maturidi goes to acknowledge that consensus (al-ijmāʿ) built upon the independent reasoning (ijtihādāt) of scholars is considered at the level of tawātur; for he says that these scholars, despite their individuality due to the divergence of intentions and the difference of inclinations, their agreement on a common opinion would not be without the grace of Allah the Most High, Who possesses the power to manifest His truth and protect His creation in whatever He wills. This means that the mutawātir report obtained by consensus is similar in his view to the reports of the messengers.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a classification and epistemological evaluation of reports as a source of knowledge. Maturidi first notes the importance of reports for knowing identity, lineage, and history. He then classifies reports into three types: (1) reports from messengers (prophetic reports), (2) mutawātir reports (continuously transmitted by a large number making collusion impossible), and (3) single reports (khabar al-wāḥid). He explains that miracles establish the truthfulness of messengers, so their reports must be accepted by rational necessity when they reach tawātur. Tawātur itself involves rational scrutiny of the transmitters and their circumstances, leading to a judgment of truth. Maturidi then makes a remarkable claim: scholarly consensus (ijmāʿ) based on ijtihad is equivalent to tawātur. Despite scholars having different motives and inclinations, their unanimous agreement indicates divine grace and protection, making their consensus as reliable as the reports of the messengers. Thus, mutawātir knowledge can arise from both mass transmission and consensus.

Key Terms
الآيات
المعجزات
ضرورة العقل
التواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any report from a prophet is automatically certain regardless of transmission.
Correction: Maturidi requires that prophetic reports reach tawatur to produce necessary knowledge; miracles verify the source but transmission must also be mass authentic.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what establishes the truthfulness of the messengers?
A) Consensus of scholars
B) The signs and miracles
C) The number of transmitters
D) Rational analysis alone

Key Takeaway
Prophetic reports, when accompanied by miracles and transmitted via tawatur, produce certain knowledge that must be accepted by reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever sincerely asked yourself: Is my belief in Allah and His Messenger built on the solid rational foundation this lesson describes—through miracles and mass-transmitted reports—or is it merely inherited opinion? What would it take for you to truly know, not just assume?
?
This passage says that tawatur requires examining the transmitters in quantity and quality, then judging truth. In your daily life, do you apply such rigorous standards to the religious claims you hear, or do you accept things based on emotion or popularity? Give one concrete change you will make starting today.
?
If you were asked today to defend the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him) using only the argument of miracles and tawatur, could you do it without hesitation? If not, why have you not made this knowledge your own? Commit to writing down the key steps of this proof this week.

Consensus (Ijma) as a Form of Tawatur
Maturidi argues that scholarly consensus (ijma) is equivalent to tawatur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's view that scholarly consensus built on ijtihad is epistemically equivalent to mutawatir reports.

Core Idea
Consensus of scholars, despite their differences, is a divine safeguard and equals tawatur.

Maturidi makes a striking claim: the consensus of scholars (ijma) based on ijtihad is at the level of tawatur (mass transmission). He argues that despite the diverse intentions and inclinations of scholars, their unanimous agreement can only come from divine grace and protection. This elevates consensus to the same certainty as the reports of the messengers. Let's explore this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on ijma'
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that Maturidi equates ijma' to tawatur in certainty.
2
Understand the reasoning
Maturidi says that despite scholars' differing motives and inclinations, their agreement cannot happen without divine grace. This suggests that consensus is divinely guided.
This is a theological argument for the authority of ijma'.
3
Compare to prophetic reports
He concludes that the mutawatir report obtained by consensus is similar to the reports of the messengers in certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الخبر فهو من وسائل المعرفة، وبه يعرف المرء قبل كل شيء نسبه واسمه ومائيته وكذلك العرق الذي ينتمي إليه، ويصل به إلى معرفة اسمه الشخصي وأسماء كل شيء من الخلق، ويؤمن بذلك العلم بمنشأ الحياة البشرية واستدامتها. فالأخبار ثلاثة أنواع: أخبار الرسل أي الأخبار الآتية عن طريق الرسل، والخبر المتواتر أي الخبر الذي ثبت عن طريق التواتر، وخبر الواحد. إن الآيات والمعجزات هي التي توضح صدق الرسل؛ فالأخبار التي تصل إلينا عن طريقها لزم قبولها بضرورة العقل، إذ الخبر الوارد من قبل هؤلاء الرسل متى بلغ درجة التواتر تنبع منه المعرفة الصادقة فالتواتر يعني النظر بالعقل إلى هؤلاء الناقلين للخبر كما وكيفا ، ثم الحكم بصدقه . وكذلك يذهب الماتريدي إلى الاعتراف بأن الإجماع المبنى على اجتهادات العلماء يعتبر في حد التواتر؛ إذ يقول بأن هؤلاء العلماء، رغم انفرادهم بتفرق الهمم واختلافف الأهواء، فإن اتفاقهم في رأي مشترك لن يكون دون لطف الله العلي الذي يملك إظهار حقه وعصمة خلقه فيما شاء. ويعني ذلك أن الخبر المتواتر الحاصل بالإجماع شبيه عنده بأخبار الرسل.
As for the report (al-khabar), it is a means of knowledge; through it a person knows before everything his lineage, name, essence (māhiyyā), as well as the race to which he belongs, and through it he reaches knowledge of his personal name and the names of everything among creation, and he believes through that knowledge the origin of human life and its continuity. Reports are of three types: reports of the messengers (akhbār al-rusul), i.e., the reports coming via the messengers; the mutawātir report, i.e., the report established by way of tawātur (mass transmission); and the report of a single person (khabar al-wāḥid). Indeed, the signs and miracles are what clarify the truthfulness of the messengers; the reports that reach us through them must be accepted by necessity of reason (bi-ḍarūrat al-ʿaql), for when the report coming from these messengers reaches the degree of tawātur, true knowledge arises from it. Tawātur means rationally examining the transmitters of the report in terms of quantity and quality, then judging its truthfulness. Likewise, al-Maturidi goes to acknowledge that consensus (al-ijmāʿ) built upon the independent reasoning (ijtihādāt) of scholars is considered at the level of tawātur; for he says that these scholars, despite their individuality due to the divergence of intentions and the difference of inclinations, their agreement on a common opinion would not be without the grace of Allah the Most High, Who possesses the power to manifest His truth and protect His creation in whatever He wills. This means that the mutawātir report obtained by consensus is similar in his view to the reports of the messengers.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a classification and epistemological evaluation of reports as a source of knowledge. Maturidi first notes the importance of reports for knowing identity, lineage, and history. He then classifies reports into three types: (1) reports from messengers (prophetic reports), (2) mutawātir reports (continuously transmitted by a large number making collusion impossible), and (3) single reports (khabar al-wāḥid). He explains that miracles establish the truthfulness of messengers, so their reports must be accepted by rational necessity when they reach tawātur. Tawātur itself involves rational scrutiny of the transmitters and their circumstances, leading to a judgment of truth. Maturidi then makes a remarkable claim: scholarly consensus (ijmāʿ) based on ijtihad is equivalent to tawātur. Despite scholars having different motives and inclinations, their unanimous agreement indicates divine grace and protection, making their consensus as reliable as the reports of the messengers. Thus, mutawātir knowledge can arise from both mass transmission and consensus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That consensus is just a human agreement with no special authority.
Correction: Maturidi argues that consensus is divinely safeguarded and reaches the certainty of tawatur.

Scholarly consensus, due to divine grace, yields certain knowledge equivalent to mutawatir reports.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says that agreement among scholars, despite their differing inclinations, is a sign of divine grace. How does this affect your trust in scholarly consensus when it confronts your personal biases?
?
If consensus carries the same certainty as prophetic reports, how should this change the way you seek guidance for your life? What is one area where you have ignored established consensus, and what will you do to realign yourself with it today?

The Epistemic Status of Khabar al-Wahid
Maturidi defines khabar al-wahid and its ruling requiring ijtihad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Maturidi's position that khabar al-wahid does not yield certain knowledge but necessitates action through ijtihad.

Core Idea
Khabar al-wahid demands action through ijtihad, not certainty.

Imam Maturidi here tackles a key question in uṣūl al-fiqh: what kind of knowledge does a single-narrator report (khabar al-wāḥid) convey? He clarifies that such reports do not yield certain knowledge (ʿilm) but still obligate action — not blindly, but through careful ijtihad and scrutiny of narrators. This passage lays the foundation for how later scholars approach the authority of solitary hadith reports.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the English translation to get a sense of the argument.
Notice the dense style of Maturidi's prose.
2
Identify the key claims
Identify what Maturidi says about the epistemic status of khabar al-wahid: does it give certainty? What does it obligate? How is ijtihad involved?
Look for phrases like 'لا يبلغ هذا القدر في إيجاب العلم' and 'يجب العمل به والترك، بالاجتهاد'.
3
Reflect on the analogy with sensory knowledge
Consider why Maturidi compares khabar al-wahid to sensory knowledge. How does that analogy support his conclusion that ijtihad is necessary?
The analogy shows that even the most certain path (sensory knowledge) can be uncertain due to weak senses or distant objects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خبر الواحد وخبر آخر لا يبلغ هذا القدر في إيجاب العلم والشهادة بأنه الحق عن نبي الرحمة، فيجب العمل به والترك، بالاجتهاد والنظر في أحوال الرواة، والظاهر مما ظهر حكمه، وجوازه في السمع الذي قد أحيط. ثم يعمل بما يغلب عليه الوجه وإن احتمل الغلط، إذ ربما يعمل به في علم الحس الذي هو أرفع طرق العلم بضعف الحواس أو بعد المحسوس ولطفه على أن ترك العمل به والعمل جميعا لا يرجع المرء فيه إلى الإحاطة. وإلى أيهما مال كان في ذلك إعراض عن حق الخبر. فلذلك لزم القول فيه بالاجتهاد بالوجهين ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The single-narrator report and another report that does not reach this level of compelling knowledge and testimony that it is the truth from the Prophet of mercy, so acting upon it and abandoning it are obligatory, by ijtihad and examination of the conditions of the narrators, and the apparent from what has become manifest of its ruling, and its permissibility in the transmitted law which has been encompassed. Then one acts upon what is preponderant in aspect, even if it admits the possibility of error, for sometimes it is acted upon in sensory knowledge, which is the highest of the ways of knowledge, due to weakness of the senses or the distance and subtlety of the perceived object, such that abandoning acting upon it and acting upon it both do not bring a person to complete certainty. And whichever of the two he inclines to, in that there is a turning away from the right of the report. Therefore it is necessary to hold ijtihad in it with both aspects, and there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage defines the epistemological status of khabar al-wahid, or the single-narrator report. Maturidi begins by stating that such a report does not reach the level of necessitating certain knowledge (ilm) or testimony that it is definitively from the Prophet. Rather, it makes action—either performing or abandoning what it conveys—obligatory through ijtihad and scrutiny of the narrators' conditions. The 'apparent' from what has been established as a ruling and its permissibility in the transmitted law that is already known form the basis. Then, one acts upon the preponderant probability even though error is possible. He draws an analogy with sensory knowledge, which, despite being the most certain path, can still be affected by weak senses or distant objects—so action without certainty is accepted. In both cases, whether one acts or abandons, complete certainty (ihata) is not achieved; yet turning away from the report is an evasion of its right. Thus, ijtihad with both possibilities (acting or leaving) is necessary. The passage guards against two misunderstandings: that khabar al-wahid yields certainty (it does not) and that it can be dismissed entirely (it cannot; it requires ijtihad).

Key Terms
خبر الواحد
إيجاب العلم
اجتهاد
الظاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that khabar al-wahid provides certain knowledge (ʿilm) and can be accepted without ijtihad.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly states it does not reach the level of compelling certain knowledge (ijāb al-ʿilm); rather, it obligates action through ijtihad and scrutiny of narrators.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the ruling of acting upon khabar al-wahid?
A) It is prohibited unless certain.
B) It is obligatory through ijtihad.
C) It is optional based on preference.
D) It requires rejection due to uncertainty.

Key Takeaway
Khabar al-wahid does not produce certainty but obligates action through ijtihad, balancing both acting and abandoning as valid possibilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a single report about a religious ruling, do you tend to treat it as certain or as something requiring careful thought? How does Maturidi's teaching challenge your instinct?
?
Think of a time you acted on a single report (khabar wahid) without fully verifying. Were you more certain than you should have been? How can you apply ijtihad in such situations moving forward?
?
If acting or abandoning a khabar wahid both involve turning away from its right, how do you decide which path to take? Does this pressure you to be more deliberate in your religious practice?

Refutation of Al-Ka'bi's Objection on Seeing God
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's objection that non-bodies are invisible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the claim that God cannot be seen because He is not a body, and why visibility depends on removal of external obstacles.

Core Idea
Visibility is about removing obstacles, not about being a body.

Al-Ka'bi objected that since God is not a body, He cannot be seen. Maturidi responds by showing that the conditions for seeing are external barriers like distance and smallness, not the object's nature. In this passage, he uses the example of the Angel of Death to illustrate that what is invisible to one sight may be visible to another when barriers are removed.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Al-Ka'bi claims that because God is not a body, He cannot be seen. Note that this is based on an analogy with physical bodies.
The objection assumes visibility requires corporeality.
2
Follow Maturidi's response
Maturidi grants that God is not a body but argues that obstacles like distance and smallness are external and can be removed. Thus vision of non-bodies is not impossible.
He uses the example of something hidden from one sight but visible to another.
3
Reflect on the Angel of Death example
Maturidi points out that the Angel of Death sees all creatures regardless of distance, which is impossible for human sight but possible for a different sight.
This shows that vision is not limited by the object's nature but by the observer's capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معارضته بالفاعل والعالم أنه لا كذلك : فيجوز وجود ذلك ولا جسم، فمثله في الرؤية. على أن البعد الذي يحجبنا والدقة يجوز أن يبلغه بصر غيرنا ؛ فصار ارتفاع الرؤية بالحجاب فإذا ارتفع جاز. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وبعد، فإن الذي يقوله [الكعبي] تقدير برؤية الأجسام، ولم يمتحن بصره بغير الأجسام والأعراض أن كيف سبيل الرؤية له. وبعد، فإن كل جسم يُرى وإن كان الدقة والبعد يحجبان، فيجوز ارتفاعهما عن بصر غير فيرى؛ على ما يرى ملك الموت من بأطراف الأرض ووسطها مما لو اعتبر ذلك ببصر البشر لما احتمل الإدراك. فثبت أن الذي قدّر به ليس هو سبب تعريف ما يبصر، ولكن سبب تعريف ما يحجب به البصر، فإذا ارتفع رأى مع ما كان المنفي رؤيته لذاته عَرَضاً، وإلا فكل جسم يُرى. فإن لزم إنكار الرؤية لما ليس بجسم أو لما لا يرى إلا بما ذكر ليلزم الإقرار به؛ لأن الذي لا يرى لذاته هو العرض، وإلا فكل عين يُرى. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
His objection by [saying God is] the Agent and the Knower that He is not such: then it is possible that there exists that [vision] and no body, so the like of it in vision. But the distance that veils us and smallness can be reached by the sight of others; so vision is raised by the veil, and if it is raised, it is possible. And there is no power except by Allah. Moreover, what he [al-Ka'bi] says is an estimation by seeing bodies, and he has not tested his sight with non-bodies and accidents to know how the path of vision is for him. Moreover, every body is seen even if smallness and distance veil it, so it is possible that both are removed from the sight of another, and he sees; just as the Angel of Death sees those at the ends of the earth and its middle, if that were considered by human sight, it would not bear perception. So it is established that what he estimated by is not the cause of defining what is seen, but the cause of defining what veils the sight; so if it is raised, he sees, along with what was denied to be seen in itself as an accident, otherwise every body is seen. For if the denial of seeing what is not a body or what is not seen except by what is mentioned is necessary, then affirmation of it becomes necessary; because what is not seen in itself is the accident, otherwise every eye is seen. And there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection made by al-Ka'bi regarding the possibility of seeing God. The objector argues that God, being the Agent and the Knower, is not like bodies, so vision of Him is impossible. The author begins by granting that God is not a body, yet vision of non-bodies is not impossible. He explains that obstacles to vision, such as distance or smallness, are contingent and can be overcome. For example, what is hidden from one sight may be visible to another. He then criticizes al-Ka'bi for basing his argument solely on the experience of seeing bodies and not acknowledging that vision of non-bodies could be different. He further supports this by noting that even bodies are only seen when obstacles are removed; the Angel of Death sees all creatures regardless of distance, which is impossible for human sight. Thus, the conditions that prevent vision are not intrinsic to the object but are external barriers. If those barriers are lifted, vision occurs. The author concludes that denying vision of non-bodies because they are not bodies would actually require affirming their visibility, because the only thing that is inherently invisible is an accident (not a substance). Hence, vision of God is not impossible.

Key Terms
رؤية
جسم
عرض
حجاب
بعد
دقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Visual perception requires the object to be a physical body.
Correction: This passage shows that visibility depends on the removal of external obstacles (distance, smallness), not on the object being a body.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's argument, why is al-Ka'bi's objection that God cannot be seen because He is not a body invalid?
A) Because God has a body but of a different kind.
B) Because bodies themselves are only seen when obstacles are removed, and those obstacles can be removed for non-bodies as well.
C) Because vision is only possible for accidents, not bodies.
D) Because the Angel of Death can see God.

Key Takeaway
Seeing God is possible because obstacles to vision are contingent, not intrinsic to the object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly long to see Allah in the Hereafter, or does your current life reflect contentment with worldly illusions? What would change in your daily priorities if you were certain that this vision is possible and near?
?
The Angel of Death sees every soul without any veil. If you lived with the awareness that your every action is witnessed and nothing is hidden, what would you do differently today?
?
Maturidi argues that obstacles to vision are external and removable. What internal obstacles—pride, sin, distraction—are you allowing to veil your heart from perceiving the reality of Allah? What concrete step will you take to remove one today?

Refutation of the Objection from This World
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's objection using the purpose of this world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the absence of the vision of God in this world does not prove its impossibility, because this world was created for trial and burden.

Core Idea
Non-occurrence in this world does not imply impossibility; it preserves the purpose of trial.

In this brief passage, Imam Maturidi responds to an objection raised by al-Ka'bi: since God is not seen in this world, it must be impossible in the afterlife. Maturidi argues that this reasoning is flawed—the lack of vision in this world is not due to impossibility but by divine wisdom. This world was created for testing, and seeing God would remove the very trial that defines this life.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Pay attention to the structure: an objection followed by a response.
Notice that the response denies impossibility and then gives the reason.
2
Identify the objection and response
Identify al-Ka'bi's objection (عارض بأمر الدنيا) and Maturidi's two-part response: first denying impossibility (ولا يحال ذلك), then explaining why (لكن يسقط المحنة ويرفع الكلفة).
3
Understand the concepts of trial and burden
Reflect on the words 'محنة' (trial) and 'كلفة' (burden). Maturidi says that if vision occurred in this world, it would remove these elements, contradicting the purpose of this world.
This world is created specifically for these two things.
4
Reflect on the argument's logic
Consider why non-occurrence does not equal impossibility. Maturidi's argument shows that the absence is intentional, not imposed. This is a key principle in kalam reasoning about the afterlife.
This lesson connects to the broader discussion of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعارض بأمر الدنيا. ولا يحال ذلك، ولكن يسقط المحنة ويرفع الكلفة، والدنيا لهما خلقت.
And he objected by the matter of this world. That is not impossible, but it would remove the trial and lift the burden, and this world was created for them.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents a brief objection and answer. Al-Ka'bi objects that since God is not seen in this world, it must be impossible. The author responds that non-occurrence in this world does not entail impossibility; rather, if it occurred in this world, it would nullify the purpose of this world as a place of trial and obligation. This world was created specifically for testing and responsibility, so vision of God is deferred to the afterlife.

Key Terms
الدنيا
المحنة
الكلفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something does not happen in this world, it is impossible.
Correction: Non-occurrence does not imply impossibility; it may be due to divine wisdom, such as preserving the purpose of trial and obligation in this world.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say that God's not being seen in this world does not prove it is impossible?
A) Because God can be seen in this world but chooses not to.
B) Because this world is for trial, and seeing God would remove the need for faith.
C) Because the afterlife is a different realm where vision is possible.
D) Because the objector has no proof.

Key Takeaway
The absence of God's vision in this world does not prove impossibility; it is a deliberate arrangement to preserve this world's purpose as a place of trial and obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This world was created precisely for trial and burden. In which area of your life—your patience, your worship, your relationships—are you seeking immediate relief or reward, forgetting that the hardship itself is the purpose of this stage? What would it look like for you to embrace the burden rather than resent it?
?
You have a limited number of days in this world. If the absence of seeing God here is not a sign of impossibility but a deliberate test, what does that demand of you right now? What changes about your prayer, your trust, and your longing if you truly believe that the vision is real but postponed for the next life?

The Objection on Tawatur and Ibn al-Rawandi's Refutation
An objection regarding the tawatur of the Quran is refuted by Ibn al-Rawandi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi and Ibn al-Rawandi defend the mass transmission (tawatur) of the Quran against skeptical objections.

Core Idea
Tawatur makes the Quran undeniable; Ibn al-Rawandi exposes the objector's ignorance of how news spreads.

This passage presents two related objections against the reliability of the Quran's transmission. The first claims it is a solitary report (khabar al-ahad), which the author dismisses by affirming its tawatur. The second attacks tawatur itself, suggesting that large groups can be deceived. Ibn al-Rawandi counters with a practical argument about how widely known events become impossible to conceal, especially in gatherings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the two objection-response pairs.
2
Understand the first objection and refutation
The objector claims the Quran is khabar al-ahad (solitary report) and therefore false. Maturidi refutes by asserting it is tawatur (transmitted by many from many).
Notice that Maturidi also notes the objector implicitly acknowledges the Quran as a proof.
3
Analyze the second objection and Ibn al-Rawandi's response
The objector attacks tawatur itself, arguing that groups are either too far (deception possible) or too close (impossible for many to witness). Ibn al-Rawandi calls them ignorant of how news spreads in assemblies, where events become widely known even to distant people.
Reflect on why proximity or distance doesn't undermine tawatur in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطعن في إخبار القرآن: إنه خبر الآحاد وذلك كذب، بل رواه كافة عن كافة، مع ما في هذا إقرار أنه حجة. وطعن التواتر بما لا تخلو الجماعة من البعد من السمع، فيحتمل الحيلة، أو القرب فلا يحتمل مباشرة مثله إلا اليسير قال ابن الروندي: هذه [هي] الجهلة بالمحافل، وإلا الأمر في ذلك ينتشر ما كان من قيد أو قيل، حتى لا يكاد شيء منه يخفى على الأبعدين فضلا عن الأقربين.
And he objected regarding the report of the Quran: that it is a solitary report (khabar al-ahad) and that is a lie, rather it was transmitted by many from many, along with this being an acknowledgement that it is a proof. And he objected to tawatur (mass transmission) in that the group is never free from distance from hearing, so trickery is possible, or proximity, then direct experience of the like of it is not possible except rarely. Ibn al-Rawandi said: These are the ignorant in the assemblies, otherwise the matter in that spreads, whatever it be of restriction or saying, until hardly anything of it remains hidden from the far ones, let alone the near ones.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage contains two objections related to the transmission of the Quran. First, the objector claims the Quran is khabar al-ahad (solitary report), which Maturidi refutes by stating it is transmitted by many from many (tawatur). Second, the objector attacks the concept of tawatur itself, arguing that a group of transmitters may either be far from the source (allowing deception) or too close (making it impossible for many to directly witness). Ibn al-Rawandi responds by calling the objectors ignorant of how news spreads in gatherings; in reality, events of such magnitude become widely known and cannot be concealed, even from distant people.

Key Terms
خبر الآحاد
التواتر
كافة عن كافة
المحافل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection denies the Quran's authenticity outright.
Correction: The objection does not deny the Quran's authenticity but attacks its mode of transmission; the refutation reasserts the certainty of tawatur.

Checkpoint
What is Ibn al-Rawandi's response to the objection about tawatur?
A) He agrees that tawatur is impossible.
B) He accuses the objectors of ignorance about how news spreads in gatherings.
C) He argues that distance prevents deception.
D) He claims the Quran was transmitted by a few.

Key Takeaway
The mass transmission (tawatur) of the Quran is a strong proof that refutes both the claim of it being a solitary report and the skepticism about the reliability of large groups.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you feel deeply certain that it has reached you unchanged, or is there a subtle doubt in your heart? What would it mean for your relationship with Allah if you truly internalized the tawatur of His speech?
?
Ibn al-Rawandi called the objectors ignorant of how news spreads in gatherings. Have you ever dismissed a well-known truth from the scholars because you thought you had a better argument? Are you willing to submit to the overwhelming evidence of the Quran's transmission, even if your intellect resists?
?
If the Quran is so widely preserved, what excuses remain for not taking every verse as a direct command to you today? How will you change your approach to the Quran after realizing its transmission is undeniable?

Two Interpretations of 'Ahl al-Dhikr' in the Quran
Maturidi offers two possible interpretations of 'ahl al-dhikr' in the Quranic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possible meanings of 'ahl al-dhikr' that Imam Maturidi derives from the Quranic verse.

Core Idea
Ahl al-dhikr can mean secret acknowledgers or honorable people who never lie.

Imam Maturidi draws our attention to a verse that rebukes those who preach righteousness but forget themselves. He then offers two nuanced readings of the phrase 'ahl al-dhikr' — the people of remembrance — that expand its possible meaning beyond the literal. These interpretations show Maturidi's careful attention to context and honor.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice how Maturidi presents two possibilities with the phrase 'وجائز أن يكون'.
2
Understand the first interpretation
The first interpretation relates 'people of remembrance' to those who secretly acknowledge the truth but do not act upon it. Maturidi connects this to the Quranic rebuke in Surah al-Baqarah 2:44.
This interpretation highlights hypocrisy.
3
Understand the second interpretation
The second interpretation identifies 'people of remembrance' as people of honor or nobility, whose social standing protects them from lying when they are called to judge.
This interpretation emphasizes moral integrity.
4
Reflect on Maturidi's approach
Maturidi ends with 'والله أعلم' (Allah knows best), indicating that these are plausible interpretations, not definitive. Consider why he offers multiple meanings.
This reflects scholarly humility and openness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجائز أن يكون في قوم يقرون بذلك سرا كقوله : أتأمرون الناس بالبر﴾ [البقرة، ٤٤/٢] الآية . وجائز أن يكون المراد بأهل الذكر هم أهل الشرف الذين يمنعهم شرفهم عند التحكيم إليهم عن الكذب. والله أعلم.
And it is possible that there are people who acknowledge that secretly, as His saying: 'Do you command people to righteousness?' [Quran 2:44] the verse. And it is possible that what is meant by the people of remembrance are the people of honor, whose honor prevents them from lying when they are referred to for judgment. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage offers two possible interpretations of the Quranic phrase 'ahl al-dhikr' (people of remembrance) from the verse 'Do you command people to righteousness while you forget yourselves?' in Surah al-Baqarah. The first interpretation is that 'the people' mentioned in the verse are those who secretly acknowledge the truth but do not act upon it publicly. The second interpretation is that 'the people of remembrance' refers to people of honor and nobility, whose social standing guards them against lying when they are called upon to judge or arbitrate. Maturidi presents these as plausible understandings and ends with 'Allah knows best', indicating that the definitive meaning is with Allah.

Key Terms
أهل الذكر
أهل الشرف
يكذب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'ahl al-dhikr' only refers to scholars or those who recite remembrance frequently.

Checkpoint

D) Angels vs. humans

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that 'ahl al-dhikr' can be understood in two ways: as those who secretly acknowledge truth or as honorable people who avoid lying.

What specific situation this week can you enter with a commitment to truth over convenience?
?

The Division of Revelation and the Unity of the Qur'an
Maturidi subdivides revelation and argues for its coherence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's classification of revelation into clear (muhkam) and ambiguous (mutashabih) and into explicit (mufassar) and implicit (mubham), and why he argues the Qur'an contains no contradiction.

Core Idea
Ambiguous revelation must be interpreted by the clear; contradiction indicates foolishness.

Maturidi begins this passage by establishing a crucial classification within revelation: it is divided into clear and ambiguous, and into explicit and implicit. This division is not arbitrary; it serves to define the limits of human knowledge and to test believers. In this lesson, we will explore how he explains the method of interpreting ambiguous verses and the rational argument for the Qur'an's unity and lack of contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the key terms: محكم, متشابه, مفسر, مبهم.
2
Identify the classifications and their purpose
Notice that Maturidi mentions two pairs of divisions: clear/ambiguous and explicit/implicit. His purpose is to show 'the ultimate goal of knowledge' and when to refrain or proceed.
These divisions are foundational for understanding how to approach the Qur'an.
3
Understand the method of interpretation
He states that the ambiguous should be interpreted in light of the clear, and the implicit by the explicit. This prevents arbitrary interpretation.
This method ensures consistency and avoids contradiction.
4
Reflect on the trial and the proof of unity
God tests through submission and seeking. Each faction thought they had the clear part, leading to disagreement, but they must know the clear from the ambiguous to avoid contradiction. Finally, the Qur'an cannot admit contradiction, as God says in 4:82, and contradiction in evidence is proof of foolishness.
This argument defends the divine origin of the Qur'an.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم جعل السمع قسمين: محكم ومتشابه ومفسر ومبهم، ليبين منتهى المعارف من الكف فيما يجب ذلك والإقدام فيما يلزمه. ومن حمل المبهم على المفسر ولزوم المحكم وعرض المتشابه عليه ما أمكن أن يكون ما فيه: مما يلزم تعرفه ومما إليه حاجة بأهل المحنة، أو ترك الخوض في ذلك فيما أمكن الغناء عن تعرف حقيقة ما فيه، فيكون محنة الوقوف؛ إذ الله تعالى أن يمتحن بوجهين بالتسليم مرة وبالطلب ثانيا، وإنما على العبد الطاعة في قدر الأمر. عند الله [؟]، ولما جمع جل ثناؤه كتابه على الأمرين تعرف الناس الذين أقروا بالكتاب أنه حق من العدول عنه، وأن من لزمه أفلح ونجا، ومن مال عنه شقى وخسر. حتى ظن كل فريق أنه قد أصاب المحكم من ذلك ولزمه، وأن عليه فيما ذهب إليه خصومه أن يقف في ذلك أو يحمله على ما تقرر عنده فيما اعتقده، فألزم تفرقهم في الحاجة كلهم [؟] تعرف المحكم من المتشابه ولزوم العلم بالمتشابه لأن لا يناقض المحكم منه. ثم معلوم أنه لا يحتمل القرآن الاختلاف، وبه وصف الله أنه {ولو كان من عند غير الله لوجدوا فيه اختلافا كثيرًا} [النساء، ٨٢/٤]. وفي العقل أن تناقض أدلة له الدليل هو دليل سفهه.
Then He made revelation two divisions: clear and ambiguous, explicit and implicit, to show the ultimate goal of knowledge: when to refrain and when to proceed. Whoever interprets the implicit by the explicit and adheres to the clear and presents the ambiguous to it [the clear] – what is possible in it: either what is necessary to know and what the people of trial need, or to refrain from delving into it when dispensing with knowing its reality is possible, so that it becomes a trial of standing still; for God Most High tests in two ways: by submission once and by seeking again, and the servant is only obliged to obey according to the capacity of the matter. With God [?]. And when God – exalted be His mention – compiled His Book upon the two aspects, the people who acknowledged the Book recognized that it is truth [worthy of] turning away from it, and that whoever adheres to it succeeds and is saved, and whoever deviates from it is wretched and lost. Until each faction thought that they had grasped the clear part thereof and adhered to it, and that they [their opponents] must stop in that or interpret it according to what they had established in their belief. So it necessitated their divergence in the need – each of them [even] knows the clear from the ambiguous and the necessity of knowledge of the ambiguous so that it does not contradict the clear thereof. Then it is known that the Qur'an does not admit difference, and God described it: 'If it had been from other than God, they would have found in it much difference' [Surah an-Nisa 4:82]. And in the intellect, contradiction in evidence of the proof is proof of his foolishness.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage is a classification and a proof. Maturidi begins by stating that God made revelation two divisions: clear (محكم) and ambiguous (متشابه), and explicit (مفسر) and ambiguous (مبهم). This is to show the limits of knowledge: when to stop and when to proceed. The implicit must be interpreted in light of the explicit, and the ambiguous in light of the clear. This creates a test: either one must know some things or can avoid delving into them, resulting in a test of standing still (محنة الوقوف). God tests in two ways: by submission and by seeking. Then he mentions that when God revealed His book with these two aspects, people recognized it as truth. Those who adhere to it succeed. But each faction thought they had grasped the clear part, leading to disagreement. However, they are required to understand the clear from the ambiguous and to know the ambiguous in a way that does not contradict the clear. It is known that the Qur'an does not admit contradiction, as God described it: 'If it were from other than God, they would find in it much contradiction' (Quran 4:82). In the intellect, contradiction in evidence is proof of foolishness.

Key Terms
المحكم
المتشابه
المفسر
المبهم
الوقف
الاختلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think ambiguous verses can be interpreted arbitrarily without reference to clear verses.
Correction: Maturidi insists they must be interpreted in light of clear verses, ensuring no contradiction.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi argue is the result if people's understanding of revelation leads to contradiction?
A) They are still correct
B) It proves the Qur'an has errors
C) It indicates foolishness
D) It is permissible

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's classification of revelation into clear and ambiguous serves to guide interpretation and defend the Qur'an's unity; contradiction would prove foolishness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse of the Qur'an that you do not fully understand, do you first seek clarification from other clearer verses, or do you rush to impose your own interpretation? Which is easier, and which is more obedient?
?
Maturidi warns that each faction thought they had grasped the clear part. Is there an area of your faith where you are so certain you are right that you dismiss others without honestly considering that you might be missing something? What would it cost you to admit uncertainty?
?
Contradiction in evidence is proof of foolishness. Look at your own life: are there contradictions between what you claim to believe and how you actually live? What specific step will you take today to align your actions with the clear guidance of the Qur'an?

The Quranic Proof Against Multiple Gods
Maturidi quotes Quran 23:91 to establish the impossibility of multiple gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses Quran 23:91 to argue that if there were multiple gods, each would claim exclusive ownership of his creation, proving that only one Creator exists.

Core Idea
Multiple gods would each take their own creation, causing cosmic conflict.

Maturidi begins a powerful scriptural argument by citing the Quranic verse that negates any other god alongside Allah. The verse presents a logical scenario: if there were multiple gods, each would take away what he created, leading to division. This lesson unpacks that argument and connects it to the nature of accidents as proofs of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and translation
Read the Quranic verse (23:91) and its literal translation. Notice how the verse negates both a son and any other god.
The verse is quoted directly; it forms the core of the argument.
2
Understand the logical implication
Reflect on the meaning: if multiple gods existed, each would take his creation. This would lead to chaos, contradicting the order of the universe.
3
Connect to accidents as proofs
Maturidi adds that God places a proof on every accident to show it is created. This reinforces that all creation bears witness to the Creator.
This point bridges to the broader kalam argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن الله تعالى قال: {ما اتخذ الله من ولد وما كان معه من إله} ثم قال: {إذا لذهب كل إله بما خلق} [المؤمنون: 91]. ثم الله جل ثناؤه لم يخلق عرضاً قط إلا جعل عليه دليلاً يعلم أنه خُلق، لما كانت الأعراض كما ذكرنا. ويجوز أن يكون في خلقه خلق يُجمع ويفرّق ويحرك ويسكن ونحن لا نراه، كما كان فيهم من لا نراه بجوهره وإن كان يُرى. وتلك الأفعال لأنفسها لا تُرى، إنما تُرى وتعلم بتغير الأحوال على الجوهر. فإذ كانت جواهر لا تُرى، جائز منها مثلها لم يجعل لما خلق عَلَمًا ولا ذهب به. فكيف ناقض به قول المعتزلة قول الملحدة وهم شركاؤهم في هذا الوجه. فنسأل الله النجاة من قول هذا عقباه.
And also, indeed God Almighty said: 'Allah has not taken any son, nor was there with Him any god.' Then He said: 'Then each god would have taken away what he created' [23:91]. Then God, exalted is His praise, never created an accident except that He placed upon it a proof by which it is known that He created it, since accidents are as we mentioned. And it is possible that in His creation there is a creation that gathers, separates, moves, and becomes still while we do not see it, just as there are among them those whom we do not see by their substance even though they are seen. And those actions themselves are not seen; they are only seen and known by the change of states upon the substance. So if there are substances that are not seen, it is possible from them the like of them that (God) did not make for what He created a sign nor went away with it. So how contradictory is the statement of the Mu'tazila to the statement of the atheists, while they are their partners in this aspect. So we ask God for deliverance from the end result of this statement.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This argument begins with a Qur'anic citation (Surah al-Mu'minun, 23:91) which negates any other god by stating that if there were multiple gods, each would take away what he created. Maturidi then makes a theological point: God never creates an accident (عرض) without placing upon it a proof indicating His creation. Since accidents are the indicators of creation. He then posits a hypothetical: it is possible that there exist created beings who can gather, separate, move, and become still, yet we do not see them (like angels, jinn). Such beings might have actions that are not directly seen but are known through changes in substances. If there are invisible substances (جواهر), it is possible that God did not make for what He created a sign or proof. Then Maturidi criticizes the Mu'tazila for contradicting themselves: on one hand, they deny that humans create their own actions (as per their doctrine), but their arguments would align with atheists (الملحدة) because both would deny that God is the sole creator of actions. In that aspect, they are partners with the atheists. Maturidi seeks God's deliverance from the consequences of such a statement.

Key Terms
إله
خلق
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Quran 23:91 as cited, what would happen if there were multiple gods?
A) They would cooperate in creation.
B) Each would take away what he created.
C) They would fight for dominance.
D) They would share creation equally.

Key Takeaway
Quran 23:91 provides a logical argument for Tawhid: multiple gods would inevitably lead to conflict over creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there any 'god' in your life—a person, a desire, a goal—that you treat as if it has independent power over your destiny? What would it take to dethrone it?
?
The verse implies that if you claim ultimate authority over any part of your life, you are acting as if you were a god. Where do you most resist submitting to Allah's exclusive lordship?
?
Maturidi says every accident—every passing thought, emotion, event—bears proof that it is created by Allah. When you reflect on your daily experiences, do you see them as created and controlled by Him, or as random? How would that realization change your reliance on Him?

The Critique of the Mu'tazila and Unseen Creation
Maturidi critiques the Mu'tazila for aligning with atheists in denying God's exclusive creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazila position on human action contradicts the Quranic proof of sole creation and aligns with atheism.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila, by claiming humans create actions, inadvertently agree with atheists.

In this part of the text, Maturidi extends the argument to address the Mu'tazila view that humans can create their own actions. He uses the possibility of unseen creatures and invisible accidents to show that the Mu'tazila's logic leads to the same conclusion as atheists: that God did not create everything. This lesson explores that critique.

Guided Path
1
Consider the possibility of unseen creation
Maturidi suggests that there might be created beings that gather, separate, move, and become still, yet we do not see them, similar to angels or jinn.
This establishes that not all creations are visible.
2
Understand how actions are known
Actions themselves are not directly seen; they are known through changes in substances. This is a subtle metaphysical point.
3
Grasp the contradiction of the Mu'tazila
The Mu'tazila claim that humans create their actions, while the Quran shows God creates everything. Maturidi argues this aligns them with atheists who deny God's sole creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi criticize the Mu'tazila in this passage?
A) Because their views on human actions align with atheistic conclusions about creation.
B) Because they reject the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi exposes the Mu'tazila as inconsistent: by attributing creation to humans, they fall into agreement with atheists who deny God's exclusive creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you take credit for a good deed or blame yourself for a sin, do you momentarily forget that every action—your success and failure—is created by Allah alone? How can you train your heart to acknowledge His exclusive creation in every breath you take? Will you start each day by saying 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' to affirm that all power belongs to Him?
?
The Mu'tazila thought they were defending God's justice by giving humans free will, but Maturidi shows they slipped into denying God's sole creation. Are there areas in your life where you subtly believe you create your own outcomes—your career, your character, your salvation—independent of Allah's will? Will you repent from that hidden shirk and rely entirely on Him?
?
If your actions were not truly created by Allah, then your prayers, your charity, and your faith would be meaningless acts without His involvement. Can you live with that emptiness? Commit today to reciting 'Qul inni la amliku lakum darran wa la rushda' (Say: I do not derive sustenance from anyone but Allah) and surrender every deed to Him.

Quranic Proof that Allah Creates All Acts
Maturidi cites Quranic verses to argue for divine creation of acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses Quranic verses to prove that all acts, including speech and inner states, are created by Allah.

Core Idea
Allah's argument from creation to knowledge requires Him as Creator of all acts.

Maturidi begins by presenting the scriptural foundation for the doctrine of the creation of acts. He selects key verses where Allah Himself argues from His creation to His attributes, showing that the acts of creatures are signs of His power and knowledge. This lesson examines those verses and the logic behind them.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verses
Read the Arabic verses Maturidi cites along with their translation. Notice how each verse attributes an action or state to Allah.
2
Understand Maturidi's argument
For each verse, Maturidi explains why it implies divine creation of the act. Pay attention to his reasoning, especially from the verse 'Does He who created not know?'
This is a key logical inference.
3
Reflect on the summary
Maturidi concludes with general statements: Allah does what He wills, and believers are obliged to praise Him for faith. Consider how these points tie the evidence together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الدليل عندنا من طريق القرآن على لزوم القول بخلق الأفعال قوله: ﴿وأسرُّوا قولكم أو اجهروا به إنه عليم بذات الصدور ألا يعلم من خلق وهو اللطيف الخبير﴾ [الملك، ١٣-١٤]، فلو لم يكن جل ثناؤه خالقاً لما يُجهر ويُخفى لم يكن ليحتج به على علمه. ومعلوم جواز الجهل من غير الذي يفعله، فإذا لم يكن للاحتجاج بفعل سواه معنى. وأيضاً إن الله تعالى قال: ﴿هو الذي يسيّركم في البر والبحر﴾ [يونس، ٢٢]، وقال في موضع آخر: ﴿وقدرنا فيها السير سيروا فيها ليالي﴾ [سبأ، ١٨]، أخبر أن تقدير السير والتسيير فعله، وبه كان السير ومن كان يسير. وقال: ﴿ومن آياته أن خلق لكم من أنفسكم أزواجاً لتسكنوا إليها وجعل بينكم مودة ورحمة﴾ [الروم، ٢١]، فيها خبر أن جعل المودة والرحمة من آياته، وأن منامكم من آياته وابتغاءكم من فضله من آياته. ومن البعيد إنشاء غيره له من الآيات؛ إذ ذلك الفاعل أحق أن تكون الآية له، وهن كلهن أفعال الخلق. وقال: ﴿وجعلنا في قلوب الذين اتبعوه رأفة ورحمة﴾ [الحديد، ٢٧]، وقال: ﴿أولئك كتب في قلوبهم الإيمان﴾ [المجادلة، ٢٢]، وقال: ﴿وجعل لكم من جلود الأنعام بيوتاً﴾ [النحل، ٨٠]، وقال: ﴿وجعلنا قلوبهم قاسية﴾ [المائدة، ١٣]. وفي الجملة قال الله: ﴿فعّال لما يريد﴾، وفي أفعال العباد ما يريد، وقد وعد أن يفعل ما يريد. وقد ذم الله من أحب أن يُحمد على ما لم يفعل، وقد ألزم المؤمنين أن يحمدوه على الإيمان، فثبت أن ذلك بفعله. ولا قوة إلا بالله.

والأصل فيه أن دلالة خلق فعل كل أحد عنده أعظم من دلالة خلق السموات والأرض فيما أريد تعرف حقيقة ذلك بالعقل؛ إنه لا أحد امتحن قوى جواهر العالم حتى يعلم خروج كل شيء عن ذلك واحتمال خلق مثله، بل إنما يعرف ذلك بخروجه عن إمكان مثله. ومعلوم وجود أمور في غيره من الجواهر مما امتنع جوهره عن احتمال ذلك، نحو الطيران وإخراق الأشياء والسباحة بالجواهر وغير ذلك بقوى فيها. ويعلم كل أن ليس لأحد من الخلق تدبير في فعله؛ فيعلم بالضرورة بما خرج عن مقصوده وقصر عن الحد الذي يحده، وكان مقدّراً بما لا يحتمل وسعه التقدير به، فيعلم به ضرورةً أن الذي به قام هو الذي قدّره وأخرجه على ما أراد ولا قوة إلا بالله.

مع ما لم يكن عند المعتزلة من الله إلى خلقه جملة سوى أنه أوجده بعد أن لم يكن، وأن الله لم يزل موجوداً؛ وذلك المعنى في فعل كل أحد موجود. على أنه لولا الأمر والنهي لم يكن العقل يحتمل إخراج شيء عن قدرة الله وصرف شيء إلى فعل غيره. والأمر والنهي في محل حق بالمعنى الذي يلزم القول به، لولاهما لم يلزم ذلك؛ فيكون المعروف بالعقل وما يوجبه ضرورة ذلك مدفوعاً بالجهل بالحكمة والحادث. ولو جاز ذلك لجاز إنكار الأمر.
Then the proof according to us from the way of the Qur'an for the necessity of affirming the creation of acts is His statement: 'And conceal your speech or publicize it; indeed, He is Knower of that within the breasts. Does He who created not know, while He is the Subtle, the Acquainted?' [Al-Mulk 13-14]. For if He, exalted be His praise, were not the Creator of what is spoken aloud and what is concealed, He would not have argued by it for His knowledge. And the possibility of ignorance is known from other than the one who does it. So if there is no meaning in arguing by an act of another. Also, Allah the Exalted said: 'It is He who causes you to travel on land and sea' [Yunus 22], and He said in another place: 'And We determined in it journeys; travel therein nights' [Saba' 18], informing that the determination of journeys and causing to travel is His act, and by it travel existed and whoever traveled. And He said: 'And of His signs is that He created for you from yourselves mates that you may find tranquility in them, and He placed between you affection and mercy' [Ar-Rum 21], containing the report that the placing of affection and mercy is among His signs, and that your sleep is among His signs and your seeking of His bounty is among His signs. And it is far-fetched that another should originate these signs; for that doer would be more deserving that the sign should belong to him, and they are all acts of the creatures. And He said: 'And We placed in the hearts of those who followed him compassion and mercy' [Al-Hadid 27], and He said: 'Those, He has inscribed faith in their hearts' [Al-Mujadilah 22], and He said: 'And Allah has made for you from the hides of the cattle houses' [An-Nahl 80], and He said: 'And We made their hearts hard' [Al-Ma'idah 13]. And in summary, Allah said: 'Doer of whatever He wills' [Hud 107, etc.], and among the acts of the servants is what He wills, and He has promised to do what He wills. And Allah has condemned those who love to be praised for what they did not do, and He has obliged the believers to praise Him for faith, so it is established that this is by His act. And there is no power except through Allah.

And the principle in this is that the indication of the creation of the act of everyone is greater in His sight than the indication of the creation of the heavens and the earth, in that which is intended to be known by reason of the reality of that: that no one has tested the powers of the substances of the world so as to know the emergence of everything from that and the possibility of creating its like; rather, it is only known by its emergence from the possibility of its like. And it is known that there exist things in other substances such that their substance is prevented from containing that, such as flying, penetrating objects, swimming by the substance, and other things by powers in them. And everyone knows that no creature has management in his act; so it is known by necessity, by what has gone beyond his intention and fallen short of the limit he sets, and was determined by what his capacity cannot bear to determine, so it is known by necessity that the one by which he stood is the one who determined it and brought it forth as He willed. And there is no power except through Allah.

Along with what was not with the Mu'tazila from Allah to His creatures at all except that He brought it into existence after it was non-existent, and that Allah did not cease to exist; and that meaning exists in the act of everyone. However, were it not for the command and prohibition, reason could not bear to remove something from the power of Allah and attribute something to the act of another. And the command and prohibition are in the place of truth in the sense that requires affirming them; without them that would not be required. So what is known by reason and what its necessity necessitates would be repelled by ignorance of wisdom and the contingent. And if that were permissible, then denying the command would be permissible.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's scriptural and rational proof for the doctrine that all acts of creatures are created by Allah, a key point against the Mu'tazila who held that humans create their own acts. The passage begins with Quranic evidence: verses where Allah argues from His creation to His knowledge (Al-Mulk 13-14) show that He must be the creator of both hidden and public speech. If He were not, the argument would be invalid. Similarly, verses about causing travel (Yunus 22, Saba' 18) attribute the determination and causing of movement to Allah. Verses about placing affection, mercy, sleep, faith, hardness of heart, and making houses from skins all show that these inner and outer states are acts of Allah — indeed they are called 'signs' (ayat), and it is unlikely that another could originate them. Maturidi then generalizes: Allah is 'Doer of whatever He wills' and among the servants' acts is what He wills. Also, Allah condemns those who want praise for what they did not do, and He obliges believers to praise Him for faith — implying faith is His act.

Then Maturidi adds a rational principle: the evidence for creation of each person's act is even greater than that for creation of the heavens and earth, when considered by reason. No one can know the full capacities of the world's substances to confirm that a creature could create. Rather, we know that things occur beyond a creature's intention and capacity (like flying, penetrating, swimming by nature), showing that the act is determined by another. Thus, by necessity, the one who brings the act into existence is Allah.

Finally, Maturidi engages with Mu'tazila theory: they hold that Allah only brings acts into existence from non-existence (as they consider acts 'things' before existence), but this meaning exists in every act. However, without the revealed command and prohibition, reason would not remove any act from Allah's power and attribute it to the creature. The command and prohibition are true in the sense that they require this attribution; without them, rational knowledge would be overturned by ignorance of wisdom and contingency. Denying this would lead to denying the command itself.

Key Terms
خلق الأفعال
دليل سمعي
فعّال لما يريد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think humans create their own acts independently.
Correction: Maturidi shows that Quranic verses consistently attribute acts to Allah, not to humans.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's argument from Al-Mulk 13-14, why must Allah be the creator of what is spoken aloud and concealed?
A) Because only the Creator can know hidden things.
B) Because if He were not the creator, He could not argue by it for His knowledge.
C) Because the verse directly says He created speech.
D) Because humans cannot create speech at all.

Key Takeaway
The Quran repeatedly attributes acts of creatures to Allah, establishing that He is the creator of all acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often feel your faith is your own achievement. After reading that Allah inscribed faith in hearts, will you thank Him today for every moment of belief, or will you still take credit?
?
If Allah can make hearts hard, and you feel your own heart growing hard, what will you do to seek His softening? Does this lesson change how you pray for guidance?
?
Allah placed affection and mercy between you and your spouse. When was the last time you acknowledged Him as the source of that love, rather than assuming it came naturally?

The Rational Evidence for Creation of Acts
Maturidi presents a rational proof for creation of acts from the limits of human capacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational argument that the creation of each person's act is more evident than the creation of the heavens and earth.

Core Idea
The act's limitations prove it is created by another.

Maturidi now deepens the argument with a rational principle. He claims that the evidence for the creation of every individual's act is actually greater than the evidence for the creation of the heavens and earth. This seems counterintuitive, but he explains why: we can directly observe the limitations of human capacity in our own acts.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the principle
Maturidi says we know creation of acts better than creation of the universe. This is because we experience our own limitations directly.
Think about why this might be.
2
Understand the analogy
He points out that no one can fully assess the potentials of substances in the world. But we do see limits: we cannot fly or penetrate objects etc. These limits show the act is beyond our power.
We don't have full control.
3
Conclude the necessity
Since the act exceeds our capacity, it must be from a superior agent. This is known by necessity.
This leads to the conclusion that Allah is the creator.

Source Text
