Worldly Hudud as Expiation for Sins
Maturidi argues worldly hudud expiate sins to prove eternal punishment for kufr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the concept of worldly legal penalties (hudud) as expiations to prove that kufr (disbelief) has no expiation and therefore its punishment is eternal.

Core Idea
If hudud expiate sins, then kufr with no expiation must have eternal punishment.

Maturidi presents a creative argument: the very existence of worldly penalties shows that they serve as expiations for sins. Because sins less than kufr can be expiated through hudud, kufr—which has no such expiation—must have a punishment that is eternal. This lesson walks through the logical chain.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the original Arabic text and its literal translation to familiarize yourself with the argument's wording.
Pay attention to the conditional phrase 'فلو لم يكن فيها تكفير' (for if there were no expiation in them).
2
Identify the three premises
Break down the argument: (a) hudud are expiations; (b) if not, they'd be extra punishment for lesser sins, which is impossible; (c) no expiation for kufr in this world.
The impossibility in (b) rests on the idea that punishment for lesser sins cannot exceed that for disbelief.
3
Understand the conclusion
From (a)-(c), Maturidi concludes: kufr's punishment is eternal, while other sins have limited punishment via hudud.
The final clause 'فكذلك العقوبة الموعودة فيه' links the worldly pattern to the afterlife.
4
Reflect on proportionality
Consider why Maturidi thinks adding punishment for lesser sins is impossible. This reveals a principle of divine justice: punishment is proportionate to sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إن الحدود في الدنيا جعلن كفارات لما يُرتكب من الذنوب، فلو لم يكن فيها تكفير كانت تكون زيادات على عقوبات الكفر ، ومحال أن يزداد عقوبة ما دون الكفر، فثبت أنها كفارات، ولا كفارة للكفر في الدنيا ؛ ثبت أنه لا يحتمل الحد في العقوبة، فجعلت عقوبته أبدية وعقوبة غيره بحد فكذلك العقوبة الموعودة فيه . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And also, the legal penalties in this world were made expiations for sins committed. For if there were no expiation in them, they would be additions to the punishments of kufr, and it is impossible that the punishment for what is less than kufr be increased. So it is established that they are expiations, and there is no expiation for kufr in this world; it is established that it does not admit a fixed penalty in punishment, so its punishment was made eternal, and the punishment of other than it by a fixed penalty—thus also the promised punishment for it [is eternal]. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage is an additional proof. The author argues that worldly legal penalties (hudud) were instituted as expiations (kaffarat) for sins. If they were not expiatory, they would be extra punishments on top of the punishment for kufr (disbelief). But it is impossible for sins less than kufr to receive increased punishment relative to kufr. Therefore, hudud must be expiations. Since there is no expiation for kufr in this world (no worldly punishment can expiate it), kufr does not admit a fixed penalty; hence its punishment in the afterlife is eternal. For other sins, a fixed penalty (like hudud) suffices, so their punishment is limited.

Key Terms
الحدود (hudud/legal penalties)
الكفارات (expiations)
الكفر (kufr/disbelief)
العقوبة الأبدية (eternal punishment)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think hudud are merely punitive and not expiatory.
Correction: This passage clarifies that hudud are indeed expiations for sins less than kufr, as indicated by the impossibility of them being extra punishments for lesser sins.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude from the fact that there is no worldly expiation for kufr?
A) Kufr is a small sin.
B) Kufr's punishment is eternal.
C) Kufr is expiated by repentance.
D) Kufr has no punishment.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses the fact that worldly legal penalties serve as expiations for lesser sins to prove that kufr, lacking any such expiation, incurs eternal punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that no worldly penalty can expiate kufr, do you treat the state of your own iman with urgency, or do you assume it is secure without examination? What specific step will you take today to verify the strength of your faith?
?
If hudud are expiations for lesser sins, are you grateful for the mercy of fixed punishments in this life, or do you resent them? How does this realization change your view of Islamic legal penalties?
?
This passage proves that eternal punishment is just for kufr. Does that reality make you fear for anyone you know who lives in disbelief? What will you do differently to guide them, starting now?

The Doubling of Punishment for Misleaders (Quran 29:13)
Maturidi uses Quran 29:13 to argue that kufr alone is punished singly, while leading astray doubles it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses Quran 29:13 to argue that the punishment for kufr alone is single, not doubled.

Core Idea
Kufr alone is punished singly; leading others astray doubles the punishment.

This lesson presents Maturidi's fourth argument from the Quran. He cites Surah al-Ankabut, verse 13, which states that those who disbelieve and mislead others will bear double punishment. Through a clever inference, Maturidi shows that the baseline punishment for kufr must be single, otherwise every disbeliever would already be subject to double punishment. This argument helps clarify the relationship between kufr and other major sins.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once silently, then read the literal translation. Note the key terms: 'doubling', 'kufr', 'leading astray'.
2
Understand the Quranic verse cited
Identify that the verse (29:13) promises double punishment for those who both disbelieve and mislead others. Recognize that Allah specifically reserves 'doubling' for misleaders.
The verse is: 'And they will certainly bear their own loads and other loads along with their loads.'
3
Follow Maturidi's hypothetical reasoning
Maturidi argues: If the basic punishment for kufr were already doubled (due to other sins), then every disbeliever would have doubled punishment. But since all disbelievers commit other major sins, this would make the verse redundant. Therefore, basic kufr must have a single punishment.
This is a logical inference from the specificity of the verse.
4
Conclude the distinction
Understand that Maturidi uses this to show that kufr and other sins (like leading astray) are distinct, with kufr having a single eternal punishment and additional sins adding extra punishment.
This reinforces the earlier arguments about kufr being a standalone category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إن الله جل ثناؤه أخبر أن الموعودة [في] عقوبة الذين كفروا وأضلوا غيرهم ضعف عقوبة من كفر ولم يضل غيره. ثم لو كان للكافر عقوبة غير الإضلال مثل عقوبة الإضلال لكان كل كافر عقوبته مضاعفة، لأنه لا كافر إلا ومعه سوى الكفر كبائر، وقد خص الله بالمضاعفة المضلين بقوله : وليحملُن أثقالهم وأثقالاً مع أثقالهم ﴾ [العنكبوت، ١٣/٢٩]،
And also, Allah, exalted be His praise, informed that the promised punishment for those who disbelieve and lead others astray is double the punishment of one who disbelieves but does not lead others astray. Then if the disbeliever had a punishment other than for misguidance equal to the punishment for misguidance, then every disbeliever's punishment would be doubled, because there is no disbeliever except that with him besides kufr are major sins. And Allah specifically reserved doubling for the misleaders by His saying: 'And they will certainly bear their own loads and other loads along with their loads' (Surah al-Ankabut, 29:13).
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage is a proof from scripture (samiyyat). The author cites the Quranic verse (29:13) which states that those who disbelieve and also lead others astray will bear double punishment. If the basic punishment for kufr were already doubled (by including other sins), then every disbeliever would have a doubled punishment—since every disbeliever inevitably commits other major sins besides kufr. But the verse specifically attributes doubling to those who mislead, implying that the basic punishment for kufr alone is single. This supports the distinction between kufr and other sins: kufr has a single eternal punishment, while leading astray adds another layer.

Key Terms
المضاعفة (doubling)
الكفر (kufr)
الإضلال (leading astray)
الآية (verse)
الأثقال (loads)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All disbelievers receive the same punishment regardless of additional sins.
Correction: This verse shows that those who also mislead others receive double punishment, so basic kufr is punished singly.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi infer from the Quranic verse about those who disbelieve and lead others astray?
A) All disbelievers receive double punishment.
B) Only those who lead others astray receive double punishment, implying basic kufr is punished singly.
C) Leading astray is not a major sin.
D) The verse refers to believers.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses Quran 29:13 to prove that kufr alone has a single punishment, while leading astray adds extra punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you aware of how your actions, words, or even your silence might lead others away from the truth? This lesson shows the severe consequence of misguidance. How will you actively guard against being a cause of misguidance for those who look up to you?
?
The lesson distinguishes between the single punishment for personal kufr and the doubled punishment for leading others astray. Which of these two weights is currently heavier on your conscience: your own shortcomings or the influence you have on the people around you? Commit to one specific change in your life to ensure you are not burdening others with your example.
?
If the punishment for disbelief alone is single, yet you are accountable for every sin you commit, how does this motivate you to take even the 'small' sins seriously? Are you living with the awareness that every sin you commit is a load you will bear, and that you cannot shift that weight onto others? What will you do today to lighten your load by seeking forgiveness?

First Argument: Sinners Have Overbalancing Good Deeds
Maturidi presents the first argument for forgiveness of major sins (excluding shirk).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's first argument that major sins other than shirk can be forgiven because the sinner's concurrent acts of obedience outweigh his transgression, while shirk leaves no room for such mercy.

Core Idea
Even in sinning, a believer's fear and hope produce good deeds that outweigh his evil.

Imam Maturidi begins his case for the forgiveness of major sins (excluding shirk) with a profound observation: the sinner is never entirely devoid of obedience. While committing a sin, he simultaneously manifests fear of God, hope for His mercy, and trust in His generosity. These motivations constitute genuine good deeds that can outweigh his transgression. This passage lays out that argument and contrasts it with the situation of the disbeliever, who lacks such deeds entirely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to get the overall argument.
2
Identify the key components of the argument
Notice the premise: every sinner of major sins (excluding shirk) has some obedience at the time of sinning. Then see how fear, dread, hope, and trust produce good deeds that outweigh the evil. Finally, follow the conclusion that it is not permissible to deprive him of the benefit of those good deeds.
3
Contrast with the disbeliever
The passage then turns to the disbeliever, who has no such good deeds because he denies God. This explains why shirk is not forgivable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدها أنه ما من أحد [الله؟] بنوع من الكبائر دون الشرك يعصى إلا وهو لوقت العصيان مكتسب الطاعة من خوف عقابه، والفزع عن مقته، ورجاء رحمته، والثقة بكرمه وذلك عن خيرات لو قوبل بها ما ارتكب من الخلاف بغلبة شهوة وقهر غضب أو نحو ذلك ليرجُح ما كان منه من خير على ما كان من شر، فلا يجوز أن يُحْرَم نفعَ الخير ويوجب له عقوبة الشر، ومن ذلك فعله [فهو] موصوف بالجود والكرم، ولا كذلك معناهما ولا قوة إلا بالله وليس مع من يكفر بالله ويشرك به معنى يستحق اسم الحسنة والخير، لأنه يكذبه وينكر أمره ونهيه، فلا يحتمل أن يكون له الرجاء وفي دوام عذابه مضادة معنى الكرم والجود ولا قوة إلا بالله.
One of them is that there is no one who commits a major sin without shirk except that at the time of sinning he is acquiring obedience due to fear of His punishment, dread of His displeasure, hope of His mercy, and trust in His generosity; and that is on account of good deeds which, if they were compared, the transgression committed due to overpowering desire, anger, or the like would be outweighed, so that his good outweighs his evil. Therefore it is not permissible to deprive him of the benefit of the good and to necessitate for him the punishment of evil. Moreover, His action is described as generosity and liberality, but the meaning of these is not so for one who disbelieves in Allah and associates partners with Him, for he has no meaning that deserves the name of good deed or virtue, because he denies Him and rejects His command and prohibition. So it is not possible for him to have hope, and in the permanence of his punishment there is contradiction to the meaning of generosity and liberality. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument (al-wajh al-awwal) for why major sins other than shirk can be forgiven, while shirk cannot. The author argues that the sinner of major sins (excluding shirk) at the moment of sinning is still 'acquiring obedience' through fear of God's punishment, dread of His displeasure, hope of His mercy, and trust in His generosity. These motivations produce good deeds that outweigh the evil of the sin. Therefore, it would be unjust ('la yajuz') to punish him with evil and deprive him of the benefit of his good deeds. This is consistent with God's attributes of generosity (jūd) and liberality (karam). In contrast, the disbeliever has no such good deeds because he denies God and rejects His command, so forgiveness for him would contradict divine generosity.

Key Terms
الكبائر
الشرك
الحسنة
الخير
الجود
الكرم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All sins, including shirk, are equally punishable and there is no hope for forgiveness for any major sin.
Correction: Maturidi argues that major sins other than shirk are accompanied by good deeds that outweigh them, allowing for forgiveness. Shirk is different because the disbeliever lacks any such good deeds.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's first argument, why can major sins other than shirk be forgiven?
A) Because the sinner repents after sinning
B) Because the sinner has good deeds from fear and hope that outweigh the sin
C) Because God does not punish any sins
D) Because the sinner's sin is not really evil

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's first argument shows that major sins other than shirk can be forgiven because the sinner's concurrent acts of obedience outweigh his transgression, consistent with divine generosity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you actually feel the fear and hope described here, or do you just move on? What would it take for you to truly feel both every time?
?
This argument says your good deeds (like feeling fear) outweigh your sins. Do you live as if that is true? Or does guilt still weigh you down? What would change if you fully believed this?
?
If the disbeliever has no good deeds at all, does that make you feel entitled? Or does it deepen your gratitude that you have faith? How does this shape the urgency of your dua for others?

The Eternal Punishment for Shirk: A Logical Argument
Maturidi argues that shirk's unique lack of good deeds necessitates eternal punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's second argument that the unique magnitude of shirk and its lack of good deeds necessitates eternal punishment.

Core Idea
Shirk's punishment is eternal because it has no good deeds, and justice requires 'like for like'.

Imam Maturidi presents a second argument focusing on the nature of shirk itself. He argues that shirk is not just any sin—it is the greatest sin according to reason, and crucially, it has no good deeds to offset it. Therefore, its just recompense must be eternal, unlike other sins which may have mitigating factors.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own reasoning come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: principle, application, illustration, conclusion.
2
Identify the key principles
Find the principle of 'like for like' recompense and the claim that shirk is the greatest sin with no good deeds. These are the premises.
Also note the contrast with other sins that have good deeds.
3
Understand the logical reasoning
The argument uses a hypothetical: the disbeliever would accept multiplied punishment for a day of salvation. This shows that eternity is the just measure.
Ask yourself why a lesser punishment would be unjust.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني أن الله جل ثناؤه وعد أن لا يجزى إلا مثلها، ومثل الشرك الذي في العقل أكبر من كل ذنب، مع ما لا حسنة تكون معه و [تكون] مع غيره، إنما [جزاؤه] هو الخلود في النار. إذ معلوم أن الكافر يرضى بأضعاف ما يعذب مع النجاة يوما من الدهر، فيبين ذلك أن تمام جزائه الخلود. فإذا كان لغيره مثله فيُجْزَى غيره أكثر من مثل الفعل، وذلك جور في حكمة، والله يَجِلّ عنه. فهذا مع ما كان لمرتكب ما دونه حسنات، وليس معه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And the second: that Allah, the Exalted, promised that He will not recompense except with the like of it. And the like of shirk in the intellect is greater than every sin, along with the fact that no good deed exists with it while [good deeds] exist with others. Indeed, his recompense is eternity in the Fire. For it is known that the disbeliever would be content with many times the punishment with salvation for a day, so that shows that the full extent of his recompense is eternity. So if another has the like of it, then that other would be recompensed with more than the like of the act, and that would be injustice in wisdom, and Allah is exalted above that. This is along with the fact that the committer of what is less than it has good deeds, while he does not. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (al-wajh al-thānī). It begins with the principle that God recompenses 'like for like' (that is, the punishment fits the sin). Shirk, in the intellect ('aql), is the greatest sin, and importantly, it has no accompanying good deeds (ḥasanāt). Good deeds exist with other sins, so those sinners have mitigating factors. The disbeliever would willingly endure immense punishment in exchange for a single day of salvation, proving that the full, just recompense for shirk is eternity. If other sins were treated like shirk, they would receive more than their due, which would be unjust ('jūr fī ḥikmah). Thus, shirk's punishment is eternal, while others are not.

Key Terms
الشرك
الجزاء
الخلود
الحسنات
العدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think shirk is punished eternally simply because it is a sin, not because of its unique magnitude and absence of good deeds.
Correction: Maturidi argues that it is specifically the combination of being the greatest sin and having no good deeds that necessitates eternity. Other sins are not eternal because they have good deeds that balance them.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, why is the recompense for shirk eternal?
A) Because God promised eternal punishment for all sins
B) Because shirk has no good deeds and is the greatest sin
C) Because the intellect cannot conceive of any punishment
D) Because the disbeliever does not want forgiveness

Key Takeaway
Shirk's eternal punishment is logically necessary because it is the greatest sin and has no good deeds, making any lesser punishment unjust.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If shirk has no good deeds at all, what does that say about your own deeds when you commit a lesser sin? Do you rely on your good deeds to 'balance' your sins, or do you rely on Allah's mercy? What must change in your heart today?
?
Maturidi argues that the disbeliever would willingly endure multiplied punishment for a day of salvation. What does that tell you about the desperation of those who reject Allah? How does that move you to act towards them in your words and actions?
?
This argument shows that shirk is uniquely deserving of eternal punishment. Search your heart: Is there any form of shirk—even subtle, like showing off in worship (riya') or trusting your own plans over Allah's decree—that you tolerate? What concrete step will you take to remove it starting today?

Third Argument: Hudud as Expiation, No Expiation for Shirk
Maturidi argues that hudud are expiations, but no expiation for shirk, so its punishment is eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that prescribed punishments (hudud) are expiations for sins, and that the absence of expiation for shirk implies its eternal punishment.

Core Idea
There is no expiation for shirk in this world, so its punishment is eternal.

Maturidi presents a third argument based on the nature of prescribed punishments (hudud) in this world. He argues that these punishments serve as expiations (kaffarat) for sins. If they were not expiations, they would be increases upon the punishment for disbelief, which is impossible. Therefore, since there is no expiation for disbelief in this world, its punishment must be eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the logical connectors like 'فلو' and 'فثبت'.
The argument is concise; pay attention to the conditional reasoning.
2
Identify the two main claims
Identify that the passage first establishes that hudud are expiations, then concludes that because there is no expiation for kufr, its punishment is eternal.
3
Reflect on the logical structure
Consider why the author thinks it's impossible for a lesser sin to have a punishment exceeding that of a greater sin. This underpins the whole argument.
This is a key principle in Islamic theology: punishment must be proportional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إن الحدود في الدنيا جعلن كفارات لما يُرتكب من الذنوب، فلو لم يكن فيها تكفير كانت تكون زيادات على عقوبات الكفر، ومحال أن يزداد عقوبة ما دون الكفر، فثبت أنها كفارات، ولا كفارة للكفر في الدنيا؛ ثبت أنه لا يحتمل الحد في العقوبة، فجعلت عقوبته أبدية وعقوبة غيره بحد فكذلك العقوبة الموعودة فيه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also, the prescribed punishments (hudud) in this world are made expiations for the sins committed. If they were not expiations, they would be increases upon the punishments of disbelief, and it is impossible that the punishment of what is less than disbelief should be increased. So it is established that they are expiations, and there is no expiation for disbelief in this world; it is established that it does not admit of a prescribed punishment, so its punishment is made eternal, and the punishment of others is by a fixed measure, and likewise the promised punishment for it. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage presents a third argument (introduced by 'wa ayḍan'). It focuses on the prescribed punishments (ḥudūd) of this world. The author asserts that these punishments serve as expiations (kaffārāt) for the sins committed. If they were not expiations, they would add to the punishment for disbelief (kufr), which is impossible because the punishment for a lesser sin cannot exceed that for a greater one. Therefore, the ḥudūd are indeed expiations. However, there is no such expiation for disbelief in this world—no prescribed punishment that expiates it. Thus, disbelief does not admit of a finite punishment (ḥadd), so its punishment in the afterlife is eternal, while the punishment for other sins is measured (bi-ḥadd).

Key Terms
الحدود
الكفارات
العقوبة
الكفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prescribed punishments are purely punitive and have no expiatory function.
Correction: Maturidi argues that hudud are specifically made as expiations (kaffarat) for sins, serving to cleanse the sinner in this world.

Checkpoint
What does the third argument use to show that shirk's punishment is eternal?
A) The fact that hudud are not expiations
B) The fact that hudud exist for disbelief
C) The fact that there is no expiation for shirk in this world
D) The fact that all sins have prescribed punishments

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that because there is no prescribed punishment (hadd) that can expiate shirk in this world, its punishment must be eternal, unlike other sins which can be expiated through hudud.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly recognize that the hardships or punishments you face in this life might be expiations from a Merciful Lord, or do you resent them? How does this passage change your perspective on your own trials?
?
Since there is no expiation for shirk in this world, what does that reveal about the urgency of purifying your heart from even hidden forms of associating partners with Allah? What specific step will you take today to guard against shirk?
?
If you truly believed that every sin besides shirk can be expiated through prescribed punishments (whether legal penalties or divine decrees), would you still fear God's punishment in the same way? How does this understanding affect your hope in God's mercy and your fear of His justice?

Fourth Argument: Doubling Punishment for Leading Astray
Maturidi argues that double punishment is specific to misleaders.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the doubling of punishment is specific to those who lead others astray, not a general feature of disbelief.

Core Idea
Double punishment is only for misleaders, not all disbelievers.

Maturidi presents a fourth argument based on Quranic verses that specify double punishment for those who both disbelieve and lead others astray. He argues that if all disbelievers received double punishment due to their other sins, the specification would be meaningless. This passage shows the uniqueness of shirk's punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic and English translation of the passage carefully. Notice the logical flow from premise to conclusion.
The argument begins with 'wa ayḍan' indicating it is a continuation.
2
Identify the Quranic proof
Locate the Quranic verse mentioned: 'They will bear their burdens and other burdens along with their burdens' (29:13) and the followers' plea. These are the scriptural basis.
3
Understand the deduction
Trace the logical deduction: if all disbelievers had double punishment, then the specification for misleaders would be redundant. Since Allah specifically singled out misleaders, the doubling must be for misguidance, not for other sins.
This refutes the idea that doubling is for major sins.
4
Contrast with major sins
Note the distinction: a disbeliever is punished for all sins, but the doubling is only for leading astray. This shows that shirk's punishment is unique.
Think about how this argument supports the distinct nature of disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إن الله جل ثناؤه أخبر أن الموعودة [في] عقوبة الذين كفروا وأضلوا غيرهم ضعف عقوبة من كفر ولم يضل غيره. ثم لو كان للكافر عقوبة غير الإضلال مثل عقوبة الإضلال لكان كل كافر عقوبته مضاعفة، لأنه لا كافر إلا ومعه سوى الكفر كبائر، وقد خص الله بالمضاعفة المضلين بقوله: وليحملُن أثقالهم وأثقالاً مع أثقالهم [العنكبوت، 13/29]، وقول الأتباع: ربنا هؤلاء أضلونا اليه الآية، وجعل لكل ضعفًا، فبطل أن يكون ذلك عقوبة الكبيرة، بل هي لو كانت في الكفر كان أحق للضعف منه في الإسلام للمثل؛ ألا ترى أنه يعاقب الكافر بجميع الآثام من صغائر وكبائر، ولا كذلك أمر من اعتقد دين الإسلام. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also, Allah, the Exalted, informed that the promise of punishment for those who disbelieved and led others astray is double the punishment of one who disbelieved but did not lead others astray. Then if the disbeliever had a punishment other than misguidance equal to the punishment of misguidance, then every disbeliever's punishment would be doubled, because there is no disbeliever except that along with disbelief he has other major sins. And Allah specifically singled out those who mislead for doubling in His saying: 'They will bear their burdens and other burdens along with their burdens' [Al-Ankabut 29:13], and the saying of the followers: 'Our Lord, these led us astray' etc., and He made for each a double. So it is invalid that this is the punishment of a major sin (kabira). Rather, if it were in disbelief, it would be more deserving of doubling than in Islam for the like. Do you not see that the disbeliever is punished for all sins, minor and major, and not so is the case of one who professes the religion of Islam? There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage presents a fourth argument (introduced by 'wa ayḍan'). It uses a Quranic proof: Allah has promised double punishment for those who disbelieved and misled others, compared to those who disbelieved alone. If all disbelievers received double punishment due to their other major sins (kabā'ir), then the specification for misleaders would be meaningless. But Allah specifically singled out the misleaders for doubling, as in the verse 'They will bear their burdens...' and the followers' plea. This shows that the doubling is not punishment for a major sin (kabira), but for misguidance. If the doubling belonged to disbelief itself, it would be more fitting for disbelief than for Islam, but the disbeliever is punished for all sins, whereas the Muslim is not. Thus, shirk's punishment is unique and distinct from other sins.

Key Terms
المضاعفة
الإضلال
الأثقال
الكبيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All sinners equally face doubled punishment for their sins.
Correction: The Quran specifies that doubling is only for those who both disbelieve and lead others astray.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, why is the punishment for shirk not doubled for all disbelievers?
A) Because only those who lead others astray get double punishment
B) Because all disbelievers get double punishment
C) Because shirk is not a sin
D) Because the Quran does not mention punishment

Key Takeaway
The doubling of punishment in the Quran is specific to those who both disbelieve and mislead others, not a general punishment for disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have been entrusted with knowledge about the severity of leading others astray. Consider your words and actions—do you ever casually share opinions about faith without verifying their truth, potentially causing someone to stumble? This lesson warns that those who mislead receive a doubled punishment. How will you change your behavior today to ensure you are a guide to truth, not a source of misguidance?
?
Reflect on the weight of your influence. If you were to lead someone away from the path of Islam, you would bear their sins as well as your own. Does this realization make you more cautious in how you speak and act around others? Commit to one concrete action—such as seeking knowledge before speaking—to protect yourself from this grave consequence.
?
The lesson highlights that shirk's punishment is unique and severe. Have you ever downplayed the seriousness of shirk or treated other sins as equally grave in your heart? Let this lesson recalibrate your understanding: the sin of misguidance is distinct and carries a double punishment. Ask yourself: Do I truly appreciate the magnitude of disbelief and the responsibility I have to steer others toward truth?

Threat Names: Specificity, Not Generality
Maturidi categorizes threat terms and argues for specific interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi argues that Qur'anic threats are specific to certain sins and not universally applicable, and how to approach ambiguous terms.

Core Idea
Threats are specific; generalizing them contradicts divine pardon.

In this lesson, Imam Maturidi examines the names used in threat verses—such as fisq, fujur, 'isyan, and zulm. He shows that these terms cover three categories of actions, and because they do not always refer to blameworthy acts, the threat must be specific. Furthermore, the existence of verses about Allah's pardon makes a general threat impossible. When ambiguity remains, the proper response is fear, not certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text slowly, then the literal translation. Notice the flow: classification, argument, conclusion.
The passage opens with 'The Shaykh said' and ends with 'wa la quwwata illa billah.'
2
Identify the three types of threat names
The passage states that threat names are of three types. One type may refer to an action that is not blameworthy, another type does not. Find these distinctions in the text.
The third type is implied by the division; Maturidi does not explicitly list all three.
3
Understand the argument for specificity
Notice the two arguments: (1) if a word can have two distant meanings, take the certain one; (2) if threats were general, they would contradict verses of pardon. This proves specificity.
The phrase 'since it is of three divisions and does not include all divisions' is key.
4
Learn the proper stance in ambiguity
When both specific and general interpretations are possible, the right response is fear (khawf), not certainty (qat'). Extracting certainty would contradict wisdom.
The passage warns against 'cutting' (qat') in ambiguous matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : ثم جملة ما جاءت به الآثار في الوعيد بالتسمية من فسق أو فجور أو عصيان أو ظلم أنها أسماء الخصال ثلاث منها ما قد يجوز أن يصير في الحكمة على الإشارة إلى الفعل الذي سمّى لأجله به غير فسق ولا غيره من الأسماء الذميمة، ومنها ما لا يجوز. ومن البعيد قصد شيئين [معا] بينهما هذا التباعد، فحق ذلك أن يُصرف إلى ما لا يرتاب في الاسم والحكم. وإذ كان على أقسام ثلاثة ولم يتضمن كل الأقسام ثبت الخصوص في ذلك، فلزم صرف ذلك إلى ما لا يشك فيه. ولا قوة إلا بالله على أن في الصرف إلى العموم يحقق له التناقض لمجيء أخبار العفو، فثبت بذلك الخصوص. ولا قوة إلا بالله. أو إذا احتمل الخصوص والعموم بما ينقسم المسمى به ما ذكرت فحق مثله الخوف لا القطع، فمن قطع خرج مما يوجبه الحكمة عند الشبه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The Shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: Then the sum of what has come in the traditions regarding the threat of naming (al-wa'id bi al-tasmiya) from transgression (fisq) or immorality (fujur) or disobedience ('isyan) or injustice (zulm) is that they are names of traits (asma' al-khisal) of three types: among them is what may be permissible in wisdom to refer to the action for which he was named with something other than transgression and other blameworthy names, and among them is what is not permissible. And it is far-fetched to intend two things together between which is this distance, so the right of that is to be referred to what is not doubted in name and judgment. And since it is of three divisions and does not include all divisions, specificity (al-khusus) in that is established, so it is necessary to refer that to what is not doubted. And there is no power except with Allah. And that in referring to generality would realize for him contradiction due to the coming of reports of pardon (akhbar al-'afw), so specificity is thereby established. And there is no power except with Allah. Or if it admits both specificity and generality in light of what the named is divided into as I mentioned, then the right of such is fear (al-khawf), not certainty (al-qat'), so whoever decides with certainty departs from what is required by wisdom in the presence of ambiguity (al-shubha). And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage, introduced by 'The Shaykh said', classifies the names used in threat verses (like fisq, fujur, 'isyan, zulm) into three categories. The author does not list all three explicitly here but states that one type may be used in a non-blameworthy sense, and another type may not. Then he argues that since these names are divided into three types and the threat does not apply to all types, the threat must be specific (khhusus) to certain sins, not general. He uses a principle: if a term can refer to two distant meanings, it should be taken in the one that is certain. Then he provides a second argument: if the threat were general, it would contradict the reports of pardon (akhbar al-'afw), so it is established that the threat is specific. Finally, he discusses the case where both general and specific interpretations are possible: the proper response is fear (khawf), not certainty (qat'), and those who assert certainty depart from wisdom when there is ambiguity. The passage repeatedly ends with 'There is no power except with Allah.'

Key Terms
وعيد
تسمية
خصال
خصوص
عموم
أخبار العفو
شبه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Qur'anic threats apply to every sin without exception.
Correction: Maturidi shows that threat terms have multiple meanings and that pardon verses exist, so threats are specific and do not apply to all sins automatically.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi argue that threats in the Qur'an are specific, not general?
A) Because the names used are of one type.
B) Because applying them generally would contradict verses of pardon.
C) Because the names are not real.
D) Because the Prophet forbade general threats.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that threat verses are specific, not general, and when in doubt, the proper stance is fear, not certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses that warn of punishment (like 'whoever does such-and-such will be punished'), do you automatically apply them to yourself or to others? How does Maturidi's argument that these threats are specific challenge your default assumption?
?
Are there sins in your life that you have 'decided' (with certainty) will lead to punishment, either for yourself or for someone else? Where does that certainty come from, and how does this lesson call you to replace it with fear and humility?
?
If you truly believe that threats are specific and that only Allah knows the true application, how would that change the way you judge yourself and others? What specific attitude will you adopt today regarding ambiguous cases of sin and punishment?

Eternal Threat Only Applies to Shirk
Maturidi presents three-fold evidence that eternal threat is limited to shirk.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi proof that eternal punishment is reserved for shirk, using arguments from human nature, intellect, and revealed texts.

Core Idea
Human nature's aversion to leaving one's religion shows that only shirk warrants eternal threat.

Imam Maturidi presents a compelling argument that eternal punishment is reserved for the gravest sin: shirk. By examining human nature, rational principles, and the testimony of scripture, he shows that the threat of eternity cannot apply to lesser sins. This lesson unpacks that evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation once without analyzing. Focus on the overall argument.
Notice the three distinct proofs presented.
2
Identify the three proofs
Look for the evidence from natural disposition (jibillah), intellect (uquul), and revealed texts (sam').
Each proof supports the same conclusion.
3
Understand the natural disposition argument
Maturidi argues that people naturally avoid leaving their religion, even if they commit sins. This shows that the eternal threat (leaving religion) is only for shirk.
This is the primary evidence.
4
Understand the intellect and revelation arguments
Intellect holds beliefs as permanent, actions as changeable. Revealed texts show variation in actions. Both support that eternal threat cannot apply to actions (sins) but only to belief (shirk).
These reinforce the natural disposition argument.
5
See the refutation of Mu'tazilism
The passage concludes that the Mu'tazili position opposing this goes against natural disposition and divine governance.
Reflect on why this refutation is significant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الدلالة على وعيد الخلود [بأنه] لا يحتمله ما دون الشرك الأمر الذي جبل عليه الخلق من نفارهم عما به الخروج من أديانهم التي اعتقدوها، وإن كانوا اعتقدوها عقلاً أو حجة أو تقليدا، على وجود ما دون ذلك من الزلات فيهم؛ وإن اختلفت أديانهم. فدل أن ذا مما جبل عليه الخلق؛ بل أيد ذلك العقول، إذ الاعتقادات تكون عند أربابها أبديات، ولا كذلك الأفعال التي تشار إليها، وعلى ذلك أضدادها. وكذلك السمع في الأفعال المشارة أنها على الاختلاف، فعلى ذلك تركها. فدل ما ذكرنا على خروج مذهب الاعتزال عن الأمر المجبول عليه، والمدفوع إليه أيضاً بالتدبير. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then the evidence for the threat of eternity (wa'id al-khulud) [is that] it does not admit what is less than shirk, a matter upon which creation is naturally disposed (jubila 'alayhi) — their aversion to that by which they would exit from their religions which they have believed, even if they believed them by reason, proof, or tradition, despite the occurrence of what is less than that of slips among them, even if their religions differ. So that indicates that this is something upon which creation is naturally disposed; rather, intellects have supported that, since beliefs with their holders are eternal (abadiat), and actions that are pointed to are not like that, and likewise their opposites. And likewise the revealed texts (al-sam') regarding the actions indicated are that they are subject to variation, so their omission is upon that. So what we have mentioned indicates the departure of the Mu'tazili school from the matter upon which creation is naturally disposed, and which is also impelled to by divine governance (al-tadbir). And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage provides evidence that the eternal threat (wa'id al-khulud) only applies to shirk, not to sins below that level. The proof is based on human nature (jibillah): people have an innate aversion to leaving their religion, regardless of how they came to believe it (reason, proof, or tradition). Even though they commit sins below shirk, they do not abandon their religion. This shows that the natural reaction to the threat of leaving religion (i.e., shirk) is far stronger than for other sins. Moreover, intellect supports this: beliefs are held as permanent (abadiyat) by their holders, while actions are changeable. Likewise, revealed texts (sam') show that actions vary in their rulings. Therefore, the eternal threat cannot apply to lesser sins. The passage concludes that this refutes the Mu'tazili position, which deviates from both natural disposition and divine governance (tadbir). It ends with 'There is no power except with Allah.'

Key Terms
وعيد الخلود (eternal threat)
شرك (shirk)
جبلة (natural disposition)
اعتقادات (beliefs)
أبديات (eternals)
سمع (revealed texts)
تدبير (divine governance)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think any major sin warrants eternal punishment.
Correction: Maturidi shows only shirk does, based on nature, reason, and revelation.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for limiting eternal threat to shirk?
A) A specific Qur'anic verse.
B) Human nature's aversion to leaving one's religion.
C) The consensus of scholars.
D) The Mu'tazili position.

Key Takeaway
The eternal threat is exclusive to shirk, as proven by innate human aversion, rational permanence of beliefs, and revealed variability of actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have been convinced of your religion by reason, proof, or tradition. What would it take for you to leave it? Reflect on Maturidi's observation that humans are averse to leaving their religion. Is your own faith that deeply rooted, or is it merely a habit? What is one concrete step you need to take today to strengthen your conviction so that no sin less than shirk could ever cause you to abandon it?
?
Maturidi says beliefs are 'eternal' with their holders while actions are changeable. Look at your own life: do you treat your fundamental beliefs as permanent and non-negotiable, or do they shift with convenience? Which belief are you most tempted to compromise for the sake of a worldly action? Repent of that and reaffirm your commitment to the truth.
?
The Mu'tazili school held that major sins merit eternal punishment, contradicting human nature and divine governance. Is there any sin you secretly think might be 'beyond' Allah's mercy? Maturidi's argument shows that only shirk carries eternal threat. Do you truly believe that Allah's mercy encompasses all sins less than shirk? What sin do you need to turn to Allah for right now, trusting that He will forgive it and not abandon you to eternal fire?

The Objection Against Intercession for Major Sins
Maturidi reports an objection about intercession for major sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a logical objection raised against the permissibility of intercession for major sins.

Core Idea
Intercession could incentivize sin—a key objection to the doctrine.

In this brief passage, Imam Maturidi transmits an opponent's argument against intercession for major sins. The objection highlights a potential logical consequence that would undermine moral responsibility. Understanding this critique is essential for grasping the broader debate within Sunni theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic sentence once. Notice the conditional structure 'if... then...' and the term for major sin (كبيرة) and intercession (شفاعة).
2
Identify the logical structure
The objection uses a conditional: if intercession is permitted, then a person who swears to do something that merits intercession would be commanded to commit a major sin. Identify the premise and conclusion.
3
Analyze the implied criticism
Why is this problematic? The objector suggests that intercession could be used as a license to sin, which would be absurd. Reflect on the moral implication.
4
Prepare for Maturidi's response
Consider how a defender of intercession might answer this objection. This prepares you for the next lessons where Maturidi likely refutes it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الكبيرة والشفاعة: قال بعضهم: لو كانت الكبيرة مما يجوز الشفاعة له لكان من يحلف بفعل شيء يستوجب به الشفاعة يؤمر بارتكاب الكبيرة.
Then major sin and intercession: Some of them said: If major sin were something for which intercession is permissible, then one who swears to do something that merits intercession would be commanded to commit a major sin.
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This short passage presents an objection regarding the issue of major sins and intercession. Someone argues that if major sins could be forgiven through intercession, then a person who makes an oath to do something that would merit intercession would be ordered to commit a major sin. The implied criticism is that this would lead to encouraging sin, which is absurd. The passage is likely setting up a position that Maturidi will refute, but the text ends here.

Key Terms
كبيرة (kabīra)
شفاعة (shafāʿa)
يستوجب (yastawjib)
يحلف (yaḥlif)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the objection raised concerning intercession for major sins?
A) It would make God unjust
B) It could encourage sinning to earn intercession
C) It contradicts the Quran
D) It denies human free will

Key Takeaway
The objection argues that intercession for major sins would lead to absurdly commanding sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself relying on the hope of eventual forgiveness or intercession to justify delaying repentance for a sin? How does this objection expose the flaw in that thinking?
?
If you truly internalized that God's mercy is not a license to sin, what specific habit or assumption in your life would need to change starting today?
?
The objector argues that intercession could incentivize sin. Is there any area of your life where you have acted as if forgiveness is cheap? What would it look like to take God's commands seriously without losing hope in His mercy?

The Quranic and Traditional Basis for Intercession
Maturidi argues that intercession is established by Quran and hadith and is necessary for hope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that intercession is a core Islamic doctrine established by Quran and hadith, and that denying it undermines the hope and supplication of believers.

Core Idea
Intercession is a divine gift that preserves hope for sinners; denying it contradicts revelation and mercy.

Imam Maturidi begins by asserting that intercession is among the greatest proofs in Islam, supported by both the Quran and the Prophet's traditions. He explains that intercession conventionally applies to major sins that merit wrath, and that minor sins and disbelief are outside its scope. In this passage, Maturidi warns that rejecting intercession would invalidate the Quran's promises of grace and destroy the very hope that sustains the faith of Muslims.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the English translation to understand the flow of Maturidi's argument.
Notice the key term 'شفاعة' and how it is defined.
2
Identify the three parts of the argument
The passage moves from (1) the scriptural basis of intercession, (2) its conventional meaning applied to major sins, and (3) the disastrous consequences if it is denied.
3
Analyze the logical condition
Maturidi argues that if intercession is not accepted, then the Quranic bestowal of favor becomes invalid, and the natural hope of believers is destroyed. Ask yourself why he sees this as a necessary conclusion.
4
Reflect on the role of hope in faith
Consider how the concept of 'رجاء' (hope) is central to Islamic spirituality. Why does denying intercession undermine hope?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشفاعة من أعظم ما احتج بها، وقد جاء القرآن بها والآثار عن رسول الله. والشفاعة في المعهود والمتعالَم من الأمر تكون عند زلات يستوجب بها المقت والعقوبة، فيعفى عن مرتكبها بشفاعة الأخيار وأهل الرضا بهم.

And there is no power except with Allah.

He explains that intercession is conventionally understood to be for sins that deserve punishment. Then he points out that according to those who hold that major sins lead to eternal punishment, minor sins do not incur punishment, and disbelievers are not forgiven through intercession. If intercession is denied, the Quranic promise of grace is lost, along with the hope of believers and their supplication for the Prophet's intercession. Thus, denying intercession contradicts revelation and undermines hope.

Intercession Is Earned by Obedience, Not Sin
The jurist argues that intercession is earned by good deeds, not by sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that intercession in Islam is earned through good deeds and obedience, not through sin, and how this refutes a common misunderstanding.

Core Idea
Intercession is earned by obedience, not by transgression.

In this passage, a jurist directly tackles a widespread misunderstanding: that the promise of intercession gives license to sin. He clarifies the precise relationship between deeds and intercession, showing that intercession is a reward for obedience, not a free pass for disobedience. Let's examine his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the jurist's refutation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Notice how he begins by calling the misunderstanding a 'delusion'.
Pay attention to the strong opening: 'ذلك وهم' (that is a delusion).
2
Analyze the two analogies
The jurist gives two examples: the person who swears by God, and the one who seeks forgiveness. How does each example show that obedience, not sin, earns intercession?
In both cases, the correct response is to command obedience, not transgression.
3
Understand the broader implications
The jurist then connects intercession to the Qur'an and Sunnah, and discusses minor vs major sins. Reflect on why he emphasizes that minor sins are not punishable and that disbelievers are not pardoned through intercession.
If intercession were for sin itself, the entire system of reward and punishment would collapse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه رحمه الله : فنقول ذلك وهم، لأنه ليس الذي له يُشفع هو الذي به يستوجب الشفاعة، بل يستوجب بالحسنات التي بها يجب الولاية فيما ترك . فحق من حلف بذلك ليس أن يقال له : «اعص» ، ولكن يقال له : «أطع لتستوجب به الشفاعة فيما عصيت». وكذلك من يحلف : «الأفعلن الفعل الذي أستوجب به المغفرة ، لا يقال له ارتكب الصغائر»، بل يؤمر باتقاء الكبائر والتوبة عنها ليُغفر له، فمثله أمر الشفاعة. والشفاعة من أعظم ما احتج بها ، و قد جاء القرآن بها والآثار عن رسول الله . والشفاعة في المعهود والمتعالَم من الأمر تكون عند زلات يستوجب بها المقت والعقوبة، فيعفى عن مرتكبها بشفاعة الأخيار وأهل الرضا بهم . ثم كانت الصغائر مما لا يجوز التعذيب عليها عند القائلين بالخلود في الكبائر، والكفار مما لا يعفى عنهم بالشفاعة؛ فإذا بطل عظيم ما جاء به القرآن والآثار في الامتنان، وسقط ما جبل عليه أهل العلم من الرجاء بالله وبرحمته ويبطل دعاء المسلمين بشفاعة الرسول . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The jurist, may God have mercy on him, said: We say: that is a delusion, because the one for whom intercession is made is not the one by whom intercession is earned; rather, it is earned by the good deeds through which nearness is obligatory in what he has neglected. So the right of one who swears by that is not to be told 'disobey', but rather to be told 'obey so that you may earn intercession thereby for what you have disobeyed'. Likewise, one who swears 'I will surely do the deed by which I earn forgiveness' is not told 'commit minor sins', but rather he is commanded to avoid major sins and repent from them so that he may be forgiven; such is the matter of intercession. And intercession is among the greatest proofs brought, and the Qur'an and the traditions from the Messenger of God came with it. And intercession, in what is customary and known, occurs at slips that deserve wrath and punishment, so the committer is pardoned through the intercession of the righteous and those who are pleased with them. Then minor sins are such that punishment is not permissible for them according to those who hold to eternal punishment for major sins, and the disbelievers are not pardoned through intercession. So if the great thing that the Qur'an and the traditions brought in bestowal of favor is invalidated, and what the people of knowledge are naturally disposed to of hope in God and His mercy falls away, and the Muslims' supplication for the intercession of the Messenger is invalidated. There is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage is a refutation by a jurist of a misunderstanding about intercession. He begins by asserting that the claim under discussion is a delusion. The key distinction is between the person for whom intercession is made and the means by which intercession is earned: intercession is not earned by sin but by good deeds that bring about divine nearness. Therefore, one who swears by God should be told to obey, not to disobey, because obedience earns intercession for past disobedience. Similarly, one who seeks forgiveness should be told to avoid major sins and repent, not to commit minor sins. The jurist then emphasizes the central role of intercession in the Qur'an and Sunnah. He explains that intercession customarily occurs when someone commits a slip deserving punishment, and the righteous intercede for pardon. He notes that minor sins do not warrant punishment according to those who believe in eternal punishment for major sins, and disbelievers are not pardoned through intercession. If this understanding is lost, the great favor of intercession mentioned in revelation is nullified, as is the hope in God's mercy and the Muslims' prayers for the Prophet's intercession. The passage ends with a formula of reliance on God.

Correction: Intercession is earned by good deeds; it is a reward for obedience, not a license to sin.

Two Types of Intercession: Merit and Supplication
Classification of intercession into two types: merit mention and supplication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of intercession according to some scholars: intercession through mentioning merits and intercession through supplication, and how the Quran supports both.

Core Idea
Intercession is not just asking—it also involves elevating someone's status by mentioning their virtues.

In this passage, some early scholars offer a nuanced understanding of intercession (shafa'ah). They divide it into two categories: one that involves praising a person before another to raise their rank, and another that is simply a supplication. This classification helps us see that intercession has both a diplomatic and a prayerful dimension.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage once
Read the Arabic passage from the source carefully. Even if you don't understand every word, let the flow of the argument sink in.
Focus on the two types mentioned at the beginning.
2
Identify the two types of intercession
Look for the two ways intercession is said to emerge. Note how each is defined and what distinguishes them.
3
Connect to Quranic evidence
See how the author quotes Quran verses to support the second type and argues that the wording also covers the first type. Reflect on how a single verse can indicate both aspects.
The verse 'they intercede only for those with whom He is pleased' implies status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال بعضهم : الشفاعة تخرج على وجهين : على ذكر محاسن أحد عند آخر ليقدر له عنده المنزلة والرتبة، والثاني أن يدعو له . فالأول هو الذي يحتمل توجيه الشفاعة إليه، والثاني قد بين فيمن [هو] بقوله " : الذين يحملون العرش إلى قوله : وذلك هو الفوز العظيم وقوله : ولا يشفعون إلا لمن ارتضى ﴾ [الأنبياء، ۲۸/۲۱]؛ والحرف يدل على وجهى لأن المرتضى هو ذو منزلة وقدر، [و] هو ممن تضمنته آية شفاعة الملائكة.
And some of them said: Intercession emerges in two ways: one is mentioning the good qualities of someone to another so that he may assign to him status and rank; the second is that he supplicates for him. The first is what admits directing intercession to him, and the second has been explained in His statement: 'Those who bear the Throne' up to His saying: 'and that is the great attainment' and His saying: 'and they intercede only for those with whom He is pleased' (Qur'an 21:28); and the letter indicates both aspects because the one with whom He is pleased is one of status and rank, and he is among those included in the verse of the angels' intercession.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of intercession into two types offered by some unnamed scholars. The first type involves mentioning someone's virtues before another person to elevate his standing. The second type is supplication (du'a) on behalf of someone. The first type is the one that can be directed to someone, while the second is explained by Quranic verses: the angels' intercession as depicted in Surah Ghafir (40:7-9) and the verse from Surah Al-Anbiya (21:28) stating they intercede only for those with whom God is pleased. The author notes that the wording of the verse indicates both aspects because the 'pleased' person is one of rank and status, and such a person is included in the angels' intercession. This clarifies that intercession is not merely a request but also a recognition of merit.

Abu Bakr al-Asamm's Argument on Forgiveness
Al-Asamm presents his interpretation of Quran 4:48 and 4:31 to limit forgiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu Bakr al-Asamm interpreted Quran 4:48 and 4:31 to limit forgiveness to minor sins only.

Core Idea
Al-Asamm argued that forgiveness is promised only for minor sins, while major sins are threatened.

Abu Bakr al-Asamm, a well-known theologian, proposed an interpretation of two key Qur'anic verses that would restrict divine forgiveness to minor sins. His argument relies on a distinction between promise (wa'd) and threat (wa'īd). In this lesson, we examine his reasoning and the scriptural basis he used.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Asamm's statement
Read the passage where al-Asamm presents his interpretation. Notice how he uses Quran 4:48 and 4:31 to build his case.
Pay attention to the terms 'wa'd' (promise) and 'wa'īd' (threat).
2
Identify his two scriptural proofs
Al-Asamm cites Quran 4:48 for general forgiveness and Quran 4:31 for minor sins. Understand how he links them.
He argues that the promise of forgiveness is clarified in the second verse as applying to minor sins.
3
Understand his conclusion
Al-Asamm concludes that only minor sins are eligible for forgiveness, while major sins are threatened. This is his main claim.
He treats the threat for major sins as established, so the promise must be for minor sins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أبو بكر الأصم في قوله تعالى: إن الله لا يغفر أن يشرك به ويغفر ما دون ذلك لمن يشاء [النساء: 48]: إن الله وعد المغفرة لمن شاء، ثم بيّن ذلك في الصغائر بقوله: وإن تجتنبوا كبائر ما تنهون عنه نكفّر عنكم سيئاتكم [النساء: 31]، وقد ثبت الوعيد في الكبائر، بقى الوعد في الصغيرة، فحقه لم يزل بالذي ذكر لاحتماله ما وصفت. قال الفقيه رحمه الله: فنقول له بأوجه أحدها أن الوعيد الذي ذكرته يحتمل الاستحلال والاستخفاف بالأمر والنهي، فلا يترك ما أطمع بهذه الآية من المغفرة، فيزول الطمع والرجاء بالوعيد المتوجه إلى وجهين؛ أو يوقف فيهما، فأما القطع في أحد الوجهين بالمحتمل ومنع القطع بالآخر للاحتمال فهو تحكم. ولا قوة إلا بالله. والثاني أن الآية في التفصيل بين المحتمل للغفران والذي لا يحتمل؛ فإذا صرفت إلى الصغائر بطل تخصيص اسم الشرك، وتلبس على السامع محله. وليس أمر الوعيد فيها جاء قوله تعالى بموضع التفصيل، بل الذي جاء بحق التفصيل ذكر الغفران بالتكفير، والتكفير يكون بمقابلة الجزاء من حسنات أو عقوبات كقوله تعالى: وإن تجتنبوا كبائر ما تنهون عنه الآية. والله الموفق. والثالث أنه قال: لمن يشاء، وهذا كناية عن الأنفس المغفورات لا عن الآثام التي تغفر، لم يجز صرف التخصيص إلى الآثام بالآية المكنى بها عن الأنفس. وفي آيات الوعيد تحقيق الذين جاءت فيهم وفيما جاء على ما قيل فيما قبل لا صرف في ذلك، فهو أولى. والله الموفق. وبعد، فإنه قال: لمن يشاء، والصغائر عندكم مغفورة بالحكمة لا بالوعد، والآية في التعريف. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And Abu Bakr al-Asamm said concerning His statement the Exalted: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him, but He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' [al-Nisa 4:48]: 'Indeed, Allah promised forgiveness for whom He wills, then He clarified that regarding minor sins by His statement: 'If you avoid the major sins which you are forbidden, We will expiate from you your misdeeds' [al-Nisa 4:31], and the threat (waʿīd) for major sins has been established, the promise (al-waʿd) for minor sin remains, so its due has not ceased by what he mentioned due to its possibility of what I described.' The faqīh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'So we say to him with aspects, one of which is that the threat which you mentioned may [theoretically] imply deeming permissible and belittling the command and prohibition, so one does not abandon what he is made to covet by this verse of forgiveness, and thus covetousness and hope would be removed by the threat directed to two faces; or one stops regarding both. As for decisively affirming one of two faces on a possibility and preventing decisiveness regarding the other due to possibility, this is arbitrary. There is no power except with Allah. The second: that the verse is about differentiation between what is possible for forgiveness and what is not; if it is diverted to minor sins, the specification of the name of shirk would be invalidated, and its place would be confusing to the listener. The matter of the threat in it is not that the statement of the Exalted came in the place of differentiation; rather, what came with the right of differentiation is the mention of forgiveness by expiation (takfīr), and expiation occurs by reciprocating recompense from good deeds or punishments, like His statement the Exalted: 'If you avoid the major sins which you are forbidden' etc. Allah is the Grantor of success. The third: that He said: 'for whom He wills', and this is a metonymy for the forgiven souls not for the sins that are forgiven; it is not permissible to divert the specification to the sins through the verse which is metonymically about souls. And in the verses of threat, the particularize of those whom they come regarding and what is said before - there is no diversion in that, so it is more appropriate. Allah is the Grantor of success. Then, he said: 'for whom He wills', and minor sins according to you are forgiven by wisdom (ḥikma) not by promise, and the verse is about definition. There is no power except with Allah.'
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage contains an objection from Abu Bakr al-Asamm and a threefold refutation by the faqīh (Imam Maturidi). Al-Asamm interprets Quran 4:48 as promising forgiveness for minor sins only, since major sins are already threatened. He argues that the promise of forgiveness for minor sins remains, and the threat for major sins is established. The faqīh responds with three aspects. First, the threat al-Asamm mentions could be interpreted as referring to those who treat sins lightly, so it does not cancel out the hope from the forgiveness verse; to decisively assign one meaning while rejecting another based on mere possibility is arbitrary. Second, the verse differentiates between what can be forgiven and what cannot; diverting it to minor sins would remove the specification of shirk, causing confusion. The threat in the verse is not about differentiation but about expiation, which occurs through good deeds or punishments as in Quran 4:31. Third, the phrase 'for whom He wills' is a metonymy for the persons forgiven, not the sins; thus it cannot be limited to minor sins. Finally, the faqīh adds a postscript: al-Asamm claims minor sins are forgiven by wisdom (i.e., rationally) not by promise, but the verse is about defining a category, not about rational judgment.

Key Terms

وعد
صغائر
كبائر

Correction: Al-Asamm argues that the verse is limited by Quran 4:31, restricting forgiveness to minor sins only.

Checkpoint

B) Minor sins only
C) Major sins only
D) No sins at all

Key Takeaway
Al-Asamm's interpretation restricts divine forgiveness to minor sins, using a distinction between promise and threat.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Al-Asamm's interpretation might limit God's mercy. Are you comfortable with a God whose forgiveness is conditional? How does this align with your experience of seeking forgiveness in your own life?

First and Second Refutations of al-Asamm
First two aspects of the refutation: threat ambiguity and verse purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two arguments against al-Asamm's interpretation, focusing on the ambiguity of threat and the true purpose of the verse.

Core Idea
The threat in the verse can be interpreted as referring to those who treat sins lightly, not a cancellation of hope.

The faqīh (Imam Maturidi) responds to al-Asamm with three aspects. The first two challenge al-Asamm's use of the threat verse and his division of sins. He argues that the threat could imply belittling, and that the verse's purpose is differentiation through expiation, not a simple sorting.

Guided Path
1
Read the first response
Read the first aspect of the refutation. Notice how it challenges al-Asamm's interpretation of the threat.
The threat might apply to those who deem sins permissible or belittle them, not to all major sins.
2
Understand the first argument about threat
The refutation argues that the threat al-Asamm mentions is ambiguous—it could refer to a specific attitude, not a general category. Thus, it does not cancel the hope from the forgiveness verse.
This undermines al-Asamm's claim that the promise is only for minor sins.
3
Read the second response
Read the second aspect. It focuses on the purpose of the verse.
The verse is about differentiation (tafṣīl) between what can be forgiven and what cannot.
4
Understand the second argument about differentiation
The second refutation states that diverting the verse to minor sins invalidates the specification of shirk. The true differentiation is through expiation (takfīr), which occurs via good deeds or punishments, as in Quran 4:31.
Thus, the verse is not simply sorting sins but describing a process of expiation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أبو بكر الأصم في قوله تعالى: إن الله لا يغفر أن يشرك به ويغفر ما دون ذلك لمن يشاء [النساء: 48]: إن الله وعد المغفرة لمن شاء، ثم بيّن ذلك في الصغائر بقوله: وإن تجتنبوا كبائر ما تنهون عنه نكفّر عنكم سيئاتكم [النساء: 31]، وقد ثبت الوعيد في الكبائر، بقى الوعد في الصغيرة، فحقه لم يزل بالذي ذكر لاحتماله ما وصفت. قال الفقيه رحمه الله: فنقول له بأوجه أحدها أن الوعيد الذي ذكرته يحتمل الاستحلال والاستخفاف بالأمر والنهي، فلا يترك ما أطمع بهذه الآية من المغفرة، فيزول الطمع والرجاء بالوعيد المتوجه إلى وجهين؛ أو يوقف فيهما، فأما القطع في أحد الوجهين بالمحتمل ومنع القطع بالآخر للاحتمال فهو تحكم. ولا قوة إلا بالله. والثاني أن الآية في التفصيل بين المحتمل للغفران والذي لا يحتمل؛ فإذا صرفت إلى الصغائر بطل تخصيص اسم الشرك، وتلبس على السامع محله. وليس أمر الوعيد فيها جاء قوله تعالى بموضع التفصيل، بل الذي جاء بحق التفصيل ذكر الغفران بالتكفير، والتكفير يكون بمقابلة الجزاء من حسنات أو عقوبات كقوله تعالى: وإن تجتنبوا كبائر ما تنهون عنه الآية. والله الموفق. والثالث أنه قال: لمن يشاء، وهذا كناية عن الأنفس المغفورات لا عن الآثام التي تغفر، لم يجز صرف التخصيص إلى الآثام بالآية المكنى بها عن الأنفس. وفي آيات الوعيد تحقيق الذين جاءت فيهم وفيما جاء على ما قيل فيما قبل لا صرف في ذلك، فهو أولى. والله الموفق. وبعد، فإنه قال: لمن يشاء، والصغائر عندكم مغفورة بالحكمة لا بالوعد، والآية في التعريف. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And Abu Bakr al-Asamm said concerning His statement the Exalted: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him, but He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' [al-Nisa 4:48]: 'Indeed, Allah promised forgiveness for whom He wills, then He clarified that regarding minor sins by His statement: 'If you avoid the major sins which you are forbidden, We will expiate from you your misdeeds' [al-Nisa 4:31], and the threat (waʿīd) for major sins has been established, the promise (al-waʿd) for minor sin remains, so its due has not ceased by what he mentioned due to its possibility of what I described.' The faqīh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'So we say to him with aspects, one of which is that the threat which you mentioned may [theoretically] imply deeming permissible and belittling the command and prohibition, so one does not abandon what he is made to covet by this verse of forgiveness, and thus covetousness and hope would be removed by the threat directed to two faces; or one stops regarding both. As for decisively affirming one of two faces on a possibility and preventing decisiveness regarding the other due to possibility, this is arbitrary. There is no power except with Allah. The second: that the verse is about differentiation between what is possible for forgiveness and what is not; if it is diverted to minor sins, the specification of the name of shirk would be invalidated, and its place would be confusing to the listener. The matter of the threat in it is not that the statement of the Exalted came in the place of differentiation; rather, what came with the right of differentiation is the mention of forgiveness by expiation (takfīr), and expiation occurs by reciprocating recompense from good deeds or punishments, like His statement the Exalted: 'If you avoid the major sins which you are forbidden' etc. Allah is the Grantor of success. The third: that He said: 'for whom He wills', and this is a metonymy for the forgiven souls not for the sins that are forgiven; it is not permissible to divert the specification to the sins through the verse which is metonymically about souls. And in the verses of threat, the particularize of those whom they come regarding and what is said before - there is no diversion in that, so it is more appropriate. Allah is the Grantor of success. Then, he said: 'for whom He wills', and minor sins according to you are forgiven by wisdom (ḥikma) not by promise, and the verse is about definition. There is no power except with Allah.'
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage contains an objection from Abu Bakr al-Asamm and a threefold refutation by the faqīh (Imam Maturidi). Al-Asamm interprets Quran 4:48 as promising forgiveness for minor sins only, since major sins are already threatened. He argues that the promise of forgiveness for minor sins remains, and the threat for major sins is established. The faqīh responds with three aspects. First, the threat al-Asamm mentions could be interpreted as referring to those who treat sins lightly, so it does not cancel out the hope from the forgiveness verse; to decisively assign one meaning while rejecting another based on mere possibility is arbitrary. Second, the verse differentiates between what can be forgiven and what cannot; diverting it to minor sins would remove the specification of shirk, causing confusion. The threat in the verse is not about differentiation but about expiation, which occurs through good deeds or punishments as in Quran 4:31. Third, the phrase 'for whom He wills' is a metonymy for the persons forgiven, not the sins; thus it cannot be limited to minor sins. Finally, the faqīh adds a postscript: al-Asamm claims minor sins are forgiven by wisdom (i.e., rationally) not by promise, but the verse is about defining a category, not about rational judgment.

Key Terms
استحلال
استخفاف
تكفير
تفصيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second refutation, what is the true purpose of Quran 4:48?
A) To threaten major sins
B) To differentiate between forgivable and unforgivable sins through expiation
C) To limit forgiveness to minor sins
D) To cancel the promise of forgiveness

Key Takeaway
The first two refutations show that al-Asamm's interpretation is based on arbitrary handling of ambiguous texts and misses the true purpose of the verse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a verse of threat like 4:48, does your heart immediately despair of Allah's mercy, or do you pause to consider the broader context and possibility of forgiveness? How can you train yourself to balance fear and hope in your daily life?
?
Have you ever used a threatening verse to justify a harsh view of others, assuming that those who commit certain sins are beyond forgiveness, ignoring the lesson that the threat may apply only to those who belittle sins (istikhfaf)? What does that reveal about the condition of your own heart?
?
The refutation teaches that true differentiation lies in expiation (takfir) through good deeds and avoiding major sins. Which of your own sins do you secretly believe are too great for Allah to forgive? What concrete step will you take today to return to hope in His mercy and actively seek expiation through good deeds?

Third Refutation and Final Remark
Third refutation and concluding remark.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third refutation about the phrase 'for whom He wills' and the concluding point about forgiveness by wisdom.

Core Idea
The phrase 'for whom He wills' refers to persons, not sins, so it cannot be restricted to minor sins.

The third aspect of the refutation focuses on the Qur'anic phrase 'for whom He wills,' arguing that it is a metonymy for the forgiven souls, not the sins. The faqīh also adds a final remark that al-Asamm's own view on minor sins being forgiven by wisdom undermines his interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the third refutation
Read the third aspect. Notice how it interprets the phrase 'for whom He wills' (لِمَن يَشَاء).
The refutation claims this is a metonymy (kināya) for the persons forgiven, not the sins.
2
Understand the metonymy argument
If 'for whom He wills' refers to persons, then the verse cannot be used to specify types of sins. It is about specific individuals whom Allah forgives.
This directly undermines al-Asamm's attempt to limit forgiveness to minor sins.
3
Read the final remark about wisdom and definition
The faqīh adds that al-Asamm himself holds that minor sins are forgiven by wisdom (ḥikma) not by promise, yet the verse is about definition (taʿrīf) – it defines a category, not a rational judgment.
This highlights an inconsistency in al-Asamm's position.
