The Reality of Divine Threats and the Division of the Afterlife
Maturidi gives examples of threats and states the afterlife division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Quranic threats establish a real division between believers and disbelievers in the afterlife.

Core Idea
Divine threats are real; they separate people into paradise and hell.

Imam al-Maturidi begins by grounding the reality of divine threats in specific Quranic verses about usury, orphans' wealth, and unlawful killing. These examples show that threats are not empty warnings but carry actual consequences, culminating in the ultimate division of humanity into two groups in the afterlife: the people of paradise and the people of the blaze.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Maturidi moves from specific examples to the general division.
2
Identify the three examples of threats
The passage mentions three types of people who were threatened: those who return to usury, those who consume orphans' wealth unjustly, and those who kill unlawfully. Observe that each is linked to a specific Quranic verse.
These examples illustrate that threats are tied to serious sins.
3
Understand the afterlife division
Maturidi then states that the classification in the afterlife follows from these realities: a group in paradise, a group in the blaze. Notice the mention of the book given in the right or left hand and the distinction between believer and disbeliever.
This division is the foundation of the threat's fulfillment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ومن عاد الآية" ، وقوله : (وأخذهم الربا " الآية، إنهم أحلوا حيث قالوا: إنما البيع مثل الربا [البقرة، ٢٧٥/٢]. وكذلك أموال اليتامى لم يكونوا يعطون الذين لم يبلغوا القتال ولا ضربوا [هم] بالسهام في المغانم . وأمر القتل كذلك ، كانوا يقتلون بغيا واستحلالاً على ما ذكر من قوله : واذكروا نعمة الله عليكم إذ كنتم أعداء " . فهذا الآن طريق حقائق الوعيد ، وما فيه [من] إبطال تسمية الإيمان. وعلى ذلك القسمة في الآخرة: فريق في الجنة وفريق في السعير ﴾ [الشورى، ٧/٤٢] ، والمعطى كتابه بيمينه وشماله ، والمؤمن والكافر، وقوله تعالى : واتقوا النار التي أعدت للكافرین ﴾ [آل عمران، ۱۳۱/۳]. ففيهم تحقق الوعيد ولزمت الأسماء التي من نهايات في القبح إياهم. وأما من لم يبلغ ذلك الحد فإن الذي جاء فيهم من الوعيد يخرج على وجوه على تحذير اختيار تلك الأحوال التي ذكرت، أو على أن ذلك جزاؤه لو لم يكن معه غير ذلك من المحاسن، أو على أن الله في حكمته فيهم ـ على ما استحقوا " ـ وجه عفو أو تشفيع " الأخيار فيهم، أو على"" تكفير بغير ذلك من الحسنات أو وجه من العذاب على قدر ذنبه من [ غير ] ذنب الشرك؛ وله من الثواب فيما جاء به على ما أكرم به وأنعم عليه في الدنيا من التوفيق لطاعة ربه، والختم " على ذلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
His statement: 'And whoever returns [to usury]' verse, and His statement: 'And their taking usury' verse, that they permitted [it] when they said: 'Indeed selling is like usury' [Al-Baqarah 2:275]. And likewise the wealth of orphans: they would not give to those who had not reached fighting and had not struck with arrows in the spoils. And the matter of killing likewise: they used to kill out of transgression and considering it lawful, as mentioned in His statement: 'And remember the favor of Allah upon you when you were enemies' [Al-Imran 3:103]. So this now is the path to the realities of the threat, and what is in it of nullifying the naming of faith. And upon that is the division in the afterlife: a group in paradise and a group in the blaze [Ash-Shura 42:7], and the one given his book in his right hand and his left, and the believer and the disbeliever, and the statement of the Most High: 'And fear the fire which is prepared for the disbelievers' [Al-Imran 3:131]. So among them the threat is realized and the names which are the ultimate in ugliness apply to them. As for those who did not reach that limit, the threat that has come concerning them issues in ways: as a warning against choosing those states mentioned, or as that being his recompense if he had no other good deeds with him, or that Allah in His wisdom regarding them — according to what they deserved — [grants] a face of pardon or intercession of the righteous among them, or expiation by other good deeds, or a type of punishment according to his sin short of the sin of association; and he has from the reward for what he brought regarding what He honored him and bestowed upon him in this world of success in obeying his Lord, and the seal upon that. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage is an exposition on the realities of divine threats (وعيد) and how they apply to different categories of people. The author begins by citing examples from the Quran of those who were threatened due to their wrongful actions: those who returned to usury, those who consumed orphans' wealth, and those who killed unlawfully. These examples illustrate the path to understanding the realities of threats. The author then states that this path nullifies the mere naming of faith (إيمان) for such people. He then turns to the division in the afterlife: two groups—one in paradise and one in the blaze. The giving of books in right and left hands distinguishes believers from disbelievers. The threat is realized for disbelievers, and the worst names apply to them. Then the author addresses those who did not reach that extreme limit (i.e., who are not outright disbelievers or who have some faith). For them, the threat can be understood in multiple ways: (1) as a warning to deter them from choosing sinful states, (2) as a conditional recompense assuming no other good deeds, (3) as a context where Allah may grant pardon or intercession, (4) as expiation through other good deeds, or (5) as a lesser punishment for sins other than shirk. Moreover, such a person still has reward for the good deeds they performed in this world, including the success of obedience and the sealing of their life with faith. The passage concludes with the formula 'There is no power except with Allah.' This nuanced view reflects the Maturidi position on the application of threats, balancing divine justice and mercy.

Key Terms
وعيد
إيمان
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Quranic threats are symbolic or that everyone will eventually be saved regardless of actions.
Correction: Maturidi affirms that threats are real and result in an actual division between paradise and hell, based on one's deeds and faith.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the result of the threats mentioned?
A) Everyone will eventually be forgiven.
B) A division into two groups in the afterlife.
C) Only disbelievers are threatened.
D) Threats apply only to the examples given.

Key Takeaway
Divine threats are real and lead to a definitive separation between the people of paradise and the people of hellfire.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The examples given—usury, consuming orphans' wealth, unlawful killing—are severe. Is there a sin in your own life that you have minimized or justified? How does this passage's emphasis on the reality of divine threat challenge that? What will you do today to turn from it?
?
The passage says divine threats nullify the naming of faith. Do you rely on merely calling yourself a believer while persisting in known sins? What concrete step will you take this week to ensure your actions match your claim?
?
The afterlife is divided into only two groups: paradise or blaze. Based on your current state of belief and action, which group are you heading towards? If you are not certain, what is the first change you will make starting tonight?

The Nuanced Application of Threats to Believers Who Sin
Maturidi details five ways threats apply to believers who sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how threats apply to believers who commit sins short of disbelief, through possible mitigations like pardon, intercession, and expiation.

Core Idea
Threats for believers are conditional and can be mitigated by mercy and good deeds.

Imam al-Maturidi now addresses those who have faith but commit sins that fall short of outright disbelief. For such people, the severe threat mentioned in the Quran can be understood in multiple ways that reflect both divine justice and mercy. He lists several possibilities: the threat may serve as a deterrent, it may presuppose no other good deeds, or it may be mitigated through pardon, intercession, expiation, or a lesser punishment. This nuanced view balances the reality of threats with the hope of forgiveness for believers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it begins by contrasting 'those who did not reach that limit' with the disbelievers previously mentioned.
2
List the five possible applications of threat
The passage enumerates five ways the threat can be applied to believers who sin: (1) as a warning, (2) conditional recompense if no good deeds, (3) pardon or intercession, (4) expiation by other good deeds, (5) a lesser punishment short of shirk. Write them down.
These are not mutually exclusive; they show the range of divine wisdom.
3
Reflect on the balance of justice and mercy
Consider how this teaching preserves the seriousness of threats while leaving room for Allah's mercy and the efficacy of good deeds. Note that even with punishment, believers still have reward for their good deeds.
This is a key Maturidi position: threats are real but not always literal for believers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ومن عاد الآية" ، وقوله : (وأخذهم الربا " الآية، إنهم أحلوا حيث قالوا: إنما البيع مثل الربا [البقرة، ٢٧٥/٢]. وكذلك أموال اليتامى لم يكونوا يعطون الذين لم يبلغوا القتال ولا ضربوا [هم] بالسهام في المغانم . وأمر القتل كذلك ، كانوا يقتلون بغيا واستحلالاً على ما ذكر من قوله : واذكروا نعمة الله عليكم إذ كنتم أعداء " . فهذا الآن طريق حقائق الوعيد ، وما فيه [من] إبطال تسمية الإيمان. وعلى ذلك القسمة في الآخرة: فريق في الجنة وفريق في السعير ﴾ [الشورى، ٧/٤٢] ، والمعطى كتابه بيمينه وشماله ، والمؤمن والكافر، وقوله تعالى : واتقوا النار التي أعدت للكافرین ﴾ [آل عمران، ۱۳۱/۳]. ففيهم تحقق الوعيد ولزمت الأسماء التي من نهايات في القبح إياهم. وأما من لم يبلغ ذلك الحد فإن الذي جاء فيهم من الوعيد يخرج على وجوه على تحذير اختيار تلك الأحوال التي ذكرت، أو على أن ذلك جزاؤه لو لم يكن معه غير ذلك من المحاسن، أو على أن الله في حكمته فيهم ـ على ما استحقوا " ـ وجه عفو أو تشفيع " الأخيار فيهم، أو على"" تكفير بغير ذلك من الحسنات أو وجه من العذاب على قدر ذنبه من [ غير ] ذنب الشرك؛ وله من الثواب فيما جاء به على ما أكرم به وأنعم عليه في الدنيا من التوفيق لطاعة ربه، والختم " على ذلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
His statement: 'And whoever returns [to usury]' verse, and His statement: 'And their taking usury' verse, that they permitted [it] when they said: 'Indeed selling is like usury' [Al-Baqarah 2:275]. And likewise the wealth of orphans: they would not give to those who had not reached fighting and had not struck with arrows in the spoils. And the matter of killing likewise: they used to kill out of transgression and considering it lawful, as mentioned in His statement: 'And remember the favor of Allah upon you when you were enemies' [Al-Imran 3:103]. So this now is the path to the realities of the threat, and what is in it of nullifying the naming of faith. And upon that is the division in the afterlife: a group in paradise and a group in the blaze [Ash-Shura 42:7], and the one given his book in his right hand and his left, and the believer and the disbeliever, and the statement of the Most High: 'And fear the fire which is prepared for the disbelievers' [Al-Imran 3:131]. So among them the threat is realized and the names which are the ultimate in ugliness apply to them. As for those who did not reach that limit, the threat that has come concerning them issues in ways: as a warning against choosing those states mentioned, or as that being his recompense if he had no other good deeds with him, or that Allah in His wisdom regarding them — according to what they deserved — [grants] a face of pardon or intercession of the righteous among them, or expiation by other good deeds, or a type of punishment according to his sin short of the sin of association; and he has from the reward for what he brought regarding what He honored him and bestowed upon him in this world of success in obeying his Lord, and the seal upon that. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage is an exposition on the realities of divine threats (وعيد) and how they apply to different categories of people. The author begins by citing examples from the Quran of those who were threatened due to their wrongful actions: those who returned to usury, those who consumed orphans' wealth, and those who killed unlawfully. These examples illustrate the path to understanding the realities of threats. The author then states that this path nullifies the mere naming of faith (إيمان) for such people. He then turns to the division in the afterlife: two groups—one in paradise and one in the blaze. The giving of books in right and left hands distinguishes believers from disbelievers. The threat is realized for disbelievers, and the worst names apply to them. Then the author addresses those who did not reach that extreme limit (i.e., who are not outright disbelievers or who have some faith). For them, the threat can be understood in multiple ways: (1) as a warning to deter them from choosing sinful states, (2) as a conditional recompense assuming no other good deeds, (3) as a context where Allah may grant pardon or intercession, (4) as expiation through other good deeds, or (5) as a lesser punishment for sins other than shirk. Moreover, such a person still has reward for the good deeds they performed in this world, including the success of obedience and the sealing of their life with faith. The passage concludes with the formula 'There is no power except with Allah.' This nuanced view reflects the Maturidi position on the application of threats, balancing divine justice and mercy.

شفاعة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that any sin by a believer will lead to eternal punishment or that threats are always literal.
Correction: This passage teaches that threats for believers are conditional and can be mitigated through pardon, intercession, expiation, or lesser punishment, preserving the possibility of ultimate salvation.

Checkpoint

A) They are punished like disbelievers.
B) They are forgiven without condition.
C) They may be pardoned, interceded for, or punished lightly.
D) They have no reward.

Key Takeaway
For believers, threats are not absolute; they can be interpreted as warnings, conditional, or mitigated by mercy and good deeds.

Maturidi's Proof and Definition of Intercession
Maturidi affirms intercession based on Quran and hadith, for believers who deserve punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's scriptural basis for intercession, its scope (for believers who sinned), and its exclusion of disbelievers.

Core Idea
Intercession is a reality, but only for sinful believers, not for disbelievers.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi provides a clear statement on the doctrine of intercession (shafāʻah). He grounds it in the Quran and prophetic traditions, then defines its proper domain: it applies to believers who have committed lapses deserving punishment, and it is carried out by the righteous and those whom God is pleased with. Crucially, he excludes disbelievers from any benefit of intercession. This lesson unpacks that succinct but authoritative definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read Maturidi's words carefully, noting his reliance on Quran and hadith as proof.
The passage begins by asserting intercession as a major proof.
2
Identify the three key elements
Notice the scriptural basis, the definition of intercession (for lapses), and the exclusion of disbelievers.
These three parts form the core of the argument.
3
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider why Maturidi emphasizes that intercession is for believers who sinned, and not for disbelievers.
This distinguishes the Sunni position from other groups.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشفاعة من أعظم ما احتج بها ، وقد جاء القرآن بها ، والآثار ، وقد جاء القرآن بها ، والآثار عن رسول الله ، والشفاعة في المعهود والمتعالم من الأمر تكون عند زلات يستوجب بها المقت والعقوبة ، فيعفى عن مرتكبها بشفاعة الأخيار وأهل الرضا والكفار مما لا يعفى عنهم بالشفاعة
And intercession (shafāʻah) is among the greatest things by which [proof] is adduced. The Quran has come with it, and the traditions (āthār), and the Quran has come with it, and the traditions from the Messenger of God. And intercession, in the customary and well-known matter, occurs at lapses that warrant wrath and punishment, so the committer is pardoned through the intercession of the righteous and those with whom God is pleased. As for the disbelievers, they are not pardoned by intercession.
Source pages p. 558

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of the doctrine of intercession (shafāʻah) from Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid. Maturidi begins by stating that intercession is one of the most significant proofs in Islamic theology. He then cites its scriptural basis: the Quran and the traditions (athar) from the Prophet. After establishing its source, he defines the scope of intercession: it occurs in the customary understanding for lapses (zallāt) that incur divine displeasure and punishment. Through the intercession of the righteous (al-akhyār) and those with whom God is pleased (ahl al-riḍā), the perpetrator is forgiven. Maturidi then explicitly excludes disbelievers (kuffār) from benefiting from intercession; they are not pardoned by it. This passage affirms the Sunni position that intercession is a reality, it is for believers who have committed sins, and it is not for disbelievers.

أخيار
أهل الرضا
كفار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe intercession can benefit anyone, including disbelievers.
Correction: Maturidi clearly states that disbelievers are not pardoned by intercession; it is only for believers who have sinned.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Intercession is a Quranic and prophetic reality, but its benefit is restricted to believers who have sinned, not to disbelievers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

In which area of your life are you counting on intercession instead of personal accountability and repentance?

Definition and Scope of Intercession
Maturidi defines intercession and clarifies its scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's definition of intercession and its limitation to believing sinners, not disbelievers.

Core Idea
Intercession benefits believers with sins, not disbelievers.

Imam Maturidi here provides a precise definition of intercession (shafāʿa), grounding it in the Qur'an and prophetic traditions. He clarifies that intercession applies only to lapses deserving punishment, and only believers can benefit. This lesson unpacks that concise statement and its theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Notice how Maturidi affirms intercession as a major proof and then defines its scope.
2
Identify the key elements
Look for: the importance of intercession, its evidence (Qur'an and traditions), its context (lapses deserving wrath), its beneficiaries (righteous), and its exclusion (disbelievers).
3
Understand the limitation
Maturidi explicitly states that disbelievers are not pardoned through intercession. This is a key point distinguishing Sunni doctrine from misunderstandings.
This refutes the idea that intercession is universal.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why intercession is restricted to believers with sins, and how this aligns with divine justice and mercy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Source pages p. 558

Commentary
This passage is a concise treatment of intercession (shafāʿa) by Maturidi. He begins by asserting its importance: it is among the greatest proofs used in theological argument, and it is confirmed by the Qur'an and the traditions (āthār) from the Prophet. Then he defines intercession as it is understood in common and well-known usage: it applies to lapses (zallāt) that deserve divine wrath and punishment. The benefit of intercession is that the sinner is pardoned through the intercession of righteous people (al-akhyār) and those who are pleased with God (ahl al-riḍā). Crucially, Maturidi excludes disbelievers (al-kuffār): they are not pardoned through intercession. This clarifies the Sunni position that intercession is for believers who have committed sins, not for those who die in disbelief.

Mutawatir Reports: Definition and Binding Force
Maturidi defines mutawatir and its binding force

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi defines mutawatir reports and why they compel certain knowledge despite the fallibility of individual narrators.

Core Idea
A mutawatir report yields certainty because it is never found to be a lie.

Maturidi begins by noting that reports from messengers come through channels where error and lying are possible. Therefore, we must examine them. He then defines a special category: the mutawatir report, which by its nature cannot be a lie and thus demands acceptance. This lesson unpacks that definition and its implications for knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow. Notice how Maturidi raises the issue of fallible narrators first.
This sets the context for why mutawatir is needed.
2
Identify the problem: fallible narrators
The first sentence says narrators can err and lie. This is the starting point for classification.
All human transmitters are potentially fallible.
3
Define mutawatir: never a lie
Maturidi says: if a report is such that it is never found to be a lie, then it is mutawatir. This is the core definition.
It's about the report's character, not just number.
4
Understand the binding force
Even though each narrator individually could err, the collective agreement at the level of tawatur makes the truth evident. This compels acceptance.
Certainty arises from the impossibility of collusion on a lie.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأخبار التي تنتهي إلينا من الرسل تنتهي على السن من يُحتمل منهم الغلط والكذب؛ إذ ليس معهم دليل الصدق ولا برهان العصمة، فحق مثله النظر فيه. فإن كان مثله مما لا يوجد كذبا قط فهو الذي من انتهى إليه مثله لزمه حق شهود القول ممن اتضح البرهان عصمته، وذلك وصف خبر المتواتر، إن كلا منهم وإن لم يقم دليل على عصمته فإن الخبر منهم إذا بلغ ذلك الحد ظهر صدقه، وثبتت عصمة مثله على الكتب، وإن أمكن خلاف ذلك في كل على الإشارة. وهكذا القول فما طريقه الاجتهاد، وإن احتمل خطأ كل على الإنفراد والغلط، فإنهم لم يتفقوا بمن يوفقهم لذلك ليظهر حقه؛ إذ الآراء لا تؤدي إليه بعد اختلاف الأهواء وتفرق لذات ذي الرأي دون لطف العزيز الحميد الذي يملك إظهار حقه وعصمة خلقه فما شاء، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then the reports that reach us from the messengers reach us through persons from whom error and lying are possible, for they do not possess a proof of truthfulness nor a proof of infallibility. So it is right to examine such reports. If it is something that is never found to be a lie at all, then it is that which, when such a report reaches its extent, the right of witnessing the statement from one whose infallibility is clarified by proof becomes binding. And that is the description of the mutawatir report. For even if each one of them has no proof of his infallibility, the report from them, when it reaches that limit, its truth appears, and the infallibility of its like is established over books, even if the opposite of that is possible in each individually. And similarly, what is based on ijtihad, even if error and mistake are possible for each individually, they do not agree by one who would guide them to that so that its truth appears, for opinions do not lead to it after the divergence of desires and the dispersion of the pleasures of the person of opinion, without the kindness of the Mighty, the Praiseworthy, who possesses the ability to manifest His truth and protect His creation as He wills. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 607

Commentary
This passage classifies reports transmitted from prophets. Maturidi explains that these reports come through chains where each narrator is fallible. Therefore, examination is needed. He then defines the mutawatir report: a report that is never found to be a lie, such that when it reaches a certain number, its truth becomes evident and establishes the infallibility of the collective. Even though each individual could err, the aggregate agreement removes doubt. This is in contrast to reports that require ijtihad (individual reasoning), where error is possible in each link, and no external guide forces agreement. The passage highlights that mutawatir yields certainty, while ahad reports require ijtihad.

Key Terms
المتواتر
الغلط
الكذب
العصمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mutawatir reports require each narrator to be individually infallible.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that certainty arises from the collective number, not individual infallibility.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the defining characteristic of a mutawatir report?
A) Each narrator is known to be infallible.
B) The report is never found to be a lie at all.
C) The report is supported by a miracle.
D) The report is accepted by the majority of scholars.

Key Takeaway
A mutawatir report is defined by never being a lie, and it compels certain knowledge despite the fallibility of individual narrators.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How many of your religious beliefs are based on reports you have never examined whether they are 'never found to be a lie'? Are you willing to scrutinise one such belief today?
?
If a mutawatir report compels certainty like witnessing, do you have that level of certainty about the core truths of Islam? What practical step can you take to move from blind acceptance to genuine conviction?
?
The passage says human narrators are fallible. Where in your life have you trusted a report about Allah or His Messenger without verifying its transmission? Will you commit to finding that report's source and evaluating it?

Why Ahad Reports Require Ijtihad
Maturidi contrasts ijtihad-based reports with mutawatir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why reports that do not reach tawatur require ijtihad and do not yield certainty, according to Maturidi.

Core Idea
Reports that are not mutawatir remain open to error and require individual reasoning.

After defining mutawatir, Maturidi contrasts it with reports that are based on ijtihad. These reports, even if from multiple narrators, do not achieve the level of certainty because each link is fallible and there is no guarantee of agreement. This lesson explores that contrast.

Guided Path
1
Read the second passage
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the part beginning with 'وهكذا القول فما طريقه الاجتهاد'
This is a contrast to the mutawatir definition.
2
Identify the characteristics of ijtihad reports
Maturidi says that for such reports, error and mistake are possible for each individual narrator. This makes them uncertain.
No collective guarantee of truth.
3
Understand why they don't yield certainty
He explains that without a guide (اللطف) from Allah, differing desires prevent agreement on the truth.
Only divine grace can ensure unanimity on truth.
4
Compare with mutawatir
Mutawatir reports have a unique property that prevents them from being lies, while ahad reports rely on human reasoning which is fallible.
This is why only mutawatir gives certain knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then the reports that reach us from the messengers reach us through persons from whom error and lying are possible, for they do not possess a proof of truthfulness nor a proof of infallibility. So it is right to examine such reports. If it is something that is never found to be a lie at all, then it is that which, when such a report reaches its extent, the right of witnessing the statement from one whose infallibility is clarified by proof becomes binding. And that is the description of the mutawatir report. For even if each one of them has no proof of his infallibility, the report from them, when it reaches that limit, its truth appears, and the infallibility of its like is established over books, even if the opposite of that is possible in each individually. And similarly, what is based on ijtihad, even if error and mistake are possible for each individually, they do not agree by one who would guide them to that so that its truth appears, for opinions do not lead to it after the divergence of desires and the dispersion of the pleasures of the person of opinion, without the kindness of the Mighty, the Praiseworthy, who possesses the ability to manifest His truth and protect His creation as He wills. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 607

Commentary
This passage classifies reports transmitted from prophets. Maturidi explains that these reports come through chains where each narrator is fallible. Therefore, examination is needed. He then defines the mutawatir report: a report that is never found to be a lie, such that when it reaches a certain number, its truth becomes evident and establishes the infallibility of the collective. Even though each individual could err, the aggregate agreement removes doubt. This is in contrast to reports that require ijtihad (individual reasoning), where error is possible in each link, and no external guide forces agreement. الغلط
الأهواء
اللطف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reports from multiple narrators always give certainty.
Correction: Maturidi distinguishes mutawatir from ahad; only mutawatir gives certainty. Ahad require ijtihad and are fallible.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why do ijtihad-based reports not yield certainty?
A) They are too few narrators.
B) Each narrator may err and there is no external guide.
C) They contradict the Quran.
D) They are only from one narrator.

Key Takeaway
Ijtihad-based reports are fallible and do not produce certain knowledge; only mutawatir reports achieve that.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says that desires (ahwā’) prevent opinions from reaching truth. What is one desire you have that might be distorting your understanding of a specific Islamic teaching? What will you do this week to confront it?
?
If only Allah’s kindness can protect from error in matters of ijtihad, how often do you invoke Him with 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' before studying the sources? Will you make that a habit starting today?

Ahad Reports Require Ijtihad
Maturidi on the obligation of ijtihad for ahad reports.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why ahad reports do not provide certain knowledge and why ijtihad is necessary to decide whether to accept or reject them.

Core Idea
Ahad reports demand ijtihad because they lack certainty.

Imam Maturidi explains that ahad reports, unlike mutawatir, do not necessitate knowledge or testimony. Instead, they obligate ijtihad—a careful examination of the narrators' conditions and the report's apparent meaning. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and the methodological principle for dealing with such traditions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the nature of ahad reports
Recognize that ahad reports do not reach the degree of certainty that mutawatir reports have. Maturidi states they do not force knowledge or testimony.
This sets the stage for why ijtihad is needed.
2
See the role of ijtihad
Maturidi says action or abandonment requires ijtihad, considering the narrators' conditions and the apparent truth. Note the parallel to sensory knowledge.
3
Grasp the necessity of taking a stance
Abandoning action altogether is not justified because both options involve turning from the report. Ijtihad must weigh both sides.
This principle avoids total rejection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخبر آخر ا لا يبلغ هذا القدر في ايجاب العلم والشهادة بأنه الحق عن نبي الرحمة، فيجب العمل به والترك بالاجتهاد والنظر في أحوال الرواة، والظاهر مما ظهر حقه، وجوازه فى السمع الذى قد أحيط. ثم يعمل بما يغلب عليه الوجه وإن احتمل الغلط، إذ ربما يعمل به في علم الحس الذي هو أرفع طرق يضعف الحواس ويبعد المحسوس ولطفه. على أن ترك العمل جميعا، لا يرجع فيه إلى الإحاطة، وإلى أيهما مال كان في ذلك إعراض عن الخبر، فلذلك لزم القول فيه بالاجتهاد بالوجهين، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And another report that does not reach this degree in necessitating knowledge and testimony that it is the truth from the Prophet of mercy, then it is obligatory to act upon it and to abandon it through ijtihad and examination of the conditions of the narrators, and the apparent from what its truth appears, and its permissibility in the hearing that has been encompassed. Then one acts upon what predominates, even if error is possible, because perhaps one acts upon it in sensory knowledge which is the highest of ways despite the weakness of the senses and the distance and subtlety of the sensed object. However, abandoning action altogether does not return to encompassing; whichever one inclines to, there is in that a turning away from the report. Therefore, it is necessary to speak about it through ijtihad concerning both aspects. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 608

Commentary
This passage deals with reports that do not reach the level of tawatur (mutawatir) — i.e., ahad reports. Maturidi states that such reports do not give certain knowledge but require ijtihad to either accept or reject them, based on examination of the narrators' conditions. The decision is made according to what is more probable (ghalaba), even though error remains possible. He compares this to sensory knowledge, where even the highest senses can be weak or distant, yet we still act on probability. Abandoning action altogether is not justified because both acting and abandoning involve a turning away from the report; so ijtihad is necessary to weigh the two options. This establishes the methodological principle for dealing with non-mutawatir traditions.

Key Terms
خبر الواحد
الاجتهاد
الرواة
الظاهر
غلب على الوجه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ahad reports must be either accepted or rejected absolutely without any examination.
Correction: Maturidi argues that both acceptance and rejection require ijtihad, considering the narrators and the preponderance of evidence.

Checkpoint
How should one treat a report that does not reach the level of tawatur?
A) Reject it absolutely.
B) Accept it without examination.
C) Apply ijtihad to decide based on preponderance.
D) Wait for more reports.

Key Takeaway
Ijtihad is necessary for ahad reports, based on examination of narrators and preponderance of evidence, because total rejection or acceptance without consideration is not justified.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you come across a hadith that is not widely known, do you quickly accept or reject it based on your feelings? How does Maturidi's insistence on ijtihad challenge your approach? What specific change will you make in how you handle such reports from today?
?
The passage compares acting on probability in religious matters to acting on sensory knowledge despite its weaknesses. In what areas of your life do you rely on probability without careful examination? How can you apply the same ijtihad to your religious practice as you do to worldly matters?
?
Maturidi says that total abandonment of a report also involves 'turning away from the report.' Have you ever dismissed a weak hadith without proper research? How can you ensure that your decisions are based on ijtihad rather than laziness? What does 'there is no power except with Allah' mean for your own reliance on your reasoning?

Objection and Answer: Why Can't Entities Be Eternal Like They Last in the Afterlife?
Maturidi answers an objection: if entities can last in the afterlife without enduring, why can't they be eternal without a prior?

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the objection that if entities can persist in the afterlife without inherent duration, they could be eternal without prior non-existence.

Core Idea
Revelation and reason operate in different domains; conflating them leads to contradiction.

Maturidi addresses a clever objection: if God can make things last forever in the afterlife even though their continued existence is not intrinsically permanent, then why couldn't things be eternal from the beginning without a prior state of non-existence? He gives two distinct answers—one conceptual and one epistemological—that protect the distinction between creation and afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the objection and the two answers.
2
Distinguish the answers
Identify the first answer based on contradiction (tanāquḍ) and the second based on revelation (samʿī).
3
Reflect on the epistemological point
Consider why Maturidi uses the second argument: the objector must accept revelation if he uses a revealed premise.
4
Connect to broader theology
Relate this to the principle that rational proofs of God's existence and creation's temporality are independent of revealed claims about the afterlife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : إذا جاز عندكم بقاء الأعيان في الآخرة بما لا يبقى ، فلم لا جاز قدمها بما لا يتقدم ؟ قيل : لوجوه ، أحدها للتناقض وهو لم يكن ، فمن لا يسبقه فقيه حقيقية ، فالقول فيه بالقدم ينقضه ، ومعنى البقاء هو الكون فى مستأنف الوقت ، معه غير أو لا ، لذلك اختلفا . والثانى أن القول بالذي ذكرت من البقاء إنما هو سمعى ، فإما أن تسلم لى ذلك ، فيجب حدث الأعيان لما به عرفناه ، أو لا تسلم. فيبطل حجاجه بالسمعي على الإنكار به ، والله المستعان .
If someone says: 'Since according to you the permanence (baqā') of entities (a'yān) in the Hereafter is permissible through that which does not last, then why is it not permissible for their eternity (qidam) through that which is not preceded?' He said: 'For several reasons: one of them is contradiction (tanāquḍ) – and it is that which was not [preceded by non-existence], so the one who is not preceded is in reality [?], so the statement about it being eternal contradicts it. The meaning of permanence (baqā') is existence in a future time, either with something else or not, therefore they differ. Second: the statement about permanence that you mentioned is only based on revelation (sam'ī). So either you accept that, then the temporality (ḥadath) of the entities is necessitated by what we know of them, or you do not accept, then his argument with revelation is invalid against denial of it. And God is the Helper.'
Source pages p. 612

Commentary
This passage contains an objection (shubha) and its answer. The objector argues: if God can make things last forever in the afterlife even though their continued existence is not inherently permanent, then why couldn't things be eternal from the beginning without a prior state of non-existence? Maturidi gives two answers. First, the notion of 'being preceded' is essential to temporality, while permanence is existence in the future; they are different concepts. Second, the claim that entities can last in the afterlife is based on revelation (sam'), not reason, so the objector must accept revelation or his argument collapses.

Key Terms
بقاء
قدم
سمعي
حدث
تناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if God can make things last forever in the afterlife, then He could have created them eternal from the beginning.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the concepts of eternity (no prior) and permanence (future existence) are distinct, and the afterlife claim depends on revelation, which also affirms creation's temporality.

Checkpoint
What is the second reason Maturidi gives for why the objection is invalid?
A) Eternity and permanence are the same
B) The argument from revelation is only convincing if one accepts revelation
C) The world is not subject to divine action
D) Entities in the afterlife do not exist

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the objection by distinguishing the concepts of eternity and permanence and by pointing out the epistemic dependence of the afterlife claim on revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own existence after death, do you find yourself relying more on rational expectations or on the promises of revelation? Which of these gives you deeper conviction, and why?
?
The objection in this passage confuses two different concepts: eternity and permanence. Are there areas in your own understanding of God or the afterlife where you might be blurring distinct ideas? What specific step can you take this week to clarify that distinction in your mind?
?
Maturidi’s answer shows that not everything known through reason can be applied to matters of revelation, and vice versa. In your daily life, do you ever demand rational proof for matters of faith that are only known through revelation, or do you accept revealed truths without question? Where is the balance for you, and how can you avoid the kind of confusion the objector fell into?

The Meaning of Resurrection and Annihilation
Maturidi clarifies the meaning of the act of resurrection and annihilation, rejecting two interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of Allah's act of resurrection and annihilation, and why assigning time to the act is impossible or corrupt.

Core Idea
The act of resurrection is not tied to a specific moment; it is an eternal attribute manifesting at its appointed time.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi clarifies a subtle theological point about the divine act of resurrection and annihilation. He distinguishes between two possible interpretations of what it means for Allah to 'do' the Hour, showing why both are problematic. This helps us understand the relationship between Allah's eternal attributes and temporal events.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the two interpretations Maturidi presents.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Maturidi mentions two possible meanings of 'doing' the resurrection: making this hour its time, or making a time for takwin. Find these in the text.
The first is dismissed as impossible, the second as corrupt.
3
Understand why each is rejected
Reflect on why the first is impossible (resurrection is not now) and the second is corrupt (it implies temporality in the eternal attribute of takwin).
The key insight: takwin is eternal and cannot be bound by time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولفعل القيامة والفناء معنى آخر، إن السائل عنها إن أراد أنه يفعل الساعة، لا يخلو من أن يريد جعل هذه الساعة وقتا للقيامة أو لتكوين الله القيامة، فالأول محال؛ ليست كذلك، والثاني فاسد فيه جعل الوقت للتكوين، وذلك أمارة الحدث.
And for the act of resurrection and annihilation there is another meaning. Indeed, the questioner about it—if he intends that He does the Hour, it is inseparable from wanting to make this hour the time for resurrection or [wanting] the takwin of Allah of resurrection. The first is impossible because it is not so. And the second is corrupt, for it includes making a time for takwin, and that is a sign of temporality.
Source pages p. 646

Commentary
This passage is a clarification regarding the divine act of resurrection (qiyama) and annihilation (fana). It addresses a potential misunderstanding of what it means for God to 'do' the resurrection. Maturidi states that the act has a specific meaning. If someone asks about it, their intention could be one of two things: either they mean that God makes the current present hour the time for resurrection, or they mean that God's takwin brings about resurrection at a specific time. The first is impossible because the resurrection is clearly not occurring at the present moment. The second is corrupt (fasid) because it involves assigning a specific time to the attribute of takwin itself, which would imply that takwin is temporal (hadath). Since takwin is eternal, it cannot be tied to a specific time as a temporal event would be. Rather, the resurrection will occur at its appointed time, and God's takwin is the eternal attribute that brings it about at that time without itself being temporal.

Key Terms
القيامة (al-qiyama)
الفناء (al-fana')
الساعة (al-sa'a)
التكوين (al-takwin)
الحدث (al-hadath)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that saying 'God does the resurrection' implies that the act is happening at the present moment.
Correction: Maturidi distinguishes between the eternal attribute of takwin (which is not confined to time) and the temporal event of resurrection which occurs at its appointed time.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi consider the second interpretation of the act of resurrection to be corrupt?
A) Because it denies the resurrection altogether.
B) Because it makes the present hour the time for resurrection, which is false.
C) Because it assigns a time to takwin, implying that takwin is temporal.
D) Because it suggests that God is incapable of doing the resurrection.

Key Takeaway
Allah's act of resurrection is not a temporal event bound to a specific moment; it is an eternal attribute manifesting at its decreed time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you catch yourself thinking of Allah's actions as if they are bound by time like your own? This passage shows the eternal nature of His act — how should that change the way you wait for the resurrection? Will you repent of reducing Allah to your timeline and submit to His eternal decree?
?
If takwin is eternal and not subject to time, then your entire life is held in an eternal act of Allah. Does that truth shake you into trusting His timing even when it feels delayed? What specific area of your life do you need to surrender to His eternal wisdom right now?
?
This passage guards you from making Allah's attributes temporal — have you ever found yourself impatient with Allah as if He should act on your schedule? Commit to memorizing this principle and repeating it when impatience arises.

The Prophet’s Response to the Question of Seeing Allah
Maturidi cites the hadith on vision and defends the question of seeing with the heart

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the Prophet’s gentle response to demonstrate that asking about the vision of Allah is permissible, contrary to those who claim it is an innovation.

Core Idea
Asking about seeing Allah is not forbidden when done respectfully.

In this passage, Maturidi addresses an important theological question: Is it permissible to ask whether the Prophet saw his Lord? He cites a hadith and a specific incident, then uses the Prophet’s reaction to counter those who label such questions as innovation or disbelief. This lesson explores his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and the question
Begin by reading the hadith about seeing Allah like the moon and the incident where someone asked the Prophet if he had seen his Lord.
Pay attention to the Prophet's answer: 'With my heart.'
2
Identify the objection
Notice that Maturidi raises an objection: Why did the Prophet not rebuke the questioner, given that Allah forbade asking about certain things?
The Quranic verse 'Do not ask about things' is key.
3
Follow Maturidi's reasoning
Maturidi argues that if the question were truly an innovation or disbelief, the Prophet would have rebuked. But he did not; he answered gently.
This shows the question is permissible.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that the Prophet's gentle response sets a precedent for respectful inquiry into matters of faith.
This counters those who label such questions as blameworthy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سترون ربكم يوم القيامة كما ترون القمر لا تضامون. وسئل: هل رأيت ربك؟ فقال: بقلبي. قيل: فلم ينكر على السائل السؤال؟ وقد علم السائل أن العلم، وأنه قد علمه، وأنه لم يسأل عن ذلك، وقد حذر الله عز وجل المؤمنين عن السؤال عن أشياء قد كفوا عنها بقوله: يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا لا تَسْأَلُوا عَنْ أَشْيَاءَ، فكيف يحتمل أن يكون السؤال عن مثله بدعة، وذلك كفر في الحقيقة عند قوم، ثم لا ينهاهم عن ذلك، ولا يوبخهم في ذلك، بل يلين القول في ذلك، ويرى أن ذلك ليس ببديع، والله الموفق.
You will see your Lord on the Day of Resurrection as you see the moon, you will not be harmed. And he was asked: Did you see your Lord? He said: With my heart. It was said: Then why did he not rebuke the questioner? And the questioner knew that knowledge, and that he had known it, and that he did not ask about that, and Allah Almighty had warned the believers against asking about things they had been forbidden from by His saying: O you who have believed, do not ask about things. So how could it be that the question about the like of this is an innovation, and that is disbelief in reality according to some people, yet he did not forbid them from that, nor rebuke them for that, but rather softened the speech in that, and saw that it is not an innovation, and Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 679

Commentary
This passage presents a hadith about the vision of Allah on the Day of Resurrection likening it to seeing the moon. It then relates an incident where someone asked the Prophet if he had seen his Lord, and the Prophet answered: 'With my heart.' A question arises: Why did the Prophet not rebuke the questioner, given that Allah had forbidden asking about certain things in the verse 'Do not ask about things'? The author argues that if such a question were an innovation or disbelief, the Prophet would have forbidden it. But since he did not rebuke, but rather replied gently and did not consider it an innovation, the question is permissible. This establishes the permissibility of inquiring about the vision of Allah, contrary to those who consider it blameworthy.

Key Terms
رؤية
قلب
بدعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any question about seeing Allah is forbidden and considered innovation.
Correction: The Prophet’s gentle response shows that respectful inquiry is allowed.

Checkpoint

A) Because the Quran explicitly permits such questions.
B) Because the Prophet answered gently and did not rebuke the questioner.
C) Because the questioner was a companion.
D) Because the hadith about seeing the moon proves it.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet’s gentle answer to the question about seeing Allah demonstrates that such questions are not inherently blameworthy or innovative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Rational Necessity of Divine Commands and Gratitude
Maturidi presents al-Husayn's rational argument for the necessity of divine commands and gratitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine commands and prohibitions are rationally necessary and why gratitude is obligatory based on human nature and reason.

Core Idea
Gratitude for God's favors is rationally required, so divine commands and prohibitions are necessary.

Al-Husayn and Maturidi present a rational argument for the necessity of divine commands and prohibitions. They ground these obligations in the nature of humans as rational beings capable of inferring unseen truths from observed evidence, and in the natural duty of gratitude for blessings. This passage establishes that without such commands, ignorance and evil would be permitted.

Guided Path
1
Recognize human endowment
The passage states humans are created disciplined, knowing benefit and harm, and able to infer from the seen to the unseen. This capacity underpins the need for divine guidance.
2
Necessity of imposing knowledge
Given human capacity, it is not permissible that knowledge (ma'rifa) be left unrequired and ignorance allowed, for that would permit lying and every blameworthy thing.
3
Obligation of gratitude
Since God bestows favors in creation, gratitude is rationally necessary, so God requires it. This makes commands and prohibitions meaningful.
Gratitude is a logical consequence of receiving blessings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القول بالأمر والنهي والترغيب والترهيب، مع ما يقدم منه الكافي من ذلك الذي ذكره الحسين: أن الله خلق خلقا مذلّلا بالتأديب، عارفا بالنفع والضر، مستدلا بالذي شهد من الحجة على الذي غاب، لم يجز أن لا يفرض المعرفة ولا يحضر عليه الجهل، فيكون فيه إباحة الكذب وكل ذميم. مع ما كان لمن نعم عليه في الخلقة، وشكر النعمة لازم فى العقل، فاستاداه. ثم الوعد والوعيد فى الترغيب بتعظيمه، والترحيب عن الاستخفاف به، ثم إذ كرمه بفنون كل الكرم فعلى ذلك ثوابه لا أمد له، وإذ كان الكفر غاية في العصيان فكذلك عقوبته. وأيضا أن الايمان تصديق بما لا نهاية له ولا نفاذ، والكفر تكذيب بما لا نهاية له ولا نفاذ، فعلى ذلك جزاؤهما، ولهذا يجوز العفو عما دون الكفر لأنه ليس يجحد لما لا نهاية له، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then comes the statement on command and prohibition and encouragement and deterrence, with what suffices from that which al-Husayn mentioned: that Allah created a creation subdued by discipline, knowing benefit and harm, inferring from the evidence witnessed to the unseen. It was not permissible that knowledge not be imposed and ignorance be brought upon him, for that would permit lying and every blameworthy thing. Along with what is due to the one upon whom He bestowed favor in creation, and gratitude for blessing is necessary in the intellect, so He required it. Then the promise and threat for encouraging His glorification and deterring from taking Him lightly. Then since He honored him with all kinds of honor, so his reward is without limit, and since disbelief is the ultimate in disobedience, so is his punishment. Also, faith is affirmation of what has no end and no termination, and disbelief is denial of what has no end and no termination, so accordingly is their requital. For this reason, pardon is permissible for what is less than disbelief, because it does not deny what has no end. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 699

Commentary
This passage continues the argument for the necessity of divine commands, prohibitions, encouragements, and deterrents. It references al-Husayn's earlier point that God created humans with the ability to be disciplined, knowledge of benefit and harm, and inference from evidence to the unseen. It argues that imposing knowledge (المعرفة) is obligatory; otherwise, ignorance would be permitted, leading to lying and evil. Since God bestows favors, gratitude is rationally obligatory, so God requires it. Then promise and threat (الوعد والوعيد) are necessary to encourage glorification and deter disrespect. Because God honors humans greatly, the reward for obedience is infinite, and because disbelief is the ultimate transgression, its punishment is likewise infinite. Additionally, faith is affirmation of what has no end (i.e., God's essence and attributes), and disbelief is denial of what has no end, so their recompense is eternal. However, forgiveness for lesser sins than disbelief is permissible because they do not involve denial of the infinite.

Key Terms
الأمر
النهي
الترغيب
الترهيب
المعرفة
الشكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine commands and prohibitions are arbitrary and based solely on God's will without rational grounding.
Correction: This passage shows that commands are grounded in human nature and the rational duty of gratitude, making them inherently meaningful.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the necessity of imposing knowledge according to al-Husayn?
A) God's power and sovereignty
B) Human capacity for discipline and inference
C) Tradition and revelation
D) Fear of punishment

Key Takeaway
Divine commands and the duty to know God are grounded in human nature and the rational obligation of gratitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says gratitude for God's favors is 'necessary in the intellect'. When did you last consciously fulfill this intellectual duty? What is one specific favor you have not genuinely thanked Allah for, and what will you do today to change that?

Eternal Reward and Punishment: The Nature of Faith and Disbelief
Maturidi explains the eternal nature of reward and punishment based on the nature of faith and disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the reward for faith and punishment for disbelief are eternal, and why forgiveness is possible for lesser sins.

Core Idea
Faith affirms the infinite, so its reward is infinite; disbelief denies the infinite, so its punishment is eternal.

Building on the necessity of commands and prohibitions, this passage addresses the consequences: promise of reward and threat of punishment. It argues that because God honors humans immensely and because faith is affirmation of the infinite, the reward is without end. Similarly, disbelief is denial of the infinite, so its punishment is eternal. However, sins less than disbelief can be forgiven because they do not involve such denial.

Guided Path
1
Understand the purpose of promise and threat
Promise and threat are meant to encourage glorification of God and deter from taking Him lightly.
2
Proportionality of reward and punishment
God's immense honor to humans demands infinite reward; ultimate disobedience (disbelief) demands infinite punishment.
3
Nature of faith and disbelief
Faith is affirmation of the infinite, so requital is eternal; disbelief is denial of the infinite, so requital is eternal.
4
Forgiveness for lesser sins
Sins less than disbelief do not involve denial of the infinite, so forgiveness is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القول بالأمر والنهي والترغيب والترهيب، مع ما يقدم منه الكافي من ذلك الذي ذكره الحسين: أن الله خلق خلقا مذلّلا بالتأديب، عارفا بالنفع والضر، مستدلا بالذي شهد من الحجة على الذي غاب، لم يجز أن لا يفرض المعرفة ولا يحضر عليه الجهل، فيكون فيه إباحة الكذب وكل ذميم. مع ما كان لمن نعم عليه في الخلقة، وشكر النعمة لازم فى العقل، فاستاداه. ثم الوعد والوعيد فى الترغيب بتعظيمه، والترحيب عن الاستخفاف به، ثم إذ كرمه بفنون كل الكرم فعلى ذلك ثوابه لا أمد له، وإذ كان الكفر غاية في العصيان فكذلك عقوبته. وأيضا أن الايمان تصديق بما لا نهاية له ولا نفاذ، والكفر تكذيب بما لا نهاية له ولا نفاذ، فعلى ذلك جزاؤهما، ولهذا يجوز العفو عما دون الكفر لأنه ليس يجحد لما لا نهاية له، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then comes the statement on command and prohibition and encouragement and deterrence, with what suffices from that which al-Husayn mentioned: that Allah created a creation subdued by discipline, knowing benefit and harm, inferring from the evidence witnessed to the unseen. It was not permissible that knowledge not be imposed and ignorance be brought upon him, for that would permit lying and every blameworthy thing. Along with what is due to the one upon whom He bestowed favor in creation, and gratitude for blessing is necessary in the intellect, so He required it. Then the promise and threat for encouraging His glorification and deterring from taking Him lightly. Then since He honored him with all kinds of honor, so his reward is without limit, and since disbelief is the ultimate in disobedience, so is his punishment. Also, faith is affirmation of what has no end and no termination, and disbelief is denial of what has no end and no termination, so accordingly is their requital. For this reason, pardon is permissible for what is less than disbelief, because it does not deny what has no end. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 699

Commentary
This passage continues the argument for the necessity of divine commands, prohibitions, encouragements, and deterrents. It references al-Husayn's earlier point that God created humans with the ability to be disciplined, knowledge of benefit and harm, and inference from evidence to the unseen. It argues that imposing knowledge (المعرفة) is obligatory; otherwise, ignorance would be permitted, leading to lying and evil. Since God bestows favors, gratitude is rationally obligatory, so God requires it. Then promise and threat (الوعد والوعيد) are necessary to encourage glorification and deter disrespect. Because God honors humans greatly, the reward for obedience is infinite, and because disbelief is the ultimate transgression, its punishment is likewise infinite. Additionally, faith is affirmation of what has no end (i.e., God's essence and attributes), and disbelief is denial of what has no end, so their recompense is eternal. However, forgiveness for lesser sins than disbelief is permissible because they do not involve denial of the infinite.

Key Terms
الوعد
الوعيد
الإيمان
الكفر
العفو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God punishes disbelief harshly out of vengeance or arbitrariness.
Correction: The passage shows that punishment is proportionate to the nature of disbelief as denial of the infinite, making it just and necessary.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can sins less than disbelief be forgiven?
A) God is merciful by nature
B) They do not involve denial of what has no end
C) They are minor and insignificant
D) Repentance is easy to achieve

Key Takeaway
The eternity of reward and punishment is proportionate to the infinite nature of faith and disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to have faith, but do you truly live as if God is infinite and His mercy has no end? Where in your life have you limited Him by your doubts or lack of trust? This lesson ties infinite reward to infinite affirmation—if your heart wavers, is your faith truly affirming the infinite? What will you do today to strengthen that affirmation?
?
When you think of disbelievers, do you feel their punishment is too harsh? The passage says it is not harsh but proportionate—denial of the infinite deserves infinite consequence. Does your heart accept God's justice in this, or does it rebel? If you find yourself objecting, what does that reveal about your own understanding of His greatness? Repent and submit.
?
The lesson says pardon is only for sins less than disbelief. Is there any area in your life where you are denying God's power or promises—perhaps by despairing of His mercy or doubting His wisdom? That small denial is a lesser sin, but it can grow. Will you confess it and return to affirming His infinite attributes today?

Refuting al-Warraq's Objection on Asking the People of the Reminder
Objection and three answers by Imam Maturidi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes al-Warraq's objection against the Quranic command to ask the People of the Reminder by presenting three distinct answers that harmonize the command with the charge of concealing truth.

Core Idea
God's command to ask can stand even if the asked are accused of concealing truth.

This passage addresses a theological objection raised by al-Warraq: how can the Quran command us to ask the People of the Book when they are also accused of concealing the truth? Imam Maturidi offers three harmonizing answers that preserve the integrity of the Quranic command and demonstrate the compatibility of divine revelations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read al-Warraq's objection carefully. He points out an apparent contradiction: the Quran commands asking the People of the Reminder, yet elsewhere testifies that they conceal the truth. Note the specific verse references.
2
First answer: divine compulsion
Understand the first answer: after God supported the Prophet with clear proofs, the People of the Book inclined to the Book. The command was given because God would subdue them and compel them to agree, making it a sign. See the supporting verse from Shu'ara.
3
Second answer: appeal to a trusted person
The second answer addresses stubbornness: even after proof, a person may only yield when asked by someone they trust. The command is to ask such a trusted individual to break the stubbornness.
4
Third answer and additional interpretation
The third answer restricts the command to those among them who have accepted Islam. Note also the possible interpretation that 'People of the Reminder' means those entrusted with refuting falsehood.
This shows the flexibility of interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطعن الوراق فى قوله : " فَاسْأَلُوا أَهْلَ الذِّكْرِ ١٤ أن كيف أمر بذلك مع " الشهادة عليهم بكتمان الحق ؟ فأجيب بما إذ أيد الله نبوة محمد بالحجج القاهرة مالوا إلى الكتاب ، فقيل لهم ذلك على أن الله يسخرهم في ذلك ويضطرهم إلى الموافقة ، فيكون ذلك من جليل آياته ، إذ جمع عليه الأعداء والأولياء وهو قوله : أو لَم يَكُن لَهُمْ آيَةٌ أَنْ يَعْلَمَه عُلَمَاءُ بَنِي إِسْرَائِيلَ ،٢ . وأيضا إن ذا على ما يعرف من لجاج الرجل بعد إقامة البرهان عليه ، أن يقال : فاسأل ذلك فلانا ممن يطمع سكون قلبه إليه فيترك اللجاج. والثالث أن يكون المراد يرجع إلى من أسلم منهم ، وبالله التوفيق . وجائز أن يكون المراد بأهل الذكر هم التحكيم إليهم عن الكذب ، والله أعلم .
And al-Warraq objected concerning His saying: "So ask the people of the Reminder" — how could He command that despite the testimony against them of concealing the truth? So it was answered: Since God supported the prophethood of Muhammad with compelling proofs, they inclined to the Book, so that was said to them on the basis that God would subdue them in that matter and compel them to agreement, so that would be among His mighty signs, as He gathered against him the enemies and the allies, and that is His saying: "Or was it not a sign for them that the scholars of the Children of Israel know it?" And also, it is according to what is known of a man's stubbornness after the proof is established against him — that one says: "Ask so-and-so, from whom one hopes his heart will be calmed, so he leaves off stubbornness." And the third: that the meaning refers to those who accepted Islam among them. And with God is success. And it is possible that the meaning of "the people of the Reminder" is those who are entrusted with refuting falsehood, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 798

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by al-Warraq and a three-part answer. The objector challenges the Quranic command "ask the people of the Reminder" (al-Nahl 16:43, al-Anbiya 21:7) by noting that the same people are accused of concealing the truth (cf. Aal 'Imran 3:187). The first answer explains that after God supported Muhammad with clear proofs, the People of the Book inclined toward the Quran; the command is to ask them because God will subdue and compel them to agree, making it a divine sign—citing the verse that the scholars of Israel know it (al-Shu'ara 26:197). The second answer says that sometimes a stubborn person, even after proof, will only yield when asked by someone he trusts; the command is to ask such a person. The third answer restricts the command to those who have already accepted Islam among them. The passage adds a possible interpretation that "people of the Reminder" refers to those entrusted with refuting falsehood.

Key Terms
أهل الذكر
الوراق
بكتمان الحق
اللجاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran contradicts itself by commanding to ask people who conceal the truth.
Correction: Imam Maturidi shows that the command is not contradictory; it can be explained by divine compulsion, addressing stubbornness, or restricting to believing individuals.

Checkpoint
What is the first answer to al-Warraq's objection regarding the command to ask the People of the Reminder?
A) They were already Muslims.
B) God would compel them to agree after supporting the Prophet with proofs.
C) The command refers only to specific individuals.
D) The command was abrogated.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic command to ask the People of the Reminder is not invalidated by the charge of concealment; multiple resolutions demonstrate the consistency of divine revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see people who know the truth but hide it, does it ever make you doubt the reliability of all knowledge? How does this lesson challenge you to trust that God is ultimately in control over what is revealed and concealed?
?
Think of a belief you hold stubbornly despite clear evidence against it. Who is the one person whose sincere counsel could break your resistance? Will you seek them out this week?
?
If you are among those entrusted with refuting falsehood, are you actually doing it? Or do you remain silent out of fear or comfort? What would it cost you to speak the truth—and what would it cost you not to?

Objection about Angels at Badr and the Responses
Al-Warraq's objection and two answers plus Ibn al-Rawandi's critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Warraq's objection regarding the presence of angels at Badr and the two responses given to defend the Prophet's report.

Core Idea
Angels were at Badr but not at Uhud because Badr was a unique first battle.

Imam Maturidi addresses Al-Warraq's objection that if angels were present at Badr, why were they not seen at Uhud? Two answers are provided, and Ibn al-Rawandi criticizes Al-Warraq's inconsistency in rejecting prophetic reports while accepting Manichaean myths. This lesson breaks down the objection and the defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read Al-Warraq's question carefully to understand the challenge: if angels were at Badr, where were they at Uhud?
2
Analyze the first answer
The first answer suggests that at Badr, people saw heads without visible killers, implying angelic intervention. The numbers mentioned refer to unrecognizable forms.
This answer highlights that the angels were not seen clearly but their effects were noticed.
3
Analyze the second answer
The second answer states that Badr was the first battle, so God wanted to give victory to manifest truth and nullify falsehood. This implies that the situation at Uhud was different.
This emphasizes the unique nature of Badr in Islamic history.
4
Reflect on Ibn al-Rawandi's critique
Ibn al-Rawandi points out the inconsistency in Al-Warraq rejecting prophetic reports while accepting absurd Manichaean claims. Consider how this strengthens the defense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطعن الوراق إخبار رسول الله بحضور الملائكة يوم بدر ، قال : أين كانوا أحد ؟ يوم جواب الأول ظهور رؤوس ببدر بلا قاتل رأوه ، وبيان المذكور من الأعداد أنهم رأوا صورا لم يعرفوهم . وجواب الثانى أن ذلك أول حرب، فأراد الله تعالى أن ينصرهم ليظهر الحق ويبطل الباطل . قال ابن الروندى : / العجب من الوراق حيث جحد أخبار الرسل مع [١٠٣ ب] ] البراهين ودعاء إلى قبول قول المنانية ، وألزم القوم حماقاتهم من بسط السماوات من جلود الشياطين ، واضطراب الأرض باضطراب الحيات والعقارب فيها ، وقبول أخبارهم بعمل النور والظلمة ، ودفع ما هو في عقلهم حسنة ، وبالله التوفيق . قال الشيخ رحمه الله : وفى أمر بدر وجوه من المعتبر أحدها عمل كف من تراب إن أصاب كلا ، وفيه ما ذكر ، وفيه ما يشبه المباهلة من قول إلى منهم جهل : اللهم ابصر أمرنا وأوصلنا للرحم ، وجمع والله الموفق الأئمة من الكفرة وغير ذلك،
And al-Warraq objected to the Prophet's informing of the presence of angels on the Day of Badr. He said: Where were they on the Day of Uhud? The first answer: The appearance of heads at Badr without a killer they saw, and the explanation of the mentioned numbers—that they saw forms they did not recognize. The second answer: That was the first battle, so God Almighty wanted to give them victory to manifest the truth and nullify falsehood. Ibn al-Rawandi said: It is amazing of al-Warraq that he denied the reports of messengers despite the proofs, and he called to accept the saying of the Manichaeans, and he saddled the people with their follies: spreading out the heavens from the skins of devils, the disturbance of the earth by the disturbance of snakes and scorpions in it, and accepting their reports about the work of light and darkness, and rejecting what in their intellect is good. And with God is success. The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: In the matter of Badr there are aspects to be considered: one of them is the handful of dust that struck every one, and in it is what was mentioned, and in it is something resembling imprecation (mubahala) from his saying to one of them in ignorance: 'O God, make our matter clear and connect us to mercy,' and he gathered—and God is the granter of success—the leaders from the disbelievers and other things.
Source pages p. 798

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by al-Warraq regarding the Prophet's report of angels at Badr: if angels were present at Badr, why were they not at the Battle of Uhud? Two answers are given. The first answer: at Badr, people saw heads without visible killers, and the reported numbers refer to unrecognizable forms, implying angelic intervention. The second answer: Badr was the first battle, and God wanted to give the Muslims victory to establish truth and destroy falsehood. Ibn al-Rawandi then criticizes al-Warraq for denying the reports of prophets while accepting Manichaean myths, listing absurdities like heavens made from demon skins and earth disturbed by snakes—a refutation by counting al-Warraq's inconsistency. Finally, the author (Imam Maturidi) adds three lessons from Badr: (1) the handful of dust that struck every enemy, (2) the mention of something resembling imprecation, where the Prophet prayed against a certain disbeliever, and (3) that God gathered the leaders of disbelief for defeat. The phrase 'and other things' indicates further aspects not detailed.

Key Terms
الوراق
بدر
الملائكة
ابن الروندي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Angels should be visibly present at every battle if they were at Badr.
Correction: Angels are sent by God according to specific wisdom; Badr was unique as the first battle to establish truth, so angelic aid was manifested distinctively.

Checkpoint
What is the second answer given for why angels were not present at Uhud?
A) The angels were only sent to help the Jews.
B) Badr was the first battle and God wanted to establish truth.
C) The angels had been recalled to heaven.
D) The Muslims did not pray for angels at Uhud.

Key Takeaway
God's sending of angels at Badr was a special dispensation for the first battle, not a routine occurrence for every conflict.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter an apparent inconsistency in religious teachings, do you react like al-Warraq—dismissing the source—or do you seek deeper understanding like the scholars? What will you do the next time such a doubt arises?
?
Do you truly believe that God can send unseen help (like angels at Badr) in your own life? How does that belief affect your reliance on Him in your daily struggles?
?
Ibn al-Rawandi criticized al-Warraq for accepting absurd beliefs from Manichaeans while rejecting the truth from prophets. Are there any beliefs or influences in your life that you have accepted without critical examination? Will you commit to examining them against the truth of revelation?

Lessons from the Battle of Badr
Three lessons Imam Maturidi derives from the Battle of Badr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three aspects of the Battle of Badr that Imam Maturidi highlights as lessons for believers.

Core Idea
Badr teaches divine intervention, prophetic prayer, and the gathering of evil for defeat.

Imam Maturidi extracts three lessons from the Battle of Badr: the miraculous handful of dust that struck every enemy, a prayer resembling imprecation (mubahala) uttered against a disbeliever, and the fact that God gathered the leaders of disbelief to be defeated. This lesson explores each point as a source of reflection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Badr's lessons
Read the short passage where Imam Maturidi enumerates three aspects to be considered from the Battle of Badr.
Notice the brevity; each point is significant.
2
Understand the handful of dust
The first lesson is about the handful of dust that the Prophet threw, which struck every enemy soldier. This was a miraculous event.
Refer to the broader tradition for details, but the lesson here is the divine intervention.
3
Understand the mubahala-like prayer
The second lesson is a prayer that resembles imprecation (mubahala) where the Prophet said, 'O God, make our matter clear and connect us to mercy.' This shows seeking divine judgment against disbelief.
The word 'mubahala' implies calling down God's curse on the lying party.
4
Understand the gathering of leaders
The third lesson is that God gathered the leaders of the disbelievers at Badr to be defeated, demonstrating His power and the futility of their leadership.
