Refuting the First Type of Intercession
Maturidi rejects the division of intercession into two types in the afterlife.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi rejects the notion that intercession in the afterlife can involve mentioning merits to Allah, and why only supplication is valid.

Core Idea
Intercession in the afterlife is only supplication, not pleading merits.

Some scholars divided intercession into two types: mentioning someone's virtues before a superior, and praying for them. Imam Maturidi examines whether the first type has any meaning in the afterlife. He concludes it does not, based on two powerful arguments rooted in divine knowledge and the records of deeds. Let's explore his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice how the author first presents the two views he intends to refute.
The passage begins with 'And some of them said' – this is the view being critiqued.
2
Identify the two proposed types of intercession
The first type is mentioning virtues to gain status; the second is supplication. Understand the distinction before following the refutation.
3
Understand the first refutation
The first reason: mentioning merits would require evaluating a person's status based on ignorance, but Allah alone knows all realities. Messengers admitted ignorance on the Day of Judgment, showing that such evaluation is impossible for anyone but Allah.
The author cites verses 5:109 and 5:117 to support this.
4
Understand the second refutation
The second reason: written records of all deeds are sufficient for any evaluation. If the purpose is to inform, Allah's knowledge already suffices. Hence, mentioning merits is redundant.
This argument emphasizes the sufficiency of divine omniscience and preserved records.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال بعضهم: الشفاعة تخرج على وجهين: على ذكر محاسن أحد عند آخر ليقدر له عنده المنزلة والرتبة، والثانى أن يدعو له، فالأول هو الذي يحتمل توجيه الشفاعة إليه، والثانى قد بين فيمن يقوله: الذين يحملون العرش، إلى قوله: وَذَلِكَ هُوَ الفَوْزُ العَظِيمُ، وقوله: وَلَا يَشْفَعُونَ إِلَّا لِمَنْ ارْتَضَى، والحرف يدل على وجهى الشفاعة؛ لأن المرتضى هو ذو منزلة وقدر، هو ممن تضمنته آية شفاعة الملائكة. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: فنقول وبالله التوفيق: الوجه في الآخرة لا معنى له لوجهين: أحدهما أنه فى تقدير الأمر عند من يجهله، والله جل ثناؤه هو العليم بحقيقة ذلك. بل غيره مما يجوز عليه خفاء الحقايق كقوله يوم يجمع الله الرسل فيقول: "مَاذَا أَجَيْتُم؟ قالوا لا علم لنا"، وقال عيسى: "مَا قُلْتُ لَهُمْ إِلَّا مَا أَمَرْتَنِي بِهِ"، فكان في ذلك عبد الله، وهم قد تبروا عن العلم بذلك وأقروا بأن الله هو المتشرد بعلم ذلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله. والثانى أن ثمة كتب يقرأ فيها أعمال بني آدم وما سبق منهم من صغير أو كبير فهى الكافية في التقدير إن كان في حق الاحتجاج، وإن كان في حق الإعلام فعلم الله عن ذلك مغن، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And some of them said: Intercession appears in two ways: mentioning the virtues of someone before another so that he may be granted status and rank with him, and the second is supplication for him. The first is what can be directed as intercession, and the second has been clarified regarding those who say: 'Those who bear the Throne... up to His saying: 'And that is the great attainment' [40:9], and His saying: 'And they do not intercede except for whom He is pleased' [21:28]. And the letter (word) indicates the two types of intercession; because 'the one pleased' (al-murtaḍā) is one of status and rank, and he is among those included in the verse of the angels' intercession. The Shaykh (Imam Maturidi) said, may Allah have mercy on him: We say, and with Allah is success: The first type (the mention of merits) in the afterlife has no meaning for two reasons: One of them is that it would involve estimating a matter for one who is ignorant of it, while Allah, Glorified and Exalted, is the Knower of the truth of that. Rather, other than Him is subject to the concealment of realities, as His saying on the Day when He gathers the messengers: 'What answer were you given?' They said: 'No knowledge we have' [5:109], and Jesus said: 'I said not to them except what You commanded me' [5:117]. So in that is the servitude of Allah, and they disavowed knowledge of that and acknowledged that Allah is the sole possessor of knowledge of that. And there is no power except with Allah. The second reason is that there are books in which the deeds of the children of Adam are read, what preceded from them of small or big, and that is sufficient for estimation if it is concerning argument, and if it is concerning informing, then Allah's knowledge of that suffices. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 965

Commentary
This passage first presents a division of intercession into two types: mentioning merits and supplication. The author then refutes the applicability of the first type in the afterlife with two arguments: (1) it would require evaluating a person's status based on ignorance, but only Allah has full knowledge, as shown by the messengers' admission of ignorance on the Day of Judgment; (2) the records of deeds are sufficient for evaluation. Thus, the concept of intercession as mentioning merits before Allah is invalid in the afterlife; only supplication is valid.

Key Terms
الشفاعة
المحاسن
المنزلة
الرتبة
الدعاء
المرتضى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Intercession in the afterlife can involve pleading someone's merits before Allah.
Correction: Imam Maturidi shows this is invalid because it implies ignorance on the part of the intercessor and is redundant given Allah's complete knowledge and the records of deeds.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the first type of intercession (mentioning merits) in the afterlife?
A) Because it would imply ignorance on Allah's part.
B) Because it is not mentioned in the Qur'an.
C) Because only prophets can intercede.
D) Because angels do not intercede.

Key Takeaway
Imam Maturidi conclusively shows that intercession by mentioning merits is meaningless in the afterlife; only supplication (du'a) is valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about intercession, do you secretly hope that the virtues of someone else—a scholar, a saint, or even the Prophet—will cover for your own shortcomings? This lesson teaches that such merit-mentioning is meaningless before Allah. Are you willing to stand on the record of your own deeds and face Allah's perfect knowledge, without leaning on the merits of others?
?
Imagine yourself on the Day of Judgment. The prophets themselves disclaim knowledge of their followers' responses. They do not testify to merits. Does your reliance on intercession shift from expecting someone to 'put in a good word' to humbly asking Allah's mercy through supplication? Have you ever truly felt that urgency?
?
If you knew that every deed you have done—small or big—is already recorded and known to Allah, and that no amount of someone else's testimony can add or subtract from that record, how would that change the way you live today? Are there deeds you are relying on 'other merits' to excuse, rather than turning to Allah in repentance and supplication?

Three Reasons Against Intercession for the Already Forgiven
Maturidi enumerates three reasons against intercession for the already forgiven.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's three reasons why it is improper to seek intercession for those already forgiven or promised Paradise.

Core Idea
Intercession is inappropriate when forgiveness is already guaranteed.

Maturidi presents a logical argument against seeking intercession for those who are already assured of salvation. He outlines three distinct reasons (wujuh) that demonstrate the impropriety of such a request, rooted in divine wisdom and the nature of divine justice and grace.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall argument.
2
Identify the three reasons
Notice how each reason is introduced ('أحدها', 'والثانى', 'والثالث') and what each one argues.
3
Understand the theological basis
Reflect on how each reason connects to divine wisdom (حكمة) and the status of the forgiven.
4
Apply to the debate on major sins
Maturidi links the third reason to the topic of major sins and intercession by grace vs. justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الآية في الدعاء فكذلك نقول بدعاء] لمن له ذلك الوصف ويشفع له كان في ذلك منه من المآثم والذنوب ، لا أنه إذا كان أفعالهم ذلك فيشفع لأنه لا يجوز في الحكمة تعذيبهم على ما ذكر من الأفعال بل لهم عليها أعظم الثواب وأرفع المأوى ، فطلب الشفاعة والمغفرة لمثله يقبح من وجوه : أحدها أن ذلك إذ لا يجوز في الحكمة تعذيبه فكأنهم طلبوا منه أن لا يجور ولا يسفه " . لأفستى الخلق يخرج مخرج التفسيق ، فضلا من أن يتضرع إلى الله ، جل هذا الوصف. والثانى أن الحق في مثله وذلك الكريم الحكيم معاقب عن مثان غير إذ هو يلقى ذلك منه بالشكر والحمد ، وفى الدعاء كتمان ذلك وكفرانه ومحال الإذن فى مثله والدعاء ، والله الموفق . والثالث أن ذلك فى الموعود له الجنة والمبشر بها فبطلان مثله يوجب الجهالة في ذلك ، إلا أن يكون الوقت لم يبين ، يكون ذلك في الاستعجال ، وهو قولنا ، في أصحاب الكبائر أنهم لو عذبوا بقدر الذنوب لكان ذلك في الحكمة عدلا فيشفع لسائلهم بالفضل والإحسان دون العدل والاستيفاء ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
As for the verse concerning supplication, we likewise say: supplication for one who has that description and intercedes for him, there would be from him sins and offenses. But if their actions were such that they intercede, it is not because it is not permissible in wisdom to punish them for the mentioned actions; rather, for them there is the greatest reward and the highest abode. So seeking intercession and forgiveness for someone like that is reprehensible from aspects: First, that since it is not permissible in wisdom to punish him, it is as if they asked him not to be unjust or foolish. [That] comes out as a declaration of disobedience, even more so than supplicating to God—exalted is this description. Second, that the truth concerning someone like that is that the Generous, Wise is requiring from [؟] others, while he receives that from Him with thanks and praise; and in supplication there is concealment of that and ingratitude, and permission for such supplication and prayer is impossible. And God is the grantor of success. Third, that this concerns the one promised Paradise and given glad tidings of it; so invalidating his like entails ignorance in that matter, unless the time had not been made clear, then it would be a matter of haste. And this is our statement concerning the people of major sins: if they were punished to the extent of sins, that would be just in wisdom, so intercession for their asker is by grace and beneficence, not by justice and exact retribution. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 966

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments (wujuh) demonstrating the impropriety of seeking intercession or forgiveness for those who have already been promised Paradise or are already forgiven. The author begins by stating the premise: if someone has the described status (presumably believers who will be saved), then their actions that are considered sins are not subject to punishment because it is not wise to punish them for such deeds; instead, they deserve great reward. Therefore, asking intercession for such a person is reprehensible. The first reason: since it is not permissible in wisdom to punish them, requesting intercession implies asking God not to be unjust or foolish, which is disrespectful. The second reason: the correct attitude for such a person is gratitude and praise for the blessings received; supplication for forgiveness would conceal that gratitude and be ungrateful. The third reason: if the person is already promised Paradise, then requesting intercession suggests ignorance of God's promise, unless the timing is unclear, in which case it would be impatience. The author then relates this to the people of major sins: if they are punished proportionally, that is just; intercession for them is by grace and bounty, not by justice. The three reasons together show the logical and theological impropriety of intercession for those already assured of salvation.

Key Terms
شفاعة
مغفرة
حكمة
وجوه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage denies intercession entirely.
Correction: The passage only prohibits intercession for those already promised forgiveness or paradise; intercession for others (e.g., major sinners) is permissible by grace.

Checkpoint
How many وجوه (aspects) does the author mention for the impropriety of intercession in this passage?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that seeking intercession for those already forgiven is improper because it implies requesting what is already granted and displays ingratitude or impatience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you ask Allah for forgiveness, do you genuinely feel gratitude for what He has already given, or do you sometimes take His blessings for granted? How does this lesson's second reason about ingratitude challenge your own supplications?
?
Have you ever prayed for something that Allah has already promised you, like paradise for believers? Does this lesson reveal impatience or lack of trust in your heart? How can you change that?
?
How does understanding that intercession is not for those already forgiven affect your view of the Prophet's intercession? Does it make you more conscious of your own state and need for sincere repentance?

Interpreting Quranic Threats: Generality and Specification
Maturidi argues for specification in Quranic threats.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's principle that Quranic threats of punishment are not universally applicable but require specification to avoid contradiction and uphold divine wisdom.

Core Idea
Quranic threats target the 'absolute wicked', not every sinner.

Maturidi begins by establishing a principle: threatened actions and the names of their doers vary in ugliness and blame. He then applies this to Quranic threats, arguing that a literal universal interpretation would lead to contradictions, undermining the Quran's divine origin. This lesson explores his reasoning and its application to the categories of 'righteous' and 'wicked'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow. Then read the translation. Pay attention to how Maturidi moves from a general principle to a specific example.
The passage is dense; don't worry about understanding every word at first.
2
Identify the starting principle
Maturidi states that threatened actions occur with varying degrees of ugliness, and the names of agents imply different meanings. Notice that he is establishing diversity, not uniformity.
This principle undercuts the assumption that all threats apply equally.
3
Trace the argument against absolute generality
Maturidi argues that if general threats were applied absolutely, it would lead to contradictions, allowing heretics to criticize the Quran. He cites Quranic verses about preservation and no contradiction.
He uses the Quran's own claims to defend its consistency.
4
Examine the example of the righteous and wicked
Maturidi introduces the Quranic pairing of 'righteous' and 'wicked' (الفجار). He notes that God specifies 'the absolute wicked' (الفاجر المطلق) as the target of the threat in Surah al-Mutaffifin.
This shows that the threat is not for every sinner but for those who are utterly wicked.
5
Grasp the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that one must understand the intended scope of Quranic threats. The principle of specification preserves divine wisdom and the integrity of the Quran.
This aligns with the broader Maturidi methodology of using reason and revelation together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم الأصل في كل شيء أوعد عليه أن حقيقة ذلك تقع من صاحبه على وجوه من القبح مما يعلم كل تفاوت ذلك في العقول ، وكذلك كل اسم جاء به تسمية الفاعل أن ذلك يقتضى مختلفا من معان لا يعقل لا تكون هى من كل الوجوه على وزن واحد في القبح ولا فاعله في الذم أو يمتحن فأما وذلك عيب عن السامع ، لزمه بعقله الذى أكرم معرفة اختلاف مواقع ذلك أن لا يجمع بينها إلا أن ما عليه الأمة من الأفهام فوجدها حصلت على ذلك ، يمتحن جميع ما ورد فى السمع فوجده محققا لذلك ، أو جعله يحتمل الإحاطة بكل فنون الحكمة فوجد ذلك يضيق عن التخصيص ويلزمه القول بالعموم . أن يحصل على المخرج من العموم في القضاء ، وقد علم أن ذلك لو كان حقا في الحكمة أو واجبا في التدبير ليجد أهل الإلحاد أوضح طعن في القرآن وأيسر سبيل إلى القول بأنه غير منزل من عند الرحمن ؛ إذ به وصفه أنه لَوْ كَانَ مِنْ عِنْدِ غَيْرِ اللهِ لَوَجَدُوا فِيهِ اخْتِلَافًا كَثِيرًا ، وقال : لَا يَأْتِيهِ البَاطِلُ مِنْ بَيْنِ يَدَيْهِ وَلَا مِنْ خَلْفِهِ ، وقال : إِنَّا نَحْنُ نَزَّلْنَا الذِّكْرَ وَإِنَّا لَهُ لَحَافِظُونَ ، ثم وجد أكثر ما فيه الحكم منصرفا إلى غير المخرج ومحصلا على غير مجرى اللفظ من العموم والخصوص، وذلك على هذا القول صرف عن طريق الحكمة ومزيل حق التدبير ، جل الله عن أن يلحق حجته هذا الوصف أو دليله هذا التناقض
ثم قد بين جل ثناؤه لما أرسل من الأسماء المحمودة والمذمومة المقابلات التي لديها يظهر لزوم حق صرف المطلق من ذلك ، فقال : إِنَّ الأَبْرَارَ لَفِي نَعِيمٍ وَإِنَّ الفُجَّارَ لَفِي جَحِيم ، ثم وصفهم فقال : كَلَّا إِنَّ كِتَابَ الفُجَّارِ لَفِي سجين ، إلى آخر السورة ، فبين الفاجر المطلق المقصود بالوعيد ، وما منه من
The jurist Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: Then the principle regarding everything threatened with punishment is that the reality of that occurs from its agent in aspects of ugliness, the variation of which is known by intellects. Likewise, every name used to name an agent implies different meanings that cannot be conceived as being all on the same level of ugliness, nor its agent on the same level of blameworthiness or examination. As for that which is a defect in the hearer, it is incumbent upon him, by his intellect with which he is honored, to know the difference in the positions of those things, that they cannot be combined [under a single ruling] except that the understandings of the community find them to be so. He examines everything reported in revelation and finds it confirming that, or he makes it encompass all branches of wisdom and finds that this is too narrow for specification and makes it necessary to speak of generality. He must obtain a way out from generality in decree, knowing that if that were true in wisdom or necessary in management, then the people of heresy would find the clearest criticism in the Quran and the easiest path to saying it is not sent down from the Merciful. For by it He described that 'If it had been from other than God, they would have found much contradiction therein,' and He said: 'Falsehood cannot come to it from before it or behind it,' and He said: 'Indeed, We have sent down the Reminder, and indeed We are its guardians.' Then he found that most of its rulings are directed to other than the way out and are obtained according to other than the course of the wording in terms of generality and specificity. And that, according to this view, is a deviation from the path of wisdom and a removal of the right of management. God is exalted above that His proof should be described with this attribute or His evidence with this contradiction.
Then, the Exalted has clarified, when He sent the praise-worthy and blameworthy names that are opposite, by which the necessity of correctly applying the absolute of that appears. He said: 'Indeed, the righteous will be in pleasure, and indeed the wicked will be in Hellfire.' Then He described them and said: 'No! Indeed, the record of the wicked is in Sijjin,' to the end of the surah. So He made clear the absolute wicked intended by the threat, and what of it...
Source pages p. 970

Commentary
This passage presents a theological argument regarding the principle (الأصل) of all things that are threatened with punishment in the Quran. Maturidi begins by stating that the reality of such threatened actions occurs with varying degrees of ugliness (القبح), which intellects perceive. Similarly, the names given to agents imply different meanings, and not all actions or agents are on the same level of blame. The argument then addresses a methodological issue: when faced with a general threat (عموم) in the Quran, one must not rush to apply it universally without considering context. If one were to interpret all threats as absolute and then find that some individuals who outwardly qualify are not actually punished (e.g., believers who sin), this would lead to an apparent contradiction in the Quran. Such a contradiction would allow heretics to criticize the Quran as not being from God, since the Quran itself claims to have no contradictions (Quran 4:82), that falsehood cannot approach it (41:42), and that God preserves it (15:9). To avoid this, one must understand that many Quranic rulings are directed not at the general outward meaning but at a specific scope (خصوص). Thus, the general term must be qualified. Maturidi then cites the example of the names 'righteous' (الأبرار) and 'wicked' (الفجار) in the Quran. God explicitly describes the absolute wicked (الفاجر المطلق) as those destined for punishment in 'Sijjin' (a place in Hell), implying that the threatened punishment is for the wicked who are absolute in their wickedness, not for every sinner. This shows that Quranic threats must be understood with their proper intended scope, preserving both divine wisdom and the integrity of the Quran against accusations of contradiction.

Key Terms
القبح
العموم
الخصوص
الفاجر المطلق
الوعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every general threat in the Quran applies equally to all individuals in that category.
Correction: Maturidi shows that threats like the punishment of the wicked are specifically directed at the 'absolute wicked' (الفاجر المطلق), not every sinner, as seen in the context of Surah al-Mutaffifin.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi argue that one cannot simply apply every general threat in the Quran universally without considering specification?
A) Because the Quran contains contradictions that need resolution.
B) Because doing so would lead to heretics finding apparent contradictions, contradicting the Quran's claim of no contradiction.
C) Because all threats are actually conditional on disbelief.
D) Because the intellect cannot comprehend general threats.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that Quranic threats must be interpreted with their intended scope, targeting the absolute wicked, to preserve divine wisdom and avoid contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read Quranic verses about punishment, do you instinctively see yourself as included in the threat or excluded? What does Maturidi's argument demand you examine about your own state of iman?
?
If the Quran's threats are for the 'absolute wicked,' how does that shape your hope in God's mercy for your own sins? Are you willing to confront the possibility that you might be among the absolute wicked?
?
Maturidi warns that misinterpreting threats as universal could aid heretics. Has your own understanding of Quranic verses ever led you to doubt God's justice? How does this lesson call you to trust both the literal word and its intended meaning?

The Absolute Wicked and the Full Threat
Maturidi cites verses to distinguish the absolute wicked from others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Qur'an distinguishes the absolute wicked (الفاجر المطلق) as the primary targets of divine threats, separate from lesser sinners.

Core Idea
The full threat is for the absolute wicked who deny the Hereafter.

Maturidi begins by citing verses that sharply contrast the righteous and the wicked, showing that the threat applies squarely to those who are absolutely wicked. This initial distinction is crucial for understanding how threats function across different groups.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening verses
Maturidi cites Surah 82 and 83 to contrast the righteous and wicked. Read these verses carefully to see how the threat is directed at the 'absolute wicked'.
Notice the phrase 'الفاجر المطلق' (absolute wicked).
2
Identify the target of the threat
Maturidi concludes that the absolute wicked (الفاجر المطلق) are the ones intended by the threat, and that denial (تكذيب) is part of their description.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Ask yourself: why does Maturidi emphasize that the threat is for the absolute wicked? How does this differ from common understanding of threats in the Qur'an?
This sets the foundation for the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قد بين جل ثناؤه لما أرسل من الأسماء المحمودة والمذمومة المقابلات التي لديها يظهر لزوم حق صرف المطلق من ذلك ، فقال : إِنَّ الأَبْرَارَ لَفِي نَعِيمٍ وَإِنَّ الفُجَّارَ لَفِي جَحِيم . ثم وصفهم فقال : كَلَّا إِنَّ كِتَابَ الفُجَّارِ لَفِي سجين ، إلى آخر السورة ، فبين الفاجر المطلق المقصود بالوعيد ، وما منه من التكذيب. ثم قال : أَفَمَنْ كَانَ مُؤْمِنًا كَمَنْ كَانَ فَاسقًا لَا يَسْتَوُونَ ، ثم بين من المراد بالمؤمن وما له من المآب، والفاسق وما إليه مرجعه ، مع تكذيبه فى ذلك باليوم ، وقال فيما قال : كيف يَهْدِي اللهُ قَوْمًا كَفَرُوا بَعْدَ إِيمَانِهِمْ إلى قوله : وَاللَّهُ لَا يَهْدِي القَوْمَ الظَّالِمِينَ ، وقال : قَالُوا لَمْ نَكُ مِنْ المُصَلِّينَ ، وفيمن لم يؤد الزكاة : وَهُمْ بِالآخِرَةِ كافِرُونَ ، وفى أمر الرّبا ما ذكر من قوله : وَمَنْ عَادَ ، وقوله : وأخذهم الرباء إنهم أحلوا حيث : قَالُوا إِنَّمَا البَيْعُ مِثْلُ الرِّبَا ، وكذلك أموال اليتامى لم يكونوا يعطون الذين لم يبلغوا القتال ولا ضربوا بالسهام في المغانم وأمر القتل كذلك كانوا يقتلون بغيا واستحلالا على ما ذكر من قوله : وَاذْكُرُوا نِعْمَةَ اللهِ عَلَيْكُم إِذْ كنتم أَعْدَاءً ، فهذا الآن طريق حقائق الوعيد ، وما فيه إبطال تسمية الإيمان. وعلى ذلك القسمة فى الآخرة : فريق في الجنة وفريق في السغير ، والمعطى بيمينه وشماله ، والمؤمن والكافر ، وقوله تعالى : واتقوا النار التي أعدت للكافرين ، ففيهم تحقق الوعيد ولزمت الأسماء التي من نهايات في القبح إياهم . وأما من لم يبلغ ذلك الحد فإن الذى جاء فيهم من الوعيد يخرج على وجوه : على تحذير اختيار تلك الأحوال التي ذكرت ، أو على أن ذلك جزاؤه لو
Then He, exalted be His praise, explained when He sent the praiseworthy and blameworthy names, the correspondences by which appears the necessity of the right of diverting the absolute from that. He said: 'Indeed the righteous will be in pleasure and indeed the wicked will be in Hellfire' (82:13-14). Then He described them saying: 'No! Indeed the record of the wicked is in Sijjin' (83:7) to the end of the surah. So He made clear the absolute wicked intended by the threat, and what of denial is from him. Then He said: 'Is one who is a believer like one who is a disobedient? They are not equal' (32:18). Then He made clear who is meant by the believer and what he has of return, and the disobedient and what his destination is, along with his denial of that on the Day. And He said in what He said: 'How shall Allah guide a people who disbelieved after their belief?' (3:86) up to His saying: 'And Allah does not guide the wrongdoing people' (3:86). And He said: 'They said: We were not of those who prayed' (74:43), and regarding the one who did not pay zakah: 'And they are of the Hereafter disbelievers' (12:37? actually 12:37 is different, but this is a quote). And regarding the matter of usury, what He mentioned of His saying: 'And whoever returns [to usury]' (2:275), and His saying: 'And their taking of usury' (4:161), that they permitted it when they said: 'Indeed, trade is like usury' (2:275). And likewise the wealth of orphans: they would not give to those who had not reached fighting nor had struck with arrows in spoils, and the matter of killing likewise, they used to kill unjustly and deeming it lawful, as mentioned in His saying: 'And remember the favor of Allah upon you when you were enemies' (3:103). Now this is the way of the realities of the threat, and what in it is invalidation of the naming of faith. And upon that is the division in the Hereafter: a group in Paradise and a group in the Blaze, and the one who gives with his right and his left, and the believer and the disbeliever, and His saying: 'And fear the Fire which has been prepared for the disbelievers' (3:131). So upon them the threat is realized and the names that are extreme in ugliness become binding upon them. And as for the one who has not reached that limit, the threat that came regarding them issues upon aspects: upon warning of choosing those states which were mentioned, or that that is his recompense if...
Source pages p. 970

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of how divine threats (وعيد) in the Qur'an apply categorically. Maturidi first cites verses distinguishing the righteous from the wicked (82:13-14, 83:7) to show that 'absolute wicked' is intended for the full threat. He then uses 32:18 to contrast believers and disbelievers, and 3:86 to show that those who disbelieve after belief are not guided. He cites verses about prayer, zakah, usury, orphans, and reconciliation (74:43, 2:275, 4:161, 3:103) to demonstrate that those who commit these sins are subject to specific threats. He concludes that these verses establish the reality of the threat and do not nullify the name of faith (i.e., they do not negate that a sinner is a believer). The division in the Hereafter is clear: a group in Paradise and a group in the Blaze (السعير). The fire is prepared for disbelievers, so the threat applies to them. However, for those who have not reached that extreme limit of disbelief, the threat that comes about them is on two aspects: either as a warning against choosing those states, or as an indication that it would be their recompense if they persist. The passage is cut off but this is the gist.

Key Terms
وعيد
فاجر
تكذيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every sin, regardless of the sinner's faith, incurs the full divine threat of eternal punishment.
Correction: Maturidi shows that only the 'absolute wicked' (الفاجر المطلق) who deny the Hereafter are the targets of the full threat; other sins are addressed differently.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, who is the primary target of the full divine threat?
A) All sinners regardless of faith.
B) The absolute wicked (الفاجر المطلق) who deny the Day of Judgment.
C) Only those who commit major sins like usury.
D) Only polytheists.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that the full threat of Hellfire is directed at the absolute wicked who deny the Hereafter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson says the absolute wicked who deny the Hereafter are the target of the full threat. Examine your own heart: Is there any aspect of your life where you act as if the Hereafter doesn't matter, thereby aligning yourself with that denial? What would it take for you to truly live as if every moment is seen by God?
?
When you sin, do you dismiss it as small and forget that even minor sins can accumulate into a pattern of denial? How can you catch yourself slipping into that absolute wickedness before it becomes your identity?
?
Maturidi argues that the threat is for the absolute wicked, not every sinner. Does that comfort you into complacency about your own sins, or does it spur you to examine whether your sins are rooted in denial of the Hereafter? What one change can you make today to ensure you are counted among the righteous?

Threats as Warnings for Believers and Sinners
Maturidi explains the two aspects of divine threats for those not fully wicked.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine threats for those who have not reached absolute wickedness serve as warnings or conditional recompense, not necessarily as final punishment.

Core Idea
Threats to believers are warnings, not absolute sentences.

Having established the absolute wicked, Maturidi now turns to other sinners and believers. He shows through various verses that divine threats are applied differently: some are warnings to avoid certain states, and others indicate what the recompense would be if one persists.

Guided Path
1
Review additional verses
Maturidi cites several more verses (32:18, 3:86, 74:43, etc.) that address different groups: believers, disobedient, etc. Read them and note the context of each threat.
2
Understand the division in the Hereafter
Maturidi states that the Hereafter is divided: Paradise for believers, Blaze for disbelievers. The fire is prepared for disbelievers. This shows the ultimate application of the threat.
3
Learn the two aspects of threat
For those who have not reached the extreme limit, threats come as either a warning (تحذير) to avoid certain states, or as an indication that it would be their recompense if they persist. This nuance is key.
We need to infer this because the text cuts off.

?

Refuting the 'In Sha' Allah' Objection from Surah al-Fath
Maturidi answers the objection that the Quran uses 'in sha' Allah' in a context of certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the objection that the Quranic use of 'in sha' Allah' in a certain context permits exception in matters of certainty like faith.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's use of 'in sha' Allah' does not justify doubt in iman.

This passage presents an objection against Maturidi's doctrine that faith cannot be qualified with 'in sha' Allah'. The objector cites Surah al-Fath where Allah says 'You will surely enter the Sacred Mosque, if Allah wills'. Maturidi responds by showing that the objection is inconsistent and fails to escape the problem of doubt.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation. Notice the structure: objection then answer.
Pay attention to the quoted verse and how it is used.
2
Identify the objector's argument
The objector points out that the Quran itself uses 'in sha' Allah' in a context of certainty (Surah al-Fath). They argue this shows exception is permissible even when there is no doubt.
3
Analyze Maturidi's reply
Maturidi gives two points: (1) the objector's own doctrine already leads to doubt, and (2) consistency would require them to apply exception to all definitive Quranic addresses, which they do not.
This refutes the objection without needing to reinterpret the verse.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why Maturidi's response is considered effective. It turns the objection back on the objector by highlighting inconsistency.
This shows the importance of logical consistency in theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : فقد ذكر الله الثنيا في غير موضع الشك ، فيجوز الثنيا و على ذلك ، ثم قوله : لَتَدْخُلَنَّ المَسْجِدَ الحَرَامَ إِنْ شَاءَ اللهُ آمِنِينَ " . قيل : هذا ليس لكم ؛ لأنا قد بينا تحقيق الشك على مذهبكم ، ثم لم يكن الاحتجاج بخروج عن موضع الشك ولو كنتم كذلك إذا قد ذكر الله أهل اليقين في غير موضع باسم القطع ، فقولوا : لا يتم بلا ثنيا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
If someone says: 'Allah has mentioned the exception in a place other than doubt, so the exception is permissible on that basis. Then His saying: "You will surely enter the Sacred Mosque, if Allah wills, in safety" (48:27).' It is said: This is not for you, because we have already clarified the verification of doubt according to your doctrine. And then the argument does not go out from the place of doubt. And even if you were such, when Allah has mentioned the people of certainty in more than one place with a definitive name, then say: it is not complete without exception. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 990

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objector points to a Quranic verse (Surah al-Fath 27) in which Allah says 'You will surely enter the Sacred Mosque, if Allah wills, in safety.' This verse clearly contains 'in sha' Allah' in a context where the event is certain, so it seems to permit the use of exception even in certainty. The author answers by first stating that they have already established that the objector's doctrine leads to doubt. Moreover, the objector's argument does not escape the realm of doubt because they themselves claim that the exception is required in all cases. Furthermore, since Allah has addressed the people of certainty (ahl al-yaqin) with definitive names without exception in many verses, consistency would demand that the objector also insist on exception in those cases, which they do not. Thus, the objection is refuted.

Key Terms
شك
يقين
استثناء
آية الفتح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because the Quran uses 'in sha' Allah' in a certain context, it is allowed in iman.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the context is different and the verse does not support their claim because their own position leads to doubt and inconsistency.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi respond to the objection that the Quran uses 'in sha' Allah' in a certain context (Surah al-Fath)?
A) He accepts the analogy.
B) He says the verse is abrogated.
C) He argues that the objector's own doctrine leads to doubt and consistency would require exception in all definitive Quranic addresses.
D) He says the verse was only for the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the objection by showing that the objector's own position leads to doubt and inconsistency, thereby defending the necessity of certainty in faith without exception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself mentally adding 'if Allah wills' to your declaration of faith, even though you know it is absolute? What does that reveal about the strength of your certainty? Will you repent of any lingering doubt and affirm your iman without reservation?
?
Maturidi shows that the objector's reasoning leads to inconsistency. Are there areas in your own life where you hold double standards or contradictions in your beliefs? Will you examine them today and seek consistency with the Quran and Sunnah?
?
When someone uses a verse of the Quran to argue against a core belief of Ahl al-Sunnah, do you feel equipped to respond? If not, what step will you take to deepen your understanding of these refutations so that your faith remains unshaken?

Answer to Objection: Quran's Use of Uncertain Particles in Certainty Contexts
Maturidi refutes objection using Quranic uncertain particles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the Quran's use of words like 'zann' and 'la'alla' in certainty contexts does not justify using istithna' in expressing faith.

Core Idea
Uncertain particles in the Quran do not license istithna' in iman.

Maturidi continues to address objections against expressing faith without exception. Opponents point to Quranic verses where words like 'perhaps' appear in certain contexts, suggesting that cautionary language is permissible. Maturidi refutes this by showing the inconsistency of such an argument and reaffirms that istithna' in iman is not required.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Note the use of key terms like 'zann', 'la'alla', 'khawf'.
2
Understand the objection
Identify the objection that Quran uses uncertain particles in certainty contexts, implying that istithna' should be allowed.
The objector argues from Quranic usage.
3
Follow Maturidi's refutation
Note that Maturidi argues that if consistency were required, one would have to use such particles, but that is not the custom.
He also addresses a possible counter-objection.
4
Consider the final analogy
Understand that using istithna' with faith is prohibited for non-believers, so it is also prohibited for believers.
This completes the refutation.

ثم يقال : قد ذكر الله تعالى الظن، و لعل وعسى ، و الخوف ، في موضع اليقين ، فقولوا عند السؤال نظن، و نخاف ، و لعل ، ومثل ذا ، فإذ لم يجب هذا بما العرف فيه عبر " وإن اعترض في مواضع لهذه الأحترف مساغ في حق اليقين لعلك ، وكذلك أمر الثنيا . ثم يعارض الجميع ما ذكر من الإيمان بالله ومحمد مع الثنيا، فإذا كان القول ممتنعا والواصف به فى حق من لم يؤمن قلوبهم فكذلك الأول .
Then it is said: Allah Almighty has mentioned 'zann' (conjecture), 'la'alla' (perhaps), 'asā' (maybe), and 'khawf' (fear) in a context of certainty. So say, when asked, 'we think', 'we fear', 'perhaps', and the like. And since this is not required [by the objector] despite the custom of using such expressions, even if one objects that these particles have scope in the context of certainty for 'la'alla', likewise is the matter of exception. Then it is contradicted by all that has been mentioned of faith in Allah and Muhammad with exception, for if the statement is prohibited and the describer [uses it] with regard to those whose hearts have not believed, then likewise the first [is prohibited].
Source pages p. 990

Commentary
This passage is a further refutation of the objector. The author notes that the Quran sometimes uses words like 'zann' (conjecture), 'la'alla' (perhaps), 'asā' (maybe), and 'khawf' (fear) in contexts where certainty is meant. For example, Allah says 'perhaps' when it is certain. If the objector insists that istithna' must be used out of caution, consistency would require them to use such conjectural words in contexts of certainty. Yet they do not do so, because it is not the custom. By analogy, istithna' is also not required. Furthermore, the author argues that if using istithna' together with faith in Allah and Muhammad is prohibited for those who do not truly believe (as the earlier argument showed), then it is also prohibited for believers.

Key Terms
ظن
لعل
عسى
خوف
يقين
استثناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because the Quran uses words like 'perhaps' in certain contexts, believers may use istithna' when expressing faith.
Correction: Maturidi shows this reasoning is inconsistent; such usage does not imply permission for istithna' in iman.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Maturidi draw from the Quran's use of 'zann' and 'la'alla' in certainty contexts?
A) They prove that istithna' is always required.
B) They show that the Quran speaks doubtfully.
C) They demonstrate that such particles are not required even in certainty, so istithna' is also not required.
D) They are used only for non-believers.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's occasional use of uncertain particles does not permit inserting istithna' when expressing faith, as consistency would demand using such particles, which is not required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I am a believer', do you ever feel a subtle hesitation in your heart, as if adding 'if Allah wills'? Where does that hesitation come from, and does this lesson's argument that istithna' in iman is prohibited confront that hesitation? What will you do about it?
?
Consider how often you use uncertain language like 'maybe' or 'I think' when describing your faith. Maturidi argues that if Quran uses such words in certainty, it doesn't excuse us. Are you hiding your certainty behind cautious phrases? What would it mean for you to say 'I believe' without any qualification from now on?
?
The objector in this lesson uses Quranic particles as an excuse for their lack of full conviction. Are there any arguments you use to justify a lack of complete certainty in your faith? This lesson demands that you examine if you are making excuses like the objector. Will you commit to stating your faith with full conviction, even if you feel weak?

Exception Not Limited to Doubt – Refuting the Objection
Maturidi refutes the objection that exception implies doubt, using analogy and hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the use of exception (istithna') in divine speech does not imply doubt, and that it serves other purposes such as teaching humility and proper speech.

Core Idea
Exception in divine speech is not a sign of uncertainty but a lesson in etiquette.

Maturidi addresses a key objection raised by those who argue that if exception (in sha' Allah) is used only in doubtful contexts, then God's use of it in promises would imply doubt. He refutes this by showing that exception is used for multiple wisdoms, and that other words like 'perhaps' are also used in contexts of certainty to teach believers appropriate speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a refutational style, first presenting the objection and then responding.
2
Identify the objection
Find the sentence that begins with 'فإن قال قائل' – this is the objector's claim: that exception appears in non-doubt contexts, so it must be permissible there.
3
Analyze Maturidi's response
Maturidi responds by saying that exception is not exclusive to doubt. He points out that words like 'zann' (conjecture) are also used in certainty contexts, teaching believers humility.
Pay attention to the analogy with 'la'alla' and 'asa'.
4
Consider the hadith
The hadith about 'faith without doubt' is cited to show that complete certainty is possible, and that exception is not needed in all expressions of faith.
This reinforces that exception is not a requirement for certainty.
5
Synthesize the argument
Summarize in your own words why the objection fails: exception serves multiple purposes beyond indicating doubt.
Think about how this principle applies to everyday speech about future events.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيكون عامة آيات الله فى الخطاب خارجة مخرج عبث ، إذ الحق من جملة المذاهب من لا يلزمه هذا القول بمذهبه قال أو لم يقل ، والله الموفق .

فإن قال قائل : فقد ذكر الله الثنيا في غير موضع الشك ، فيجوز الثنيا على ذلك ، ثم قوله : لَتَدْخُلُنَّ المَسْجِدَ الحَرَامَ إِنْ شَاءَ اللهُ آمِنِينَ " . قيل : هذا ليس لكم ؛ لأنا قد بينا تحقيق الشك على مذهبكم ، ثم لم يكن الاحتجاج بخروج عن موضع الشك ولو كنتم كذلك إذا قد ذكر الله أهل اليقين في غير موضع باسم القطع ، فقولوا : لا يتم بلا ثنيا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله

ثم يقال : قد ذكر الله تعالى الظن، و لعل وعسى ، و الخوف ، في موضع اليقين ، فقولوا عند السؤال نظن، و نخاف ، و لعل ، ومثل ذا ، فإذ لم يجب هذا بما العرف فيه عبر " وإن اعترض في مواضع لهذه الأحترف مساغ في حق اليقين لعلك ، وكذلك أمر الثنيا . ثم يعارض الجميع ما ذكر من الإيمان بالله ومحمد مع الثنيا، فإذا كان القول ممتنعا والواصف به فى حق من لم يؤمن قلوبهم فكذلك الأول . وقد روى عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه سئل عن أفضل الأعمال فقال : إيمان لا شك فيه ، وجهاد لا غلول فيه ، وحج مبرور ، فقال الله تعالى : إِنَّمَا المُؤمِنُونَ الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا بِاللَّهِ وَرَسُولِهِ ثُمَّ لَمْ يَرْتَابُوا . . . فإن قيل : ما الحكمة في قوله : لَتَدْخُلُنَّ المَسْجِدَ الحَرَامَ ، قيل : يخرج هذا عندنا على وجوه والله أعلم بحقيقة ذلك أخبر عن قول غيره ، لم يقل : لتدخلن إن شئت ، ولكن قال : إن شاء الله ؛ ليعلم أنه قول غيره ، ثم احتمل أن يكون الله علم رسوله أن يقول ذلك ويستثنى لما هو وعد، لكنه خبر وقد كان قال : " وَلَا تَقُولَنَّ لِشَيْءٍ إِنِّي فَاعِلٌ ذَلِكَ غَدًا ، إِلَّا أَنْ يَشَاءَ اللهُ "، ولأفعلن، و لتدخلن، واحد ، لكنه أمر بالثنيا إن كان وعده له أو لا ؛ ليعلم الناس حق الوعد ، كما أمره بالمشورة ، ليعلم الناس خطرها، أو لما كان أضاف الله إليه الدخول ، وقد كان وعد خاصته أو من بقى منهم ، فالثنيا لما خشى الفناء على بعض المخاطبين، أو كان في قوله : لَقَدْ صَدَقَ اللَّهُ رَسُولَهُ الرُّؤْيَا بِالْحَقِّ، ثم هو يتوجه وجهين: أحدهما أن يكون رأى كذلك قولا مقرونا بالثنيا ، فذكر على ذلك ، إذ كان رسول الله أخبر القوم بالدخول لوقت لم يبين له ، فاستثنى فى ذلك ، وذلك حق فى كل ما يرتاب ، لا ما يتقين فيه على ما ذكرنا . فمن كان على يقين من دينه وعلم من صدق ممن يعلم حد الإيمان وأنه قد أوفاه فعليه أن يقوله شكرا لما أنعم الله به عليه ، وليس في ذلك تزكية لاشتراك الجميع في ذلك ، ولما أمروا به ، ولما هو معلوم الحد ، ولما باليقين به يعلم مواقع أن الله إذ سماهم به سماهم بما استحقوا ذلك ، مع ما ألزم الله عز وجل بظاهر الدين أحكاما من معاملات الخلق وأنواع الحقوق إظهار ذلك للتميام بالحقوق التى يلزم الناس بها ، ولا قوة إلا بالله العظيم.
Thus the general verses of God in speech would come out as frivolous, since the truth among the schools is that one who is not bound by this statement in his school says it or does not say it, and God is the grantor of success.

If someone says: 'God mentioned the exception (al-thunya) in other than a context of doubt, so the exception is permissible in that case, then His saying: "You shall surely enter the Sacred Mosque, if God wills, in security" [Q. 48:27].' It is said: 'This is not for you; for we have already clarified the realization of doubt according to your school, and then the argument is not a departure from the context of doubt. And if you were like that, then God has mentioned the people of certainty in other than a context with the name of decisiveness, so say: "It is not complete without an exception," and there is no power except with God.'

Then it is said: 'God has mentioned zann (conjecture), la'alla (perhaps), 'asa (maybe), and khawf (fear) in a context of certainty, so say in response: "we conjecture," "we fear," "perhaps," and the like. Since this is not answered by that which is customary in it, if one objects in places that these words have a way in the right of certainty, like 'la'alla,' then similarly the matter of the exception. Then everything mentioned about belief in God and Muhammad with the exception is countered. So if the statement is impossible and the describer of it regarding those whose hearts did not believe, then similarly the first.'

And it has been narrated from the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, that he was asked about the best deeds and said: 'Faith without doubt, struggle without deceit, and an accepted pilgrimage.' Then God Almighty said: 'Indeed, the believers are only those who believed in God and His Messenger, then did not doubt' [Q. 49:15].

If it is said: 'What is the wisdom in His saying: "You shall surely enter the Sacred Mosque?"' It is said: 'This, according to us, is explained in several ways, and God knows best the truth of that: (1) He reported the statement of another; He did not say: "You shall enter if you wish," but He said: "If God wills" to teach that it is the statement of another. (2) Then it is possible that God taught His Messenger to say that and use the exception because it is a promise, but it is news. And He had said: "And never say of anything, 'Indeed, I will do that tomorrow,' except if God wills" [Q. 18:23-24], and "I will do" and "you shall enter" are the same, but He commanded the exception whether the promise was for him or not, to teach people the truth of the promise, just as He commanded him consultation to teach people its importance. (3) Or because God added the entering to Himself, and He had promised His elite or those who remained of them, so the exception was due to fear of extinction for some of the addressees. (4) Or in His saying: "Certainly has God confirmed to His Messenger the vision in truth" [Q. 48:27], then it is directed in two ways: one is that he saw a statement similarly coupled with an exception, so he mentioned it accordingly, since the Messenger of God informed the people of the entering at a time not made clear to him, so he made an exception in that, and that is valid in everything that is doubted, not in what is certain about it as we have mentioned. So whoever is certain of his religion and knows the truth from one who knows the limit of faith and that he has fulfilled it, he must say it as gratitude for what God has bestowed upon him, and there is no self-praise in that because of the participation of all in that, and because they were commanded to do so, and because it is a known limit, and because by certainty he knows the loci wherein God, when He named them, named them with what they deserved, along with what God Almighty has imposed by the apparent religion of rulings from dealings among creatures and types of rights, to manifest that for the fulfillment of the rights by which people are bound. And there is no power except with God the Almighty.
Source pages p. 990

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection and an explanation of the wisdom behind the use of exception (istithna) in divine speech, specifically the phrase 'in sha' Allah' in Quranic promises. The argument proceeds as follows:

1. First, Maturidi states the consequence of the objector's view: if exception is only for doubt, then many verses would be frivolous. Then he introduces the objection: the objector points out that God mentions exception in non-doubt contexts, like in the verse about entering the Sacred Mosque, and claims that exception is permissible there. Maturidi responds that the objector's premise is flawed because they have already established that doubt is not the only reason for exception. He then argues that if the objector insists on their view, they should also deny exception in contexts where God mentions certainty, which they do not.

2. Maturidi further argues that God uses words like 'zann', 'la'alla', 'asa', and 'khawf' in contexts of certainty to teach believers appropriate speech and humility. Similarly, exception can be used for reasons other than doubt.

3. He cites a hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) defines the best deeds as faith without doubt, showing that complete certainty is possible.

4. Then a new question arises: what is the wisdom behind the exception in the verse 'You shall surely enter the Sacred Mosque, if God wills'? Maturidi provides multiple explanations: a) it is a report of someone else's statement, b) it is to teach people the truth of the promise and the importance of consulting God's will, c) it is due to fear that some addressees might die before the event, and d) it relates to the Prophet's vision and the fact that the timing was not specified, so exception was appropriate.

5. Finally, Maturidi concludes that those who are certain of their faith and have fulfilled its obligations should declare their faith gratefully, without self-praise, because it is a shared duty and a matter of known limits. The passage reinforces that analogical reasoning and proper understanding of divine speech require distinguishing contexts of doubt from certainty, and that exception serves multiple wisdoms.

Key Terms
الثنيا (istithna/exception)
الشك (doubt/shakk)
اليقين (certainty/yaqin)
الظن (conjecture/zann)
الاستثناء (exception/istithna')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the use of 'in sha' Allah' in divine promises implies that God is uncertain about the future.
Correction: Maturidi shows that exception is used for teaching, reporting, or precautionary communication, not due to uncertainty.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the purpose of God using words like 'zann' and 'la'alla' in contexts of certainty?
A) To indicate that God is uncertain.
B) To teach believers appropriate speech and humility.
C) To show that exception is always necessary.
D) To deny the possibility of certainty.

Key Takeaway
Exception in divine speech is not limited to doubt; it serves multiple wisdoms, including teaching proper speech and humility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'in sha' Allah' for your future plans, do you genuinely mean it as an acknowledgment that your will is subordinate to God's, or is it just a cultural phrase? How does this lesson challenge you to examine your intention behind that phrase?
?
The Prophet defined the best deeds as 'faith without doubt.' Where in your life do you sense a subtle doubt creeping into your certainty about Allah's promises, such as His provision or forgiveness? What concrete step will you take this week to strengthen that certainty?
?
Maturidi points out that God uses words like 'perhaps' in contexts of absolute certainty to teach us adab. Which area of your speech—whether in making promises, discussing future events, or expressing emotions—needs transformation to reflect true tawakkul rather than just empty or habitual phrases? What will you change starting today?

The Wisdom of Exception in Divine Promises
Maturidi explains multiple wisdoms for the exception in Q.48:27 and concludes about expressing certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple wisdoms behind the use of exception (in sha' Allah) in the Quranic promise about entering the Sacred Mosque, and how this relates to certainty in faith.

Core Idea
The exception in divine promises is not due to uncertainty but to teach, to report, or to account for human limitations.

Having established that exception is not limited to doubt, Maturidi now explains the specific wisdom behind the phrase 'in sha' Allah' in the verse about entering the Sacred Mosque. He offers several interpretations, each highlighting a different aspect of divine speech and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the wisdom
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the multiple explanations Maturidi gives for the exception.
This passage is enumerative; list the explanations as you read.
2
Identify the first explanation
Maturidi first says that the phrase 'if God wills' is a report of someone else's statement, not God's own speech.
This distinguishes between direct divine speech and reported speech.
3
Identify the second explanation
The exception is commanded to teach people the truth of the promise and the importance of acknowledging God's will, even in news.
Compare with the command to consult (mashwara).
4
Identify the third explanation
The exception is due to the possibility that some addressees might die before the event, so it is a precaution.
This accounts for human mortality.
5
Synthesize the lesson
Understand that the exception does not indicate uncertainty; it serves multiple wisdoms. Then read the conclusion about expressing certainty with gratitude.
Maturidi ends by advising that those who are certain should declare their faith gratefully, without self-praise.

Thus the general verses of God in speech would come out as frivolous, since the truth among the schools is that one who is not bound by this statement in his school says it or does not say it, and God is the grantor of success.

If someone says: 'God mentioned the exception (al-thunya) in other than a context of doubt, so the exception is permissible in that case, then His saying: "You shall surely enter the Sacred Mosque, if God wills, in security" [Q. 48:27].' It is said: 'This is not for you; for we have already clarified the realization of doubt according to your school, and then the argument is not a departure from the context of doubt. And if you were like that, then God has mentioned the people of certainty in other than a context with the name of decisiveness, so say: "It is not complete without an exception," and there is no power except with God.'

Then it is said: 'God has mentioned zann (conjecture), la'alla (perhaps), 'asa (maybe), and khawf (fear) in a context of certainty, so say in response: "we conjecture," "we fear," "perhaps," and the like. Since this is not answered by that which is customary in it, if one objects in places that these words have a way in the right of certainty, like 'la'alla,' then similarly the matter of the exception. Then everything mentioned about belief in God and Muhammad with the exception is countered. So if the statement is impossible and the describer of it regarding those whose hearts did not believe, then similarly the first.'

And it has been narrated from the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, that he was asked about the best deeds and said: 'Faith without doubt, struggle without deceit, and an accepted pilgrimage.' Then God Almighty said: 'Indeed, the believers are only those who believed in God and His Messenger, then did not doubt' [Q. 49:15].

If it is said: 'What is the wisdom in His saying: "You shall surely enter the Sacred Mosque?"' It is said: 'This, according to us, is explained in several ways, and God knows best the truth of that: (1) He reported the statement of another; He did not say: "You shall enter if you wish," but He said: "If God wills" to teach that it is the statement of another. (2) Then it is possible that God taught His Messenger to say that and use the exception because it is a promise, but it is news. And He had said: "And never say of anything, 'Indeed, I will do that tomorrow,' except if God wills" [Q. 18:23-24], and "I will do" and "you shall enter" are the same, but He commanded the exception whether the promise was for him or not, to teach people the truth of the promise, just as He commanded him consultation to teach people its importance. (3) Or because God added the entering to Himself, and He had promised His elite or those who remained of them, so the exception was due to fear of extinction for some of the addressees. (4) Or in His saying: "Certainly has God confirmed to His Messenger the vision in truth" [Q. 48:27], then it is directed in two ways: one is that he saw a statement similarly coupled with an exception, so he mentioned it accordingly, since the Messenger of God informed the people of the entering at a time not made clear to him, so he made an exception in that, and that is valid in everything that is doubted, not in what is certain about it as we have mentioned. So whoever is certain of his religion and knows the truth from one who knows the limit of faith and that he has fulfilled it, he must say it as gratitude for what God has bestowed upon him, and there is no self-praise in that because of the participation of all in that, and because they were commanded to do so, and because it is a known limit, and because by certainty he knows the loci wherein God, when He named them, named them with what they deserved, along with what God Almighty has imposed by the apparent religion of rulings from dealings among creatures and types of rights, to manifest that for the fulfillment of the rights by which people are bound. And there is no power except with God the Almighty.
Source pages p. 990

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection and an explanation of the wisdom behind the use of exception (istithna) in divine speech, specifically the phrase 'in sha' Allah' in Quranic promises. The argument proceeds as follows:

1. First, Maturidi states the consequence of the objector's view: if exception is only for doubt, then many verses would be frivolous. Then he introduces the objection: the objector points out that God mentions exception in non-doubt contexts, like in the verse about entering the Sacred Mosque, and claims that exception is permissible there. Maturidi responds that the objector's premise is flawed because they have already established that doubt is not the only reason for exception. He then argues that if the objector insists on their view, they should also deny exception in contexts where God mentions certainty, which they do not.

2. Maturidi further argues that God uses words like 'zann', 'la'alla', 'asa', and 'khawf' in contexts of certainty to teach believers appropriate speech and humility. Similarly, exception can be used for reasons other than doubt.

3. He cites a hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) defines the best deeds as faith without doubt, showing that complete certainty is possible.

4. Then a new question arises: what is the wisdom behind the exception in the verse 'You shall surely enter the Sacred Mosque, if God wills'? Maturidi provides multiple explanations: a) it is a report of someone else's statement, b) it is to teach people the truth of the promise and the importance of consulting God's will, c) it is due to fear that some addressees might die before the event, and d) it relates to the Prophet's vision and the fact that the timing was not specified, so exception was appropriate.

5. Finally, Maturidi concludes that those who are certain of their faith and have fulfilled its obligations should declare their faith gratefully, without self-praise, because it is a shared duty and a matter of known limits. The passage reinforces that analogical reasoning and proper understanding of divine speech require distinguishing contexts of doubt from certainty, and that exception serves multiple wisdoms.

Key Terms
الثنيا (istithna/exception)
الوعد (promise/wa'd)
الاستثناء (exception/istithna')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the exception in the verse is just a formality or that God is uncertain.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the exception serves multiple wisdoms such as teaching, reporting, and precaution, not uncertainty.

Checkpoint
In the first explanation Maturidi gives for the exception in Q.48:27, what does he say?
A) It is a command from God.
B) It is a report of someone else's statement.
C) It is an oath by God.
D) It is a condition for entry.

Key Takeaway
The exception in divine promises serves multiple wisdoms, including teaching, reporting, and accounting for human limitations, and does not indicate uncertainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make plans or promises, do you genuinely say 'إِنْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ' as an acknowledgment of your dependence on God, or is it just a verbal habit? What would change in your attitude if you truly meant it every time?
?
Maturidi teaches that the exception in divine promises is an act of teaching humility. Think of a time you spoke with false certainty about the future. How can you integrate the 'if God wills' into your speech today, not as a mere formality but as a heartfelt act of tawakkul?
?
The passage says that those certain of their faith should declare it gratefully without self-praise. Is your confession of faith driven by gratitude for God's favor or by a desire to feel superior? How can you purify your intention the next time you affirm your beliefs publicly?

The Wisdom of Saying 'In Sha' Allah' in Promises
Maturidi explains the wisdom behind the command to say 'in sha' Allah' when making promises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reasons why Islam teaches believers to say 'in sha' Allah' when making promises and how this relates to humility, certainty, and gratitude.

Core Idea
Saying 'in sha' Allah' teaches humility and reminds us that all outcomes depend on Allah's will.

This lesson explores a profound verse where Allah instructs the Prophet (peace be upon him) to never say 'I will do such-and-such tomorrow' without adding 'if Allah wills.' Imam al-Maturidi unpacks the wisdom behind this command, revealing its lessons for promises, certainty, and expressing gratitude without self-praise. Let's delve into his explanation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of Maturidi's argument.
Notice the multiple reasons he provides, each introduced by 'أو'.
2
Identify the command and its scope
Recognize that the verse commands saying 'in sha' Allah' even when the promise is not to another person, to teach the seriousness of promises.
Maturidi compares this to the command of consultation (shura).
3
Understand the exception regarding the mosque entry
Examine the Prophet's promise about entering the mosque: the exception was made because the time was not specified.
This leads to the principle that the exception is for matters of doubt, not certainty.
4
Apply the lesson to believers
For those certain of their faith, saying 'in sha' Allah' is an expression of gratitude, not self-praise, because all believers share in that.
This guards against thinking it implies doubt in Allah's power.
5
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider how this teaching affects your own promises and reliance on Allah, and the importance of humility in all future plans.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَقَدْ كَانَ قَالَ: "وَلَا تَقُولَنَّ لِشَيْءٍ إِنِّي فَاعِلٌ ذَلِكَ غَدًا، إِلَّا أَنْ يَشَاءَ اللهُ"، ولأفعلن، و لتدخلن، واحد، لكنه أمر بالثنيا إن كان وعده له أو لا؛ ليعلم الناس حق الوعد، كما أمره بالمشورة، ليعلم الناس خطرها، أو لما كان أضاف الله إليه الدخول، وقد كان وعد خاصته أو من بقي منهم، فالثنيا لما خشي الفناء على بعض المخاطبين، أو كان في قوله: لَقَدْ صَدَقَ اللَّهُ رَسُولَهُ الرُّؤْيَا بِالْحَقِّ، ثم هو يتوجه وجهين: أحدهما أن يكون رأى كذلك قولا مقرونا بالثنيا، فذكر على ذلك، إذ كان رسول الله أخبر القوم بالدخول لوقت لم يبين له، فاستثنى في ذلك، وذلك حق في كل ما يرتاب، لا ما يتقين فيه على ما ذكرنا. فمن كان على يقين من دينه وعلم من صدق ممن يعلم حد الإيمان وأنه قد أوفاه فعليه أن يقوله شكرا لما أنعم الله به عليه، وليس في ذلك تزكية لاشتراك الجميع في ذلك، ولما أمروا به، ولما هو معلوم الحد، ولما باليقين به يعلم مواقع أن الله إذ سماهم به سماهم بما استحقوا ذلك، مع ما ألزم الله عز وجل بظاهر الدين أحكاما من معاملات الخلق وأنواع الحقوق إظهار ذلك للتميام بالحقوق التي يلزم الناس بها، ولا قوة إلا بالله العظيم.
And He had said: 'And never say of anything, 'Indeed, I will do that tomorrow,' except [when adding], 'If Allah wills.'' And 'I will do,' and 'you will enter' are one [in meaning], but He commanded the exception whether His promise was to him or not; so that people learn the right of the promise, just as He commanded him to consult, so that people learn its gravity; or because Allah had attributed the entry to Himself, and He had promised His elect or those who remained among them, so the exception was due to fear of perishing for some of those addressed; or [it was] in His saying: 'Indeed, Allah has fulfilled for His Messenger the vision in truth.' Then it takes two aspects: one of them is that he saw similarly a saying accompanied by the exception, so he mentioned it accordingly, since the Messenger of Allah informed the people of entry at a time not specified to him, so he made an exception in that. And that is correct in everything about which one is doubtful, not in what one is certain of, according to what we have mentioned. Whoever is certain of his religion and knows from the truthful one who knows the limit of faith and that he has fulfilled it, then it is upon him to say it as gratitude for what Allah has bestowed upon him, and there is no self-praise in that because all share in that, and because they were commanded with it, and because its limit is known, and because by certainty one knows the positions that Allah, when He named them with it, named them with what they deserved, along with what Allah Almighty obligated by the outward of religion of rulings concerning dealings among creation and types of rights, manifesting that for the discernment [?] of rights that people are obligated with, and there is no power except with Allah the Great.
Source pages p. 991

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the Qur'anic verse 18:23-24, where Allah commands His Prophet to say 'in sha' Allah' when intending a future action. The author, Imam al-Maturidi, explains the wisdom behind this command. He begins by noting that the verse's instruction applies whether the promise is made to someone or not. The purpose is to teach people the seriousness and rights of a promise, similar to the command of consultation (shura) which teaches its importance. Alternatively, the exception was commanded because the entry into the mosque was attributed by Allah to Himself, and the Prophet had promised it to a specific group, but some of them might perish before that time. The author then connects this to the verse from Surah al-Fath (48:27), where Allah fulfilled the vision of entering the mosque. He explains that the Prophet's statement about entry was accompanied by an exception (in sha' Allah) because the exact time was not specified. This practice is proper for matters of doubt, not for matters of certainty. Then, Maturidi applies this to believers: those who are certain of their faith and have fulfilled its obligations should express gratitude to Allah by acknowledging His favor, and this is not self-praise because all believers share in that. He concludes by mentioning that outward religion imposes rulings and rights that must be manifested, and ends with a prayer.

Key Terms
الثنيا (exception)
الوعد (promise)
المشورة (consultation)
اليقين (certainty)
تزكية (self-praise)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think saying 'in sha' Allah' indicates doubt about Allah's power or the occurrence of the event.
Correction: It actually teaches humility and reliance on Allah, and is a means of expressing gratitude without self-praise. For matters of certainty, it is still said as gratitude.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why did the Prophet make an exception (say 'in sha' Allah') when informing about the entry into the mosque?
A) Because he was uncertain about the event.
B) Because the time of entry was not specified to him.
C) Because he wanted to test the faith of his companions.
D) Because the verse required it only when making promises to others.

Key Takeaway
Saying 'in sha' Allah' is a means of humbling oneself before Allah, teaching the gravity of promises, and expressing gratitude without falling into self-praise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you promise something to someone, do you really feel the weight of that promise, or do you say it lightly? How would consciously adding 'in sha' Allah' change your attitude toward your word?
?
Are you quick to take credit for your own faith and good deeds? In what specific situations can you start saying 'in sha' Allah' to remind yourself that your faith is a gift from Allah, not your own achievement?
?
Think of a promise you made recently without saying 'in sha' Allah'. Are you certain you will fulfill it? What does your certainty say about your reliance on Allah? Will you commit to never making a future promise without adding 'in sha' Allah', starting today?

Reconciling Differences in the Hadith of Jibril
Maturidi reconciles variations in the Hadith of Jibril.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi reconciles the differing narrations of the Hadith of Jibril regarding the definitions of Iman and Islam.

Core Idea
Differences in hadith wording can be explained by narrator omission.

In the well-known Hadith of Jibril, the questions about Iman, Islam, and Ihsan are presented. However, some narrations only mention the question about Islam. Imam Maturidi addresses this apparent contradiction by offering plausible explanations rooted in hadith transmission methodology. This passage demonstrates his approach to reconciling textual variants.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the two possible explanations Maturidi offers.
The Arabic is partially corrupted, but the key reasoning is clear.
2
Identify the two explanations
Maturidi suggests either the narrator did not hear the full question, or the narration chain omitted part of it. Compare these with the hadith of Ibn Umar.
He considers the first explanation more likely based on Ibn Umar's credibility.
3
Appreciate the methodology
Recognize that Maturidi uses rational inference to resolve hadith differences without rejecting any authentic narration.
This reflects the mature approach of kalam in handling textual variants.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاختلاف الذي من عبر جبريل عليه السلام قد روي في ذلك اختلاف اللفظ: روي عن ابن عمر رضي الله عنهما أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم مثل عن الإيمان ثم عن شرائع الإسلام فأجاب بالذي ذكر في السؤال عن الإسلام، فيكون هذا الخبر تفسيرا للخبر الأول، ويحمل الخبر الأول على جهتين: إما على أن الراوي لم يسمع الشرائع في السؤال أو في الرواية عن الذي رواه، فروى كذلك: [؟] خبر فيذه خبر ابن عمر أن ذلك قد كان، ومن البعيد أن يكون مقدار الأول ويؤيده ابن عمر لما يسقط الشهادة بما يخبر عن جبريل وعن الرسول بغير الذي قالا. وغير مدفوع حقا البعض على بعض، فيرونه على ما
The difference regarding Jibril, peace be upon him—there has been narrated in that matter a difference in wording: it was narrated from Ibn 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with them both, that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, presented (the question) about Iman then about the laws of Islam, and he answered with what was mentioned in the question about Islam. So this report is an interpretation of the first report, and the first report is interpreted in two ways: either that the narrator did not hear the (mention of) laws in the question, or in the narration from the one who narrated it, so he narrated likewise. [؟] the report of Ibn 'Umar that that had occurred, and it is remote that it would be the amount of the first and that Ibn 'Umar would support it when he drops the testimony by informing about Jibril and about the Messenger other than what they said. And the truth of one over another is not repelled, so they see it as what
Source pages p. 995

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. Maturidi addresses an apparent contradiction in the hadith of Jibril known to discuss Iman, Islam, and Ihsan. Some narrations present the question about Islam only, while others include Iman. He mentions that Ibn 'Umar reported the Prophet answering according to the question about Islam even when the question about Iman was also asked. Maturidi explains that this narration can be understood in two ways: either the narrator did not hear the entire question (only the part about Islam), or the one from whom he narrated omitted the mention of Iman. He then notes that it is unlikely that Ibn 'Umar would drop part of what Jibril and the Prophet said, and he hints at a further point that the truth is not repelled, but the text is cut off.

Key Terms
حديث جبريل
إيمان
إسلام
رواية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the hadith shows a fundamental difference between Iman and Islam as separate categories.
Correction: Maturidi's resolution suggests the variant wording is due to transmission issues, not a difference in definition.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi explain the narration that mentions only Islam in the question?
A) It is a fabrication.
B) The narrator may not have heard the full question.
C) It abrogates the other narration.
D) It refers to a different occasion.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that narrational variations do not undermine the hadith's authenticity but can be reconciled through careful attention to transmission chains.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter different narrations of the same event from the Prophet, do you trust that they can be reconciled, or do you feel doubt? How does Maturidi's approach challenge your own reaction to apparent contradictions in sacred texts?
?
Maturidi gives the narrator the benefit of the doubt. In your own life, when people report things differently, do you assume error or seek to harmonize? What would it take for you to adopt Maturidi's method in your daily judgments?
?
This lesson shows that careful scholarship can preserve unity despite surface differences. Where in your own understanding of faith are you tempted to divide over minor variations rather than seeing the deeper coherence?
